Jungle\r\n     Mowgli\xe2\x80\x99s Song against People\r\n     The Undertakers\r\n     A Ripple Song\r\n     The King\xe2\x80\x99s Ankus\r\n     The Song of the Little Hunter\r\n     Quiquern\r\n     \xe2\x80\x98Angutivaun Taina\xe2\x80\x99\r\n     Red Dog\r\n     Chil\xe2\x80\x99s Song\r\n     The Spring Running\r\n     The Outsong\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nHOW FEAR CAME\r\n\r\n     The stream is shrunk--the pool is dry,\r\n     And we be comrades, thou and I;\r\n     With fevered jowl and dusty flank\r\n     Each jostling each along the bank;\r\n     And by one drouthy fear made still,\r\n     Forgoing thought of quest or kill.\r\n     Now \xe2\x80\x98neath his dam the fawn may see,\r\n     The lean Pack-wolf as cowed as he,\r\n     And the tall buck, unflinching, note\r\n     The fangs that tore his father\xe2\x80\x99s throat.\r\n     The pools are shrunk--the streams are dry,\r\n     And we be playmates, thou and I,\r\n     Till yonder cloud--Good Hunting!--loose\r\n     The rain that breaks our Water Truce.\r\n\r\nThe Law of the Jungle--which is by far the oldest law in the world--has\r\narranged for almost every kind of accident that may befall the Jungle\r\nPeople, till now its code is as perfect as time and custom can make\r\nit. You will remember that Mowgli spent a great part of his life in the\r\nSeeonee Wolf-Pack, learning the Law from Baloo, the Brown Bear; and\r\nit was Baloo who told him, when the boy grew impatient at the constant\r\norders, that the Law was like the Giant Creeper, because it dropped\r\nacross every one\xe2\x80\x99s back and no one could escape. \xe2\x80\x9cWhen thou hast lived\r\nas long as I have, Little Brother, thou wilt see how all the Jungle\r\nobeys at least one Law. And that will be no pleasant sight,\xe2\x80\x9d said Baloo.\r\n\r\nThis talk went in at one ear and out at the other, for a boy who spends\r\nhis life eating and sleeping does not worry about anything till it\r\nactually stares him in the face. But, one year, Baloo\xe2\x80\x99s words came true,\r\nand Mowgli saw all the Jungle working under the Law.\r\n\r\nIt began when the winter Rains failed almost entirely, and Ikki, the\r\nPorcupine, meeting Mowgli in a bamboo-thicket, told him that the wild\r\nyams were drying up. Now everybody knows that Ikki is ridiculously\r\nfastidious in his choice of food, and will eat nothing but the very best\r\nand ripest. So Mowgli laughed and said, \xe2\x80\x9cWhat is that to me?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot much NOW,\xe2\x80\x9d said Ikki, rattling his quills in a stiff, uncomfortable\r\nway, \xe2\x80\x9cbut later we shall see. Is there any more diving into the deep\r\nrock-pool below the Bee-Rocks, Little Brother?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo. The foolish water is going all away, and I do not wish to break my\r\nhead,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mowgli, who, in those days, was quite sure that he knew as\r\nmuch as any five of the Jungle People put together.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is thy loss. A small crack might let in some wisdom.\xe2\x80\x9d Ikki ducked\r\nquickly to prevent Mowgli from pulling his nose-bristles, and Mowgli\r\ntold Baloo what Ikki had said. Baloo looked very grave, and mumbled\r\nhalf to himself: \xe2\x80\x9cIf I were alone I would change my hunting-grounds now,\r\nbefore the others began to think. And yet--hunting among strangers ends\r\nin fighting; and they might hurt the Man-cub. We must wait and see how\r\nthe mohwa blooms.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThat spring the mohwa tree, that Baloo was so fond of, never flowered.\r\nThe greeny, cream-coloured, waxy blossoms were heat-killed before they\r\nwere born, and only a few bad-smelling petals came down when he stood\r\non his hind legs and shook the tree. Then, inch by inch, the untempered\r\nheat crept into the heart of the Jungle, turning it yellow, brown, and\r\nat last black. The green growths in the sides of the ravines burned up\r\nto broken wires and curled films of dead stuff; the hidden pools sank\r\ndown and caked over, keeping the last least footmark on their edges as\r\nif it had been cast in iron; the juicy-stemmed creepers fell away from\r\nthe trees they clung to and died at their feet; the bamboos withered,\r\nclanking when the hot winds blew, and the moss peeled off the rocks deep\r\nin the Jungle, till they were as bare and as hot as the quivering blue\r\nboulders in the bed of the stream.\r\n\r\nThe birds and the monkey-people went north early in the year, for they\r\nknew what was coming; and the deer and the wild pig broke far away to\r\nthe perished fields of the villages, dying sometimes before the eyes\r\nof men too weak to kill them. Chil, the Kite, stayed and grew fat, for\r\nthere was a great deal of carrion, and evening after evening he\r\nbrought the news to the beasts, too weak to force their way to fresh\r\nhunting-grounds, that the sun was killing the Jungle for three days\xe2\x80\x99\r\nflight in every direction.\r\n\r\nMowgli, who had never known what real hunger meant, fell back on stale\r\nhoney, three years old, scraped out of deserted rock-hives--honey black\r\nas a sloe, and dusty with dried sugar. He hunted, too, for deep-boring\r\ngrubs under the bark of the trees, and robbed the wasps of their new\r\nbroods. All the game in the jungle was no more than skin and bone, and\r\nBagheera could kill thrice in a night, and hardly get a full meal. But\r\nthe want of water was the worst, for though the Jungle People drink\r\nseldom they must drink deep.\r\n\r\nAnd the heat went on and on, and sucked up all the moisture, till at\r\nlast the main channel of the Waingunga was the only stream that carried\r\na trickle of water between its dead banks; and when Hathi, the wild\r\nelephant, who lives for a hundred years and more, saw a long, lean blue\r\nridge of rock show dry in the very centre of the stream, he knew that he\r\nwas looking at the Peace Rock, and then and there he lifted up his trunk\r\nand proclaimed the Water Truce, as his father before him had proclaimed\r\nit fifty years ago. The deer, wild pig, and buffalo took up the cry\r\nhoarsely; and Chil, the Kite, flew in great circles far and wide,\r\nwhistling and shrieking the warning.\r\n\r\nBy the Law of the Jungle it is death to kill at the drinking-places\r\nwhen once the Water Truce has been declared. The reason of this is that\r\ndrinking comes before eating. Every one in the Jungle can scramble along\r\nsomehow when only game is scarce; but water is water, and when there is\r\nbut one source of supply, all hunting stops while the Jungle People go\r\nthere for their needs. In good seasons, when water was plentiful, those\r\nwho came down to drink at the Waingunga--or anywhere else, for that\r\nmatter--did so at the risk of their lives, and that risk made no small\r\npart of the fascination of the night\xe2\x80\x99s doings. To move down so cunningly\r\nthat never a leaf stirred; to wade knee-deep in the roaring shallows\r\nthat drown all noise from behind; to drink, looking backward over one\r\nshoulder, every muscle ready for the first desperate bound of keen\r\nterror; to roll on the sandy margin, and return, wet-muzzled and well\r\nplumped out, to the admiring herd, was a thing that all tall-antlered\r\nyoung bucks took a delight in, precisely because they knew that at any\r\nmoment Bagheera or Shere Khan might leap upon them and bear them down.\r\nBut now all that life-and-death fun was ended, and the Jungle People\r\ncame up, starved and weary, to the shrunken river,--tiger, bear, deer,\r\nbuffalo, and pig, all together,--drank the fouled waters, and hung above\r\nthem, too exhausted to move off.\r\n\r\nThe deer and the pig had tramped all day in search of something better\r\nthan dried bark and withered leaves. The buffaloes had found no wallows\r\nto be cool in, and no green crops to steal. The snakes had left the\r\nJungle and come down to the river in the hope of finding a stray frog.\r\nThey curled round wet stones, and never offered to strike when the nose\r\nof a rooting pig dislodged them. The river-turtles had long ago been\r\nkilled by Bagheera, cleverest of hunters, and the fish had buried\r\nthemselves deep in the dry mud. Only the Peace Rock lay across the\r\nshallows like a long snake, and the little tired ripples hissed as they\r\ndried on its hot side.\r\n\r\nIt was here that Mowgli came nightly for the cool and the companionship.\r\nThe most hungry of his enemies would hardly have cared for the boy then,\r\nHis naked hide made him seem more lean and wretched than any of his\r\nfellows. His hair was bleached to tow colour by the sun; his ribs stood\r\nout like the ribs of a basket, and the lumps on his knees and elbows,\r\nwhere he was used to track on all fours, gave his shrunken limbs the\r\nlook of knotted grass-stems. But his eye, under his matted forelock, was\r\ncool and quiet, for Bagheera was his adviser in this time of trouble,\r\nand told him to go quietly, hunt slowly, and never, on any account, to\r\nlose his temper.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is an evil time,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Black Panther, one furnace-hot evening,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cbut it will go if we can live till the end. Is thy stomach full,\r\nMan-cub?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere is stuff in my stomach, but I get no good of it. Think you,\r\nBagheera, the Rains have forgotten us and will never come again?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot I! We shall see the mohwa in blossom yet, and the little fawns all\r\nfat with new grass. Come down to the Peace Rock and hear the news. On my\r\nback, Little Brother.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThis is no time to carry weight. I can still stand alone, but--indeed\r\nwe be no fatted bullocks, we two.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBagheera looked along his ragged, dusty flank and whispered. \xe2\x80\x9cLast night\r\nI killed a bullock under the yoke. So low was I brought that I think I\r\nshould not have dared to spring if he had been loose. WOU!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMowgli laughed. \xe2\x80\x9cYes, we be great hunters now,\xe2\x80\x9d said he. \xe2\x80\x9cI am very\r\nbold--to eat grubs,\xe2\x80\x9d and the two came down together through the\r\ncrackling undergrowth to the river-bank and the lace-work of shoals that\r\nran out from it in every direction.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe water cannot live long,\xe2\x80\x9d said Baloo, joining them. \xe2\x80\x9cLook across.\r\nYonder are trails like the roads of Man.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nOn the level plain of the farther bank the stiff jungle-grass had died\r\nstanding, and, dying, had mummied. The beaten tracks of the deer and\r\nthe pig, all heading toward the river, had striped that colourless plain\r\nwith dusty gullies driven through the ten-foot grass, and, early as it\r\nwas, each long avenue was full of first-comers hastening to the water.\r\nYou could hear the does and fawns coughing in the snuff-like dust.\r\n\r\nUp-stream, at the bend of the sluggish pool round the Peace Rock, and\r\nWarden of the Water Truce, stood Hathi, the wild elephant, with his\r\nsons, gaunt and gray in the moonlight, rocking to and fro--always\r\nrocking. Below him a little were the vanguard of the deer; below these,\r\nagain, the pig and the wild buffalo; and on the opposite bank, where the\r\ntall trees came down to the water\xe2\x80\x99s edge, was the place set apart for\r\nthe Eaters of Flesh--the tiger, the wolves, the panther, the bear, and\r\nthe others.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe are under one Law, indeed,\xe2\x80\x9d said Bagheera, wading into the water and\r\nlooking across at the lines of clicking horns and starting eyes where\r\nthe deer and the pig pushed each other to and fro. \xe2\x80\x9cGood hunting, all\r\nyou of my blood,\xe2\x80\x9d he added, lying own at full length, one flank thrust\r\nout of the shallows; and then, between his teeth, \xe2\x80\x9cBut for that which is\r\nthe Law it would be VERY good hunting.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe quick-spread ears of the deer caught the last sentence, and a\r\nfrightened whisper ran along the ranks. \xe2\x80\x9cThe Truce! Remember the Truce!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPeace there, peace!\xe2\x80\x9d gurgled Hathi, the wild elephant. \xe2\x80\x9cThe Truce\r\nholds, Bagheera. This is no time to talk of hunting.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWho should know better than I?\xe2\x80\x9d Bagheera answered, rolling his yellow\r\neyes up-stream. \xe2\x80\x9cI am an eater of turtles--a fisher of frogs. Ngaayah!\r\nWould I could get good from chewing branches!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWE wish so, very greatly,\xe2\x80\x9d bleated a young fawn, who had only been born\r\nthat spring, and did not at all like it. Wretched as the Jungle People\r\nwere, even Hathi could not help chuckling; while Mowgli, lying on his\r\nelbows in the warm water, laughed aloud, and beat up the scum with his\r\nfeet.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell spoken, little bud-horn,\xe2\x80\x9d Bagheera purred. \xe2\x80\x9cWhen the Truce ends\r\nthat shall be remembered in thy favour,\xe2\x80\x9d and he looked keenly through\r\nthe darkness to make sure of recognising the fawn again.\r\n\r\nGradually the talking spread up and down the drinking-places. One could\r\nhear the scuffling, snorting pig asking for more room; the buffaloes\r\ngrunting among themselves as they lurched out across the sand-bars, and\r\nthe deer telling pitiful stories of their long foot-sore wanderings in\r\nquest of food. Now and again they asked some question of the Eaters of\r\nFlesh across the river, but all the news was bad, and the roaring hot\r\nwind of the Jungle came and went between the rocks and the rattling\r\nbranches, and scattered twigs, and dust on the water.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe men-folk, too, they die beside their ploughs,\xe2\x80\x9d said a young\r\nsambhur. \xe2\x80\x9cI passed three between sunset and night. They lay still, and\r\ntheir Bullocks with them. We also shall lie still in a little.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe river has fallen since last night,\xe2\x80\x9d said Baloo. \xe2\x80\x9cO Hathi, hast thou\r\never seen the like of this drought?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt will pass, it will pass,\xe2\x80\x9d said Hathi, squirting water along his back\r\nand sides.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe have one here that cannot endure long,\xe2\x80\x9d said Baloo; and he looked\r\ntoward the boy he loved.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI?\xe2\x80\x9d said Mowgli indignantly, sitting up in the water. \xe2\x80\x9cI have no long\r\nfur to cover my bones, but--but if THY hide were taken off, Baloo----\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHathi shook all over at the idea, and Baloo said severely:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMan-cub, that is not seemly to tell a Teacher of the Law. Never have I\r\nbeen seen without my hide.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNay, I meant no harm, Baloo; but only that thou art, as it were, like\r\nthe cocoanut in the husk, and I am the same cocoanut all naked. Now that\r\nbrown husk of thine----\xe2\x80\x9d Mowgli was sitting cross-legged, and explaining\r\nthings with his forefinger in his usual way, when Bagheera put out a\r\npaddy paw and pulled him over backward into the water.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWorse and worse,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Black Panther, as the boy rose spluttering.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFirst Baloo is to be skinned, and now he is a cocoanut. Be careful that\r\nhe does not do what the ripe cocoanuts do.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd what is that?\xe2\x80\x9d said Mowgli, off his guard for the minute, though\r\nthat is one of the oldest catches in the Jungle.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBreak thy head,\xe2\x80\x9d said Bagheera quietly, pulling him under again.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is not good to make a jest of thy teacher,\xe2\x80\x9d said the bear, when\r\nMowgli had been ducked for the third time.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot good! What would ye have? That naked thing running to and fro makes\r\na monkey-jest of those who have once been good hunters, and pulls the\r\nbest of us by the whiskers for sport.\xe2\x80\x9d This was Shere Khan, the Lame\r\nTiger, limping down to the water. He waited a little to enjoy the\r\nsensation he made among the deer on the opposite to lap, growling: \xe2\x80\x9cThe\r\njungle has become a whelping-ground for naked cubs now. Look at me,\r\nMan-cub!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMowgli looked--stared, rather--as insolently as he knew how, and in\r\na minute Shere Khan turned away uneasily. \xe2\x80\x9cMan-cub this, and Man-cub\r\nthat,\xe2\x80\x9d he rumbled, going on with his drink, \xe2\x80\x9cthe cub is neither man nor\r\ncub, or he would have been afraid. Next season I shall have to beg his\r\nleave for a drink. Augrh!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat may come, too,\xe2\x80\x9d said Bagheera, looking him steadily between the\r\neyes. \xe2\x80\x9cThat may come, too--Faugh, Shere Khan!--what new shame hast thou\r\nbrought here?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe Lame Tiger had dipped his chin and jowl in the water, and dark, oily\r\nstreaks were floating from it down-stream.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMan!\xe2\x80\x9d said Shere Khan coolly, \xe2\x80\x9cI killed an hour since.\xe2\x80\x9d He went on\r\npurring and growling to himself.\r\n\r\nThe line of beasts shook and wavered to and fro, and a whisper went\r\nup that grew to a cry. \xe2\x80\x9cMan! Man! He has killed Man!\xe2\x80\x9d Then all looked\r\ntowards Hathi, the wild elephant, but he seemed not to hear. Hathi never\r\ndoes anything till the time comes, and that is one of the reasons why he\r\nlives so long.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAt such a season as this to kill Man! Was no other game afoot?\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nBagheera scornfully, drawing himself out of the tainted water, and\r\nshaking each paw, cat-fashion, as he did so.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI killed for choice--not for food.\xe2\x80\x9d The horrified whisper began again,\r\nand Hathi\xe2\x80\x99s watchful little white eye cocked itself in Shere Khan\xe2\x80\x99s\r\ndirection. \xe2\x80\x9cFor choice,\xe2\x80\x9d Shere Khan drawled. \xe2\x80\x9cNow come I to drink and\r\nmake me clean again. Is there any to forbid?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBagheera\xe2\x80\x99s back began to curve like a bamboo in a high wind, but Hathi\r\nlifted up his trunk and spoke quietly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThy kill was from choice?\xe2\x80\x9d he asked; and when Hathi asks a question it\r\nis best to answer.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cEven so. It was my right and my Night. Thou knowest, O Hathi.\xe2\x80\x9d Shere\r\nKhan spoke almost courteously.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, I know,\xe2\x80\x9d Hathi answered; and, after a little silence, \xe2\x80\x9cHast thou\r\ndrunk thy fill?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFor to-night, yes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGo, then. The river is to drink, and not to defile. None but the Lame\r\nTiger would so have boasted of his right at this season when--when we\r\nsuffer together--Man and Jungle People alike. Clean or unclean, get to\r\nthy lair, Shere Khan!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe last words rang out like silver trumpets, and Hathi\xe2\x80\x99s three sons\r\nrolled forward half a pace, though there was no need. Shere Khan slunk\r\naway, not daring to growl, for he knew--what every one else knows--that\r\nwhen the last comes to the last, Hathi is the Master of the Jungle.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat is this right Shere Khan speaks of?\xe2\x80\x9d Mowgli whispered in\r\nBagheera\xe2\x80\x99s ear. \xe2\x80\x9cTo kill Man is always, shameful. The Law says so. And\r\nyet Hathi says----\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAsk him. I do not know, Little Brother. Right or no right, if Hathi had\r\nnot spoken I would have taught that lame butcher his lesson. To come\r\nto the Peace Rock fresh from a kill of Man--and to boast of it--is a\r\njackal\xe2\x80\x99s trick. Besides, he tainted the good water.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMowgli waited for a minute to pick up his courage, because no one cared\r\nto address Hathi directly, and then he cried: \xe2\x80\x9cWhat is Shere Khan\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nright, O Hathi?\xe2\x80\x9d Both banks echoed his words, for all the People of the\r\nJungle are intensely curious, and they had just seen something that none\r\nexcept Baloo, who looked very thoughtful, seemed to understand.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is an old tale,\xe2\x80\x9d said Hathi; \xe2\x80\x9ca tale older than the Jungle. Keep\r\nsilence along the banks and I will tell that tale.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThere was a minute or two of pushing a shouldering among the pigs\r\nand the buffalo, and then the leaders of the herds grunted, one after\r\nanother, \xe2\x80\x9cWe wait,\xe2\x80\x9d and Hathi strode forward, till he was nearly\r\nknee-deep in the pool by the Peace Rock. Lean and wrinkled and\r\nyellow-tusked though he was, he looked what the Jungle knew him to\r\nbe--their master.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYe know, children,\xe2\x80\x9d he began, \xe2\x80\x9cthat of all things ye most fear Man;\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n and there was a mutter of agreement.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThis tale touches thee, Little Brother,\xe2\x80\x9d said Bagheera to Mowgli.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI? I am of the Pack--a hunter of the Free People,\xe2\x80\x9d Mowgli answered.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat have I to do with Man?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd ye do not know why ye fear Man?\xe2\x80\x9d Hathi went on. \xe2\x80\x9cThis is the\r\nreason. In the beginning of the Jungle, and none know when that was, we\r\nof the Jungle walked together, having no fear of one another. In those\r\ndays there was no drought, and leaves and flowers and fruit grew on the\r\nsame tree, and we ate nothing at all except leaves and flowers and grass\r\nand fruit and bark.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am glad I was not born in those days,\xe2\x80\x9d said Bagheera. \xe2\x80\x9cBark is only\r\ngood to sharpen claws.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd the Lord of the Jungle was Tha, the First of the Elephants. He drew\r\nthe Jungle out of deep waters with his trunk; and where he made furrows\r\nin the ground with his tusks, there the rivers ran; and where he struck\r\nwith his foot, there rose ponds of good water; and when he blew through\r\nhis trunk,--thus,--the trees fell. That was the manner in which the\r\nJungle was made by Tha; and so the tale was told to me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt has not lost fat in the telling,\xe2\x80\x9d Bagheera whispered, and Mowgli\r\nlaughed behind his hand.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIn those days there was no corn or melons or pepper or sugar-cane,\r\nnor were there any little huts such as ye have all seen; and the Jungle\r\nPeople knew nothing of Man, but lived in the Jungle together, making\r\none people. But presently they began to dispute over their food, though\r\nthere was grazing enough for all. They were lazy. Each wished to eat\r\nwhere he lay, as sometimes we can do now when the spring rains are good.\r\nTha, the First of the Elephants, was busy making new jungles and leading\r\nthe rivers in their beds. He could not walk in all places; therefore he\r\nmade the First of the Tigers the master and the judge of the Jungle, to\r\nwhom the Jungle People should bring their disputes. In those days the\r\nFirst of the Tigers ate fruit and grass with the others. He was as large\r\nas I am, and he was very beautiful, in colour all over like the blossom\r\nof the yellow creeper. There was never stripe nor bar upon his hide in\r\nthose good days when this the Jungle was new. All the Jungle People came\r\nbefore him without fear, and his word was the Law of all the Jungle. We\r\nwere then, remember ye, one people.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYet upon a night there was a dispute between two bucks--a\r\ngrazing-quarrel such as ye now settle with the horns and the\r\nfore-feet--and it is said that as the two spoke together before the\r\nFirst of the First of the Tigers lying among the flowers, a buck pushed\r\nhim with his horns, and the First of the Tigers forgot that he was the\r\nmaster and judge of the Jungle, and, leaping upon that buck, broke his\r\nneck.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTill that night never one of us had died, and the First of the Tigers,\r\nseeing what he had done, and being made foolish by the scent of the\r\nblood, ran away into the marshes of the North, and we of the Jungle,\r\nleft without a judge, fell to fighting among ourselves; and Tha heard\r\nthe noise of it and came back. Then some of us said this and some of us\r\nsaid that, but he saw the dead buck among the flowers, and asked who\r\nhad killed, and we of the Jungle would not tell because the smell of the\r\nblood made us foolish. We ran to and fro in circles, capering and crying\r\nout and shaking our heads. Then Tha gave an order to the trees that hang\r\nlow, and to the trailing creepers of the Jungle, that they should mark\r\nthe killer of the buck so that he should know him again, and he said,\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Who will now be master of the Jungle People?\xe2\x80\x99 Then up leaped the Gray\r\nApe who lives in the branches, and said, \xe2\x80\x98I will now be master of the\r\nJungle.\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAt this Tha laughed, and said, \xe2\x80\x9cSo be it,\xe2\x80\x9d and went away very angry.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cChildren, ye know the Gray Ape. He was then as he is now. At the first\r\nhe made a wise face for himself, but in a little while he began to\r\nscratch and to leap up and down, and when Tha came back he found the\r\nGray Ape hanging, head down, from a bough, mocking those who stood\r\nbelow; and they mocked him again. And so there was no Law in the\r\nJungle--only foolish talk and senseless words.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen Tha called us all together and said: \xe2\x80\x98The first of your masters\r\nhas brought Death into the Jungle, and the second Shame. Now it is time\r\nthere was a Law, and a Law that ye must not break. Now ye shall know\r\nFear, and when ye have found him ye shall know that he is your master,\r\nand the rest shall follow.\xe2\x80\x99 Then we of the jungle said, \xe2\x80\x98What is Fear?\xe2\x80\x99\r\nAnd Tha said, \xe2\x80\x98Seek till ye find.\xe2\x80\x99 So we went up and down the Jungle\r\nseeking for Fear, and presently the buffaloes----\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cUgh!\xe2\x80\x9d said Mysa, the leader of the buffaloes, from their sand-bank.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, Mysa, it was the buffaloes. They came back with the news that in a\r\ncave in the Jungle sat Fear, and that he had no hair, and went upon his\r\nhind legs. Then we of the Jungle followed the herd till we came to that\r\ncave, and Fear stood at the mouth of it, and he was, as the buffaloes\r\nhad said, hairless, and he walked upon his hinder legs. When he saw us\r\nhe cried out, and his voice filled us with the fear that we have now of\r\nthat voice when we hear it, and we ran away, tramping upon and tearing\r\neach other because we were afraid. That night, so it was told to me, we\r\nof the Jungle did not lie down together as used to be our custom, but\r\neach tribe drew off by itself--the pig with the pig, the deer with the\r\ndeer; horn to horn, hoof to hoof,--like keeping to like, and so lay\r\nshaking in the Jungle.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOnly the First of the Tigers was not with us, for he was still hidden\r\nin the marshes of the North, and when word was brought to him of the\r\nThing we had seen in the cave, he said. \xe2\x80\x98I will go to this Thing and\r\nbreak his neck.\xe2\x80\x99 So he ran all the night till he came to the cave; but\r\nthe trees and the creepers on his path, remembering the order that Tha\r\nhad given, let down their branches and marked him as he ran, drawing\r\ntheir fingers across his back, his flank, his forehead, and his jowl.\r\nWherever they touched him there was a mark and a stripe upon his yellow\r\nhide. AND THOSE STRIPES DO THIS CHILDREN WEAR TO THIS DAY! When he came\r\nto the cave, Fear, the Hairless One, put out his hand and called him\r\n\xe2\x80\x98The Striped One that comes by night,\xe2\x80\x99 and the First of the Tigers was\r\nafraid of the Hairless One, and ran back to the swamps howling.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMowgli chuckled quietly here, his chin in the water.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo loud did he howl that Tha heard him and said, \xe2\x80\x98What is the sorrow?\xe2\x80\x99\r\nAnd the First of the Tigers, lifting up his muzzle to the new-made sky,\r\nwhich is now so old, said: \xe2\x80\x98Give me back my power, O Tha. I am made\r\nashamed before all the Jungle, and I have run away from a Hairless One,\r\nand he has called me a shameful name.\xe2\x80\x99 \xe2\x80\x98And why?\xe2\x80\x99 said Tha. \xe2\x80\x98Because I\r\nam smeared with the mud of the marshes,\xe2\x80\x99 said the First of the Tigers.\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Swim, then, and roll on the wet grass, and if it be mud it will wash\r\naway,\xe2\x80\x99 said Tha; and the First of the Tigers swam, and rolled and rolled\r\nupon the grass, till the Jungle ran round and round before his eyes, but\r\nnot one little bar upon all his hide was changed, and Tha, watching him,\r\nlaughed. Then the First of the Tigers said: \xe2\x80\x98What have I done that this\r\ncomes to me?\xe2\x80\x99 Tha said, \xe2\x80\x98Thou hast killed the buck, and thou hast let\r\nDeath loose in the Jungle, and with Death has come Fear, so that the\r\npeople of the Jungle are afraid one of the other, as thou art afraid of\r\nthe Hairless One.\xe2\x80\x99 The First of the Tigers said, \xe2\x80\x98They will never fear\r\nme, for I knew them since the beginning.\xe2\x80\x99 Tha said, \xe2\x80\x98Go and see.\xe2\x80\x99 And\r\nthe First of the Tigers ran to and fro, calling aloud to the deer and\r\nthe pig and the sambhur and the porcupine and all the Jungle Peoples,\r\nand they all ran away from him who had been their judge, because they\r\nwere afraid.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen the First of the Tigers came back, and his pride was broken in\r\nhim, and, beating his head upon the ground, he tore up the earth with\r\nall his feet and said: \xe2\x80\x98Remember that I was once the Master of the\r\nJungle. Do not forget me, O Tha! Let my children remember that I was\r\nonce without shame or fear!\xe2\x80\x99 And Tha said: \xe2\x80\x98This much I will do, because\r\nthou and I together saw the Jungle made. For one night in each year\r\nit shall be as it was before the buck was killed--for thee and for thy\r\nchildren. In that one night, if ye meet the Hairless One--and his name\r\nis Man--ye shall not be afraid of him, but he shall be afraid of you, as\r\nthough ye were judges of the Jungle and masters of all things. Show him\r\nmercy in that night of his fear, for thou hast known what Fear is.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen the First of the Tigers answered, \xe2\x80\x98I am content\xe2\x80\x99; but when next\r\nhe drank he saw the black stripes upon his flank and his side, and he\r\nremembered the name that the Hairless One had given him, and he was\r\nangry. For a year he lived in the marshes waiting till Tha should keep\r\nhis promise. And upon a night when the jackal of the Moon [the Evening\r\nStar] stood clear of the Jungle, he felt that his Night was upon him,\r\nand he went to that cave to meet the Hairless One. Then it happened as\r\nTha promised, for the Hairless One fell down before him and lay along\r\nthe ground, and the First of the Tigers struck him and broke his back,\r\nfor he thought that there was but one such Thing in the Jungle, and that\r\nhe had killed Fear. Then, nosing above the kill, he heard Tha coming\r\ndown from the woods of the North, and presently the voice of the First\r\nof the Elephants, which is the voice that we hear now----\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe thunder was rolling up and down the dry, scarred hills, but\r\nit brought no rain--only heat--lightning that flickered along the\r\nridges--and Hathi went on: \xe2\x80\x9cTHAT was the voice he heard, and it said:\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Is this thy mercy?\xe2\x80\x99 The First of the Tigers licked his lips and said:\r\n\xe2\x80\x98What matter? I have killed Fear.\xe2\x80\x99 And Tha said: \xe2\x80\x98O blind and foolish!\r\nThou hast untied the feet of Death, and he will follow thy trail till\r\nthou diest. Thou hast taught Man to kill!\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe First of the Tigers, standing stiffly to his kill, said. \xe2\x80\x98He is as\r\nthe buck was. There is no Fear. Now I will judge the Jungle Peoples once\r\nmore.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd Tha said: \xe2\x80\x98Never again shall the Jungle Peoples come to thee. They\r\nshall never cross thy trail, nor sleep near thee, nor follow after thee,\r\nnor browse by thy lair. Only Fear shall follow thee, and with a blow\r\nthat thou canst not see he shall bid thee wait his pleasure. He shall\r\nmake the ground to open under thy feet, and the creeper to twist about\r\nthy neck, and the tree-trunks to grow together about thee higher than\r\nthou canst leap, and at the last he shall take thy hide to wrap his cubs\r\nwhen they are cold. Thou hast shown him no mercy, and none will he show\r\nthee.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe First of the Tigers was very bold, for his Night was still on him,\r\nand he said: \xe2\x80\x98The Promise of Tha is the Promise of Tha. He will not take\r\naway my Night?\xe2\x80\x99 And Tha said: \xe2\x80\x98The one Night is thine, as I have said,\r\nbut there is a price to pay. Thou hast taught Man to kill, and he is no\r\nslow learner.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe First of the Tigers said: \xe2\x80\x98He is here under my foot, and his back\r\nis broken. Let the Jungle know I have killed Fear.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen Tha laughed, and said: \xe2\x80\x98Thou hast killed one of many, but thou\r\nthyself shalt tell the Jungle--for thy Night is ended.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo the day came; and from the mouth of the cave went out another\r\nHairless One, and he saw the kill in the path, and the First of the\r\nTigers above it, and he took a pointed stick----\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey throw a thing that cuts now,\xe2\x80\x9d said Ikki, rustling down the bank;\r\nfor Ikki was considered uncommonly good eating by the Gonds--they called\r\nhim Ho-Igoo--and he knew something of the wicked little Gondee axe that\r\nwhirls across a clearing like a dragon-fly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt was a pointed stick, such as they put in the foot of a pit-trap,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n said Hathi, \xe2\x80\x9cand throwing it, he struck the First of the Tigers deep in\r\nthe flank. Thus it happened as Tha said, for the First of the Tigers ran\r\nhowling up and down the Jungle till he tore out the stick, and all the\r\nJungle knew that the Hairless One could strike from far off, and they\r\nfeared more than before. So it came about that the First of the Tigers\r\ntaught the Hairless One to kill--and ye know what harm that has since\r\ndone to all our peoples--through the noose, and the pitfall, and the\r\nhidden trap, and the flying stick and the stinging fly that comes out of\r\nwhite smoke [Hathi meant the rifle], and the Red Flower that drives us\r\ninto the open. Yet for one night in the year the Hairless One fears the\r\nTiger, as Tha promised, and never has the Tiger given him cause to be\r\nless afraid. Where he finds him, there he kills him, remembering how the\r\nFirst of the Tigers was made ashamed. For the rest, Fear walks up and\r\ndown the Jungle by day and by night.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAhi! Aoo!\xe2\x80\x9d said the deer, thinking of what it all meant to them.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd only when there is one great Fear over all, as there is now, can we\r\nof the Jungle lay aside our little fears, and meet together in one place\r\nas we do now.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFor one night only does Man fear the Tiger?\xe2\x80\x9d said Mowgli.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFor one night only,\xe2\x80\x9d said Hathi.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut I--but we--but all the Jungle knows that Shere Khan kills Man twice\r\nand thrice in a moon.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cEven so. THEN he springs from behind and turns his head aside as he\r\nstrikes, for he is full of fear. If Man looked at him he would run. But\r\non his one Night he goes openly down to the village. He walks between\r\nthe houses and thrusts his head into the doorway, and the men fall on\r\ntheir faces, and there he does his kill. One kill in that Night.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh!\xe2\x80\x9d said Mowgli to himself, rolling over in the water. \xe2\x80\x9cNOW I see\r\nwhy it was Shere Khan bade me look at him! He got no good of it, for he\r\ncould not hold his eyes steady, and--and I certainly did not fall down\r\nat his feet. But then I am not a man, being of the Free People.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cUmm!\xe2\x80\x9d said Bagheera deep in his furry throat. \xe2\x80\x9cDoes the Tiger know his\r\nNight?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNever till the Jackal of the Moon stands clear of the evening\r\nmist. Sometimes it falls in the dry summer and sometimes in the wet\r\nrains--this one Night of the Tiger. But for the First of the Tigers,\r\nthis would never have been, nor would any of us have known fear.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe deer grunted sorrowfully and Bagheera\xe2\x80\x99s lips curled in a wicked\r\nsmile. \xe2\x80\x9cDo men know this--tale?\xe2\x80\x9d said he.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNone know it except the tigers, and we, the elephants--the children of\r\nTha. Now ye by the pools have heard it, and I have spoken.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHathi dipped his trunk into the water as a sign that he did not wish to\r\ntalk.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut--but--but,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mowgli, turning to Baloo, \xe2\x80\x9cwhy did not the First\r\nof the Tigers continue to eat grass and leaves and trees? He did but\r\nbreak the buck\xe2\x80\x99s neck. He did not EAT. What led him to the hot meat?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe trees and the creepers marked him, Little Brother, and made him\r\nthe striped thing that we see. Never again would he eat their fruit;\r\nbut from that day he revenged himself upon the deer, and the others, the\r\nEaters of Grass,\xe2\x80\x9d said Baloo.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen THOU knowest the tale. Heh? Why have I never heard?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBecause the Jungle is full of such tales. If I made a beginning there\r\nwould never be an end to them. Let go my ear, Little Brother.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nTHE LAW OF THE JUNGLE\r\n\r\nJust to give you an idea of the immense variety of the Jungle Law,\r\nI have translated into verse (Baloo always recited them in a sort of\r\nsing-song) a few of the laws that apply to the wolves. There are, of\r\ncourse, hundreds and hundreds more, but these will do for specimens of\r\nthe simpler rulings.\r\n\r\n     Now this is the Law of the Jungle--as old and as true as\r\n        the sky;\r\n     And the Wolf that shall keep it may prosper, but the Wolf\r\n        that shall break it must die.\r\n\r\n     As the creeper that girdles the tree-trunk the Law runneth\r\n        forward and back--\r\n     For the strength of the Pack is the Wolf, and the strength\r\n        of the Wolf is the Pack.\r\n\r\n     Wash daily from nose-tip to tail-tip; drink deeply, but\r\n        never too deep;\r\n     And remember the night is for hunting, and forget not\r\n        the day is for sleep.\r\n\r\n     The jackal may follow the Tiger, but, Cub, when thy\r\n        whiskers are grown,\r\n     Remember the Wolf is a hunter--go forth and get food\r\n        of thine own.\r\n\r\n     Keep peace with the Lords of the Jungle--the Tiger, the\r\n        Panther, the Bear;\r\n     And trouble not Hathi the Silent, and mock not the Boar\r\n        in his lair.\r\n\r\n     When Pack meets with Pack in the Jungle, and neither\r\n        will go from the trail,\r\n     Lie down till the leaders have spoken--it may be fair\r\n        words shall prevail.\r\n\r\n     When ye fight with a Wolf of the Pack, ye must\r\n        fight him alone and afar,\r\n     Lest others take part in the quarrel, and the Pack be\r\n        diminished by war.\r\n\r\n     The Lair of the Wolf is his refuge, and where he has\r\n        made him his home,\r\n     Not even the Head Wolf may enter, not even the Council\r\n        may come.\r\n\r\n     The Lair of the Wolf is his refuge, but where he has\r\n        digged it too plain,\r\n     The Council shall send him a message, and so he shall\r\n        change it again.\r\n\r\n     If ye kill before midnight, be silent, and wake not the\r\n        woods with your bay,\r\n     Lest ye frighten the deer from the crops, and the brothers\r\n        go empty away.\r\n\r\n     Ye may kill for yourselves, and your mates, and your cubs\r\n        as they need, and ye can;\r\n     But kill not for pleasure of killing, and SEVEN TIMES NEVER\r\n        KILL MAN.\r\n\r\n     If ye plunder his Kill from a weaker, devour not all in\r\n        thy pride;\r\n     Pack-Right is the right of the meanest; so leave him the\r\n        head and the hide.\r\n\r\n     The Kill of the Pack is the meat of the Pack. Ye must\r\n        eat where it lies;\r\n     And no one may carry away of that meat to his lair, or\r\n        he dies.\r\n\r\n     The Kill of the Wolf is the meat of the Wolf. He may\r\n        do what he will,\r\n     But, till he has given permission, the Pack may not eat\r\n        of that Kill.\r\n\r\n     Cub-Right is the right of the Yearling. From all of his\r\n        Pack he may claim\r\n     Full-gorge when the killer has eaten; and none may\r\n        refuse him the same.\r\n\r\n     Lair-Right is the right of the Mother. From all of her\r\n        year she may claim\r\n     One haunch of each kill for her litter, and none may\r\n        deny her the same.\r\n\r\n     Cave-Right is the right of the Father--to hunt by himself\r\n        for his own.\r\n     He is freed of all calls to the Pack; he is judged by the\r\n        Council alone.\r\n\r\n     Because of his age and his cunning, because of his gripe\r\n        and his paw,\r\n     In all that the Law leaveth open, the word of the Head\r\n        Wolf is Law.\r\n\r\n     Now these are the Laws of the Jungle, and many and\r\n        mighty are they;\r\n     But the head and the hoof of the Law and the haunch\r\n        and the hump is--Obey!\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nTHE MIRACLE OF PURUN BHAGAT\r\n\r\n     The night we felt the earth would move\r\n       We stole and plucked him by the hand,\r\n     Because we loved him with the love\r\n       That knows but cannot understand.\r\n\r\n     And when the roaring hillside broke,\r\n       And all our world fell down in rain,\r\n     We saved him, we the Little Folk;\r\n       But lo! he does not come again!\r\n\r\n     Mourn now, we saved him for the sake\r\n       Of such poor love as wild ones may.\r\n     Mourn ye! Our brother will not wake,\r\n       And his own kind drive us away!\r\n\r\n                             Dirge of the Langurs.\r\n\r\n\r\nThere was once a man in India who was Prime Minister of one of the\r\nsemi-independent native States in the north-western part of the country.\r\nHe was a Brahmin, so high-caste that caste ceased to have any particular\r\nmeaning for him; and his father had been an important official in the\r\ngay-coloured tag-rag and bobtail of an old-fashioned Hindu Court. But\r\nas Purun Dass grew up he felt that the old order of things was changing,\r\nand that if any one wished to get on in the world he must stand well\r\nwith the English, and imitate all that the English believed to be good.\r\nAt the same time a native official must keep his own master\xe2\x80\x99s favour.\r\nThis was a difficult game, but the quiet, close-mouthed young Brahmin,\r\nhelped by a good English education at a Bombay University, played it\r\ncoolly, and rose, step by step, to be Prime Minister of the kingdom.\r\nThat is to say, he held more real power than his master the Maharajah.\r\n\r\nWhen the old king--who was suspicious of the English, their railways and\r\ntelegraphs--died, Purun Dass stood high with his young successor, who\r\nhad been tutored by an Englishman; and between them, though he always\r\ntook care that his master should have the credit, they established\r\nschools for little girls, made roads, and started State dispensaries and\r\nshows of agricultural implements, and published a yearly blue-book on\r\nthe \xe2\x80\x9cMoral and Material Progress of the State,\xe2\x80\x9d and the Foreign Office\r\nand the Government of India were delighted. Very few native States take\r\nup English progress altogether, for they will not believe, as Purun Dass\r\nshowed he did, that what was good for the Englishman must be twice as\r\ngood for the Asiatic. The Prime Minister became the honoured friend\r\nof Viceroys, and Governors, and Lieutenant-Governors, and medical\r\nmissionaries, and common missionaries, and hard-riding English officers\r\nwho came to shoot in the State preserves, as well as of whole hosts of\r\ntourists who travelled up and down India in the cold weather, showing\r\nhow things ought to be managed. In his spare time he would endow\r\nscholarships for the study of medicine and manufactures on strictly\r\nEnglish lines, and write letters to the \xe2\x80\x9cPioneer\xe2\x80\x9d, the greatest Indian\r\ndaily paper, explaining his master\xe2\x80\x99s aims and objects.\r\n\r\nAt last he went to England on a visit, and had to pay enormous sums to\r\nthe priests when he came back; for even so high-caste a Brahmin as Purun\r\nDass lost caste by crossing the black sea. In London he met and talked\r\nwith every one worth knowing--men whose names go all over the world--and\r\nsaw a great deal more than he said. He was given honorary degrees by\r\nlearned universities, and he made speeches and talked of Hindu social\r\nreform to English ladies in evening dress, till all London cried, \xe2\x80\x9cThis\r\nis the most fascinating man we have ever met at dinner since cloths were\r\nfirst laid.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWhen he returned to India there was a blaze of glory, for the Viceroy\r\nhimself made a special visit to confer upon the Maharajah the Grand\r\nCross of the Star of India--all diamonds and ribbons and enamel; and at\r\nthe same ceremony, while the cannon boomed, Purun Dass was made a Knight\r\nCommander of the Order of the Indian Empire; so that his name stood Sir\r\nPurun Dass, K.C.I.E.\r\n\r\nThat evening, at dinner in the big Viceregal tent, he stood up with the\r\nbadge and the collar of the Order on his breast, and replying to the\r\ntoast of his master\xe2\x80\x99s health, made a speech few Englishmen could have\r\nbettered.\r\n\r\nNext month, when the city had returned to its sun-baked quiet, he did\r\na thing no Englishman would have dreamed of doing; for, so far as the\r\nworld\xe2\x80\x99s affairs went, he died. The jewelled order of his knighthood went\r\nback to the Indian Government, and a new Prime Minister was appointed to\r\nthe charge of affairs, and a great game of General Post began in all the\r\nsubordinate appointments. The priests knew what had happened, and the\r\npeople guessed; but India is the one place in the world where a man can\r\ndo as he pleases and nobody asks why; and the fact that Dewan Sir Purun\r\nDass, K.C.I.E., had resigned position, palace, and power, and taken up\r\nthe begging-bowl and ochre-coloured dress of a Sunnyasi, or holy man,\r\nwas considered nothing extraordinary. He had been, as the Old Law\r\nrecommends, twenty years a youth, twenty years a fighter,--though he\r\nhad never carried a weapon in his life,--and twenty years head of a\r\nhousehold. He had used his wealth and his power for what he knew both to\r\nbe worth; he had taken honour when it came his way; he had seen men and\r\ncities far and near, and men and cities had stood up and honoured him.\r\nNow he would let those things go, as a man drops the cloak he no longer\r\nneeds.\r\n\r\nBehind him, as he walked through the city gates, an antelope skin and\r\nbrass-handled crutch under his arm, and a begging-bowl of polished\r\nbrown coco-de-mer in his hand, barefoot, alone, with eyes cast on the\r\nground--behind him they were firing salutes from the bastions in honour\r\nof his happy successor. Purun Dass nodded. All that life was ended;\r\nand he bore it no more ill-will or good-will than a man bears to a\r\ncolourless dream of the night. He was a Sunnyasi--a houseless, wandering\r\nmendicant, depending on his neighbours for his daily bread; and so\r\nlong as there is a morsel to divide in India, neither priest nor beggar\r\nstarves. He had never in his life tasted meat, and very seldom eaten\r\neven fish. A five-pound note would have covered his personal expenses\r\nfor food through any one of the many years in which he had been absolute\r\nmaster of millions of money. Even when he was being lionised in London\r\nhe had held before him his dream of peace and quiet--the long, white,\r\ndusty Indian road, printed all over with bare feet, the incessant,\r\nslow-moving traffic, and the sharp-smelling wood smoke curling up under\r\nthe fig-trees in the twilight, where the wayfarers sit at their evening\r\nmeal.\r\n\r\nWhen the time came to make that dream true the Prime Minister took\r\nthe proper steps, and in three days you might more easily have found a\r\nbubble in the trough of the long Atlantic seas, than Purun Dass among\r\nthe roving, gathering, separating millions of India.\r\n\r\nAt night his antelope skin was spread where the darkness overtook\r\nhim--sometimes in a Sunnyasi monastery by the roadside; sometimes by a\r\nmud-pillar shrine of Kala Pir, where the Jogis, who are another misty\r\ndivision of holy men, would receive him as they do those who know what\r\ncastes and divisions are worth; sometimes on the outskirts of a little\r\nHindu village, where the children would steal up with the food\r\ntheir parents had prepared; and sometimes on the pitch of the bare\r\ngrazing-grounds, where the flame of his stick fire waked the drowsy\r\ncamels. It was all one to Purun Dass--or Purun Bhagat, as he called\r\nhimself now. Earth, people, and food were all one. But unconsciously\r\nhis feet drew him away northward and eastward; from the south to\r\nRohtak; from Rohtak to Kurnool; from Kurnool to ruined Samanah, and then\r\nup-stream along the dried bed of the Gugger river that fills only when\r\nthe rain falls in the hills, till one day he saw the far line of the\r\ngreat Himalayas.\r\n\r\nThen Purun Bhagat smiled, for he remembered that his mother was of\r\nRajput Brahmin birth, from Kulu way--a Hill-woman, always home-sick for\r\nthe snows--and that the least touch of Hill blood draws a man in the end\r\nback to where he belongs.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYonder,\xe2\x80\x9d said Purun Bhagat, breasting the lower slopes of the Sewaliks,\r\nwhere the cacti stand up like seven-branched candlesticks-\xe2\x80\x9cyonder I\r\nshall sit down and get knowledge\xe2\x80\x9d; and the cool wind of the Himalayas\r\nwhistled about his ears as he trod the road that led to Simla.\r\n\r\nThe last time he had come that way it had been in state, with a\r\nclattering cavalry escort, to visit the gentlest and most affable of\r\nViceroys; and the two had talked for an hour together about mutual\r\nfriends in London, and what the Indian common folk really thought of\r\nthings. This time Purun Bhagat paid no calls, but leaned on the rail\r\nof the Mall, watching that glorious view of the Plains spread out\r\nforty miles below, till a native Mohammedan policeman told him he was\r\nobstructing traffic; and Purun Bhagat salaamed reverently to the Law,\r\nbecause he knew the value of it, and was seeking for a Law of his own.\r\nThen he moved on, and slept that night in an empty hut at Chota Simla,\r\nwhich looks like the very last end of the earth, but it was only the\r\nbeginning of his journey. He followed the Himalaya-Thibet road, the\r\nlittle ten-foot track that is blasted out of solid rock, or strutted out\r\non timbers over gulfs a thousand feet deep; that dips into warm, wet,\r\nshut-in valleys, and climbs out across bare, grassy hill-shoulders where\r\nthe sun strikes like a burning-glass; or turns through dripping, dark\r\nforests where the tree-ferns dress the trunks from head to heel, and the\r\npheasant calls to his mate. And he met Thibetan herdsmen with their dogs\r\nand flocks of sheep, each sheep with a little bag of borax on his back,\r\nand wandering wood-cutters, and cloaked and blanketed Lamas from\r\nThibet, coming into India on pilgrimage, and envoys of little solitary\r\nHill-states, posting furiously on ring-streaked and piebald ponies, or\r\nthe cavalcade of a Rajah paying a visit; or else for a long, clear day\r\nhe would see nothing more than a black bear grunting and rooting below\r\nin the valley. When he first started, the roar of the world he had left\r\nstill rang in his ears, as the roar of a tunnel rings long after the\r\ntrain has passed through; but when he had put the Mutteeanee Pass behind\r\nhim that was all done, and Purun Bhagat was alone with himself, walking,\r\nwondering, and thinking, his eyes on the ground, and his thoughts with\r\nthe clouds.\r\n\r\nOne evening he crossed the highest pass he had met till then--it had\r\nbeen a two-day\xe2\x80\x99s climb--and came out on a line of snow-peaks that banded\r\nall the horizon--mountains from fifteen to twenty thousand feet high,\r\nlooking almost near enough to hit with a stone, though they were\r\nfifty or sixty miles away. The pass was crowned with dense, dark\r\nforest--deodar, walnut, wild cherry, wild olive, and wild pear, but\r\nmostly deodar, which is the Himalayan cedar; and under the shadow of the\r\ndeodars stood a deserted shrine to Kali--who is Durga, who is Sitala,\r\nwho is sometimes worshipped against the smallpox.\r\n\r\nPurun Dass swept the stone floor clean, smiled at the grinning statue,\r\nmade himself a little mud fireplace at the back of the shrine,\r\nspread his antelope skin on a bed of fresh pine-needles, tucked his\r\nbairagi--his brass-handled crutch--under his armpit, and sat down to\r\nrest.\r\n\r\nImmediately below him the hillside fell away, clean and cleared for\r\nfifteen hundred feet, where a little village of stone-walled houses,\r\nwith roofs of beaten earth, clung to the steep tilt. All round it the\r\ntiny terraced fields lay out like aprons of patchwork on the knees of\r\nthe mountain, and cows no bigger than beetles grazed between the smooth\r\nstone circles of the threshing-floors. Looking across the valley, the\r\neye was deceived by the size of things, and could not at first realise\r\nthat what seemed to be low scrub, on the opposite mountain-flank, was\r\nin truth a forest of hundred-foot pines. Purun Bhagat saw an eagle swoop\r\nacross the gigantic hollow, but the great bird dwindled to a dot ere it\r\nwas half-way over. A few bands of scattered clouds strung up and down\r\nthe valley, catching on a shoulder of the hills, or rising up and dying\r\nout when they were level with the head of the pass. And \xe2\x80\x9cHere shall I\r\nfind peace,\xe2\x80\x9d said Purun Bhagat.\r\n\r\nNow, a Hill-man makes nothing of a few hundred feet up or down, and as\r\nsoon as the villagers saw the smoke in the deserted shrine, the village\r\npriest climbed up the terraced hillside to welcome the stranger.\r\n\r\nWhen he met Purun Bhagat\xe2\x80\x99s eyes--the eyes of a man used to control\r\nthousands--he bowed to the earth, took the begging-bowl without a word,\r\nand returned to the village, saying, \xe2\x80\x9cWe have at last a holy man.\r\nNever have I seen such a man. He is of the Plains--but pale-coloured--a\r\nBrahmin of the Brahmins.\xe2\x80\x9d Then all the housewives of the village said,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThink you he will stay with us?\xe2\x80\x9d and each did her best to cook the\r\nmost savoury meal for the Bhagat. Hill-food is very simple, but with\r\nbuckwheat and Indian corn, and rice and red pepper, and little fish out\r\nof the stream in the valley, and honey from the flue-like hives built in\r\nthe stone walls, and dried apricots, and turmeric, and wild ginger, and\r\nbannocks of flour, a devout woman can make good things, and it was a\r\nfull bowl that the priest carried to the Bhagat. Was he going to stay?\r\nasked the priest. Would he need a chela--a disciple--to beg for him? Had\r\nhe a blanket against the cold weather? Was the food good?\r\n\r\nPurun Bhagat ate, and thanked the giver. It was in his mind to stay.\r\nThat was sufficient, said the priest. Let the begging-bowl be placed\r\noutside the shrine, in the hollow made by those two twisted roots, and\r\ndaily should the Bhagat be fed; for the village felt honoured that such\r\na man--he looked timidly into the Bhagat\xe2\x80\x99s face--should tarry among\r\nthem.\r\n\r\nThat day saw the end of Purun Bhagat\xe2\x80\x99s wanderings. He had come to the\r\nplace appointed for him--the silence and the space. After this, time\r\nstopped, and he, sitting at the mouth of the shrine, could not tell\r\nwhether he were alive or dead; a man with control of his limbs, or a\r\npart of the hills, and the clouds, and the shifting rain and sunlight.\r\nHe would repeat a Name softly to himself a hundred hundred times, till,\r\nat each repetition, he seemed to move more and more out of his body,\r\nsweeping up to the doors of some tremendous discovery; but, just as the\r\ndoor was opening, his body would drag him back, and, with grief, he felt\r\nhe was locked up again in the flesh and bones of Purun Bhagat.\r\n\r\nEvery morning the filled begging-bowl was laid silently in the crutch of\r\nthe roots outside the shrine. Sometimes the priest brought it; sometimes\r\na Ladakhi trader, lodging in the village, and anxious to get merit,\r\ntrudged up the path; but, more often, it was the woman who had cooked\r\nthe meal overnight; and she would murmur, hardly above her breath.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSpeak for me before the gods, Bhagat. Speak for such a one, the wife\r\nof so-and-so!\xe2\x80\x9d Now and then some bold child would be allowed the honour,\r\nand Purun Bhagat would hear him drop the bowl and run as fast as his\r\nlittle legs could carry him, but the Bhagat never came down to the\r\nvillage. It was laid out like a map at his feet. He could see the\r\nevening gatherings, held on the circle of the threshing-floors, because\r\nthat was the only level ground; could see the wonderful unnamed green\r\nof the young rice, the indigo blues of the Indian corn, the dock-like\r\npatches of buckwheat, and, in its season, the red bloom of the amaranth,\r\nwhose tiny seeds, being neither grain nor pulse, make a food that can be\r\nlawfully eaten by Hindus in time of fasts.\r\n\r\nWhen the year turned, the roofs of the huts were all little squares of\r\npurest gold, for it was on the roofs that they laid out their cobs of\r\nthe corn to dry. Hiving and harvest, rice-sowing and husking, passed\r\nbefore his eyes, all embroidered down there on the many-sided plots of\r\nfields, and he thought of them all, and wondered what they all led to at\r\nthe long last.\r\n\r\nEven in populated India a man cannot a day sit still before the wild\r\nthings run over him as though he were a rock; and in that wilderness\r\nvery soon the wild things, who knew Kali\xe2\x80\x99s Shrine well, came back to\r\nlook at the intruder. The langurs, the big gray-whiskered monkeys of\r\nthe Himalayas, were, naturally, the first, for they are alive with\r\ncuriosity; and when they had upset the begging-bowl, and rolled it round\r\nthe floor, and tried their teeth on the brass-handled crutch, and made\r\nfaces at the antelope skin, they decided that the human being who sat so\r\nstill was harmless. At evening, they would leap down from the pines, and\r\nbeg with their hands for things to eat, and then swing off in graceful\r\ncurves. They liked the warmth of the fire, too, and huddled round it\r\ntill Purun Bhagat had to push them aside to throw on more fuel; and\r\nin the morning, as often as not, he would find a furry ape sharing his\r\nblanket. All day long, one or other of the tribe would sit by his side,\r\nstaring out at the snows, crooning and looking unspeakably wise and\r\nsorrowful.\r\n\r\nAfter the monkeys came the barasingh, that big deer which is like our\r\nred deer, but stronger. He wished to rub off the velvet of his horns\r\nagainst the cold stones of Kali\xe2\x80\x99s statue, and stamped his feet when he\r\nsaw the man at the shrine. But Purun Bhagat never moved, and, little by\r\nlittle, the royal stag edged up and nuzzled his shoulder. Purun Bhagat\r\nslid one cool hand along the hot antlers, and the touch soothed the\r\nfretted beast, who bowed his head, and Purun Bhagat very softly rubbed\r\nand ravelled off the velvet. Afterward, the barasingh brought his doe\r\nand fawn--gentle things that mumbled on the holy man\xe2\x80\x99s blanket--or would\r\ncome alone at night, his eyes green in the fire-flicker, to take his\r\nshare of fresh walnuts. At last, the musk-deer, the shyest and almost\r\nthe smallest of the deerlets, came, too, her big rabbity ears erect;\r\neven brindled, silent mushick-nabha must needs find out what the\r\nlight in the shrine meant, and drop out her moose-like nose into Purun\r\nBhagat\xe2\x80\x99s lap, coming and going with the shadows of the fire. Purun\r\nBhagat called them all \xe2\x80\x9cmy brothers,\xe2\x80\x9d and his low call of \xe2\x80\x9cBhai! Bhai!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n would draw them from the forest at noon if they were within ear shot.\r\nThe Himalayan black bear, moody and suspicious--Sona, who has the\r\nV-shaped white mark under his chin--passed that way more than once; and\r\nsince the Bhagat showed no fear, Sona showed no anger, but watched him,\r\nand came closer, and begged a share of the caresses, and a dole of bread\r\nor wild berries. Often, in the still dawns, when the Bhagat would climb\r\nto the very crest of the pass to watch the red day walking along the\r\npeaks of the snows, he would find Sona shuffling and grunting at his\r\nheels, thrusting, a curious fore-paw under fallen trunks, and bringing\r\nit away with a WHOOF of impatience; or his early steps would wake Sona\r\nwhere he lay curled up, and the great brute, rising erect, would think\r\nto fight, till he heard the Bhagat\xe2\x80\x99s voice and knew his best friend.\r\n\r\nNearly all hermits and holy men who live apart from the big cities have\r\nthe reputation of being able to work miracles with the wild things, but\r\nall the miracle lies in keeping still, in never making a hasty movement,\r\nand, for a long time, at least, in never looking directly at a visitor.\r\nThe villagers saw the outline of the barasingh stalking like a shadow\r\nthrough the dark forest behind the shrine; saw the minaul, the Himalayan\r\npheasant, blazing in her best colours before Kali\xe2\x80\x99s statue; and the\r\nlangurs on their haunches, inside, playing with the walnut shells. Some\r\nof the children, too, had heard Sona singing to himself, bear-fashion,\r\nbehind the fallen rocks, and the Bhagat\xe2\x80\x99s reputation as miracle-worker\r\nstood firm.\r\n\r\nYet nothing was farther from his mind than miracles. He believed that\r\nall things were one big Miracle, and when a man knows that much he knows\r\nsomething to go upon. He knew for a certainty that there was nothing\r\ngreat and nothing little in this world: and day and night he strove to\r\nthink out his way into the heart of things, back to the place whence his\r\nsoul had come.\r\n\r\nSo thinking, his untrimmed hair fell down about his shoulders, the stone\r\nslab at the side of the antelope skin was dented into a little hole\r\nby the foot of his brass-handled crutch, and the place between the\r\ntree-trunks, where the begging-bowl rested day after day, sunk and wore\r\ninto a hollow almost as smooth as the brown shell itself; and each beast\r\nknew his exact place at the fire. The fields changed their colours with\r\nthe seasons; the threshing-floors filled and emptied, and filled again\r\nand again; and again and again, when winter came, the langurs frisked\r\namong the branches feathered with light snow, till the mother-monkeys\r\nbrought their sad-eyed little babies up from the warmer valleys with the\r\nspring. There were few changes in the village. The priest was older, and\r\nmany of the little children who used to come with the begging-dish sent\r\ntheir own children now; and when you asked of the villagers how long\r\ntheir holy man had lived in Kali\xe2\x80\x99s Shrine at the head of the pass, they\r\nanswered, \xe2\x80\x9cAlways.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThen came such summer rains as had not been known in the Hills for many\r\nseasons. Through three good months the valley was wrapped in cloud\r\nand soaking mist--steady, unrelenting downfall, breaking off into\r\nthunder-shower after thunder-shower. Kali\xe2\x80\x99s Shrine stood above the\r\nclouds, for the most part, and there was a whole month in which the\r\nBhagat never caught a glimpse of his village. It was packed away under\r\na white floor of cloud that swayed and shifted and rolled on itself and\r\nbulged upward, but never broke from its piers--the streaming flanks of\r\nthe valley.\r\n\r\nAll that time he heard nothing but the sound of a million little waters,\r\noverhead from the trees, and underfoot along the ground, soaking through\r\nthe pine-needles, dripping from the tongues of draggled fern, and\r\nspouting in newly-torn muddy channels down the slopes. Then the sun\r\ncame out, and drew forth the good incense of the deodars and the\r\nrhododendrons, and that far-off, clean smell which the Hill people call\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cthe smell of the snows.\xe2\x80\x9d The hot sunshine lasted for a week, and then\r\nthe rains gathered together for their last downpour, and the water fell\r\nin sheets that flayed off the skin of the ground and leaped back in\r\nmud. Purun Bhagat heaped his fire high that night, for he was sure his\r\nbrothers would need warmth; but never a beast came to the shrine, though\r\nhe called and called till he dropped asleep, wondering what had happened\r\nin the woods.\r\n\r\nIt was in the black heart of the night, the rain drumming like a\r\nthousand drums, that he was roused by a plucking at his blanket, and,\r\nstretching out, felt the little hand of a langur. \xe2\x80\x9cIt is better here\r\nthan in the trees,\xe2\x80\x9d he said sleepily, loosening a fold of blanket; \xe2\x80\x9ctake\r\nit and be warm.\xe2\x80\x9d The monkey caught his hand and pulled hard. \xe2\x80\x9cIs it\r\nfood, then?\xe2\x80\x9d said Purun Bhagat. \xe2\x80\x9cWait awhile, and I will prepare some.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n As he kneeled to throw fuel on the fire the langur ran to the door of\r\nthe shrine, crooned and ran back again, plucking at the man\xe2\x80\x99s knee.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat is it? What is thy trouble, Brother?\xe2\x80\x9d said Purun Bhagat, for the\r\nlangur\xe2\x80\x99s eyes were full of things that he could not tell. \xe2\x80\x9cUnless one of\r\nthy caste be in a trap--and none set traps here--I will not go into that\r\nweather. Look, Brother, even the barasingh comes for shelter!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe deer\xe2\x80\x99s antlers clashed as he strode into the shrine, clashed against\r\nthe grinning statue of Kali. He lowered them in Purun Bhagat\xe2\x80\x99s direction\r\nand stamped uneasily, hissing through his half-shut nostrils.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHai! Hai! Hai!\xe2\x80\x9d said the Bhagat, snapping his fingers, \xe2\x80\x9cIs THIS payment\r\nfor a night\xe2\x80\x99s lodging?\xe2\x80\x9d But the deer pushed him toward the door, and as\r\nhe did so Purun Bhagat heard the sound of something opening with a sigh,\r\nand saw two slabs of the floor draw away from each other, while the\r\nsticky earth below smacked its lips.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow I see,\xe2\x80\x9d said Purun Bhagat. \xe2\x80\x9cNo blame to my brothers that they did\r\nnot sit by the fire to-night. The mountain is falling. And yet--why\r\nshould I go?\xe2\x80\x9d His eye fell on the empty begging-bowl, and his face\r\nchanged. \xe2\x80\x9cThey have given me good food daily since--since I came, and,\r\nif I am not swift, to-morrow there will not be one mouth in the valley.\r\nIndeed, I must go and warn them below. Back there, Brother! Let me get\r\nto the fire.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe barasingh backed unwillingly as Purun Bhagat drove a pine torch deep\r\ninto the flame, twirling it till it was well lit. \xe2\x80\x9cAh! ye came to warn\r\nme,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, rising. \xe2\x80\x9cBetter than that we shall do; better than that.\r\nOut, now, and lend me thy neck, Brother, for I have but two feet.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe clutched the bristling withers of the barasingh with his right hand,\r\nheld the torch away with his left, and stepped out of the shrine into\r\nthe desperate night. There was no breath of wind, but the rain nearly\r\ndrowned the flare as the great deer hurried down the slope, sliding\r\non his haunches. As soon as they were clear of the forest more of the\r\nBhagat\xe2\x80\x99s brothers joined them. He heard, though he could not see, the\r\nlangurs pressing about him, and behind them the uhh! uhh! of Sona. The\r\nrain matted his long white hair into ropes; the water splashed beneath\r\nhis bare feet, and his yellow robe clung to his frail old body, but he\r\nstepped down steadily, leaning against the barasingh. He was no longer\r\na holy man, but Sir Purun Dass, K.C.I.E., Prime Minister of no small\r\nState, a man accustomed to command, going out to save life. Down\r\nthe steep, plashy path they poured all together, the Bhagat and his\r\nbrothers, down and down till the deer\xe2\x80\x99s feet clicked and stumbled on the\r\nwall of a threshing-floor, and he snorted because he smelt Man. Now they\r\nwere at the head of the one crooked village street, and the Bhagat beat\r\nwith his crutch on the barred windows of the blacksmith\xe2\x80\x99s house, as his\r\ntorch blazed up in the shelter of the eaves. \xe2\x80\x9cUp and out!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Purun\r\nBhagat; and he did not know his own voice, for it was years since he had\r\nspoken aloud to a man. \xe2\x80\x9cThe hill falls! The hill is falling! Up and out,\r\noh, you within!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is our Bhagat,\xe2\x80\x9d said the blacksmith\xe2\x80\x99s wife. \xe2\x80\x9cHe stands among his\r\nbeasts. Gather the little ones and give the call.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIt ran from house to house, while the beasts, cramped in the narrow way,\r\nsurged and huddled round the Bhagat, and Sona puffed impatiently.\r\n\r\nThe people hurried into the street--they were no more than seventy souls\r\nall told--and in the glare of the torches they saw their Bhagat holding\r\nback the terrified barasingh, while the monkeys plucked piteously at his\r\nskirts, and Sona sat on his haunches and roared.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAcross the valley and up the next hill!\xe2\x80\x9d shouted Purun Bhagat. \xe2\x80\x9cLeave\r\nnone behind! We follow!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThen the people ran as only Hill folk can run, for they knew that in a\r\nlandslip you must climb for the highest ground across the valley. They\r\nfled, splashing through the little river at the bottom, and panted up\r\nthe terraced fields on the far side, while the Bhagat and his brethren\r\nfollowed. Up and up the opposite mountain they climbed, calling to each\r\nother by name--the roll-call of the village--and at their heels toiled\r\nthe big barasingh, weighted by the failing strength of Purun Bhagat.\r\nAt last the deer stopped in the shadow of a deep pinewood, five hundred\r\nfeet up the hillside. His instinct, that had warned him of the coming\r\nslide, told him he would he safe here.\r\n\r\nPurun Bhagat dropped fainting by his side, for the chill of the rain and\r\nthat fierce climb were killing him; but first he called to the scattered\r\ntorches ahead, \xe2\x80\x9cStay and count your numbers\xe2\x80\x9d; then, whispering to the\r\ndeer as he saw the lights gather in a cluster: \xe2\x80\x9cStay with me, Brother.\r\nStay--till--I--go!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThere was a sigh in the air that grew to a mutter, and a mutter that\r\ngrew to a roar, and a roar that passed all sense of hearing, and the\r\nhillside on which the villagers stood was hit in the darkness, and\r\nrocked to the blow. Then a note as steady, deep, and true as the deep C\r\nof the organ drowned everything for perhaps five minutes, while the very\r\nroots of the pines quivered to it. It died away, and the sound of the\r\nrain falling on miles of hard ground and grass changed to the muffled\r\ndrum of water on soft earth. That told its own tale.\r\n\r\nNever a villager--not even the priest--was bold enough to speak to the\r\nBhagat who had saved their lives. They crouched under the pines and\r\nwaited till the day. When it came they looked across the valley and\r\nsaw that what had been forest, and terraced field, and track-threaded\r\ngrazing-ground was one raw, red, fan-shaped smear, with a few trees\r\nflung head-down on the scarp. That red ran high up the hill of their\r\nrefuge, damming back the little river, which had begun to spread into a\r\nbrick-coloured lake. Of the village, of the road to the shrine, of the\r\nshrine itself, and the forest behind, there was no trace. For one mile\r\nin width and two thousand feet in sheer depth the mountain-side had come\r\naway bodily, planed clean from head to heel.\r\n\r\nAnd the villagers, one by one, crept through the wood to pray before\r\ntheir Bhagat. They saw the barasingh standing over him, who fled when\r\nthey came near, and they heard the langurs wailing in the branches,\r\nand Sona moaning up the hill; but their Bhagat was dead, sitting\r\ncross-legged, his back against a tree, his crutch under his armpit, and\r\nhis face turned to the north-east.\r\n\r\nThe priest said: \xe2\x80\x9cBehold a miracle after a miracle, for in this very\r\nattitude must all Sunnyasis be buried! Therefore where he now is we will\r\nbuild the temple to our holy man.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThey built the temple before a year was ended--a little stone-and-earth\r\nshrine--and they called the hill the Bhagat\xe2\x80\x99s hill, and they worship\r\nthere with lights and flowers and offerings to this day. But they do\r\nnot know that the saint of their worship is the late Sir Purun Dass,\r\nK.C.I.E., D.C.L., Ph.D., etc., once Prime Minister of the progressive\r\nand enlightened State of Mohiniwala, and honorary or corresponding\r\nmember of more learned and scientific societies than will ever do any\r\ngood in this world or the next.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nA SONG OF KABIR\r\n\r\n     Oh, light was the world that he weighed in his hands!\r\n     Oh, heavy the tale of his fiefs and his lands!\r\n     He has gone from the guddee and put on the shroud,\r\n     And departed in guise of bairagi avowed!\r\n\r\n     Now the white road to Delhi is mat for his feet,\r\n     The sal and the kikar must guard him from heat;\r\n     His home is the camp, and the waste, and the crowd--\r\n     He is seeking the Way as bairagi avowed!\r\n\r\n     He has looked upon Man, and his eyeballs are clear\r\n     (There was One; there is One, and but One, saith Kabir);\r\n     The Red Mist of Doing has thinned to a cloud--\r\n     He has taken the Path for bairagi avowed!\r\n\r\n     To learn and discern of his brother the clod,\r\n     Of his brother the brute, and his brother the God.\r\n     He has gone from the council and put on the shroud\r\n     (\xe2\x80\x9cCan ye hear?\xe2\x80\x9d saith Kabir), a bairagi avowed!\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nLETTING IN THE JUNGLE\r\n\r\n      Veil them, cover them, wall them round--\r\n        Blossom, and creeper, and weed--\r\n      Let us forget the sight and the sound,\r\n        The smell and the touch of the breed!\r\n\r\n      Fat black ash by the altar-stone,\r\n        Here is the white-foot rain,\r\n      And the does bring forth in the fields unsown,\r\n        And none shall affright them again;\r\n      And the blind walls crumble, unknown, o\xe2\x80\x99erthrown\r\n        And none shall inhabit again!\r\n\r\n\r\nYou will remember that after Mowgli had pinned Shere Khan\xe2\x80\x99s hide to\r\nthe Council Rock, he told as many as were left of the Seeonee Pack that\r\nhenceforward he would hunt in the Jungle alone; and the four children of\r\nMother and Father Wolf said that they would hunt with him. But it is not\r\neasy to change one\xe2\x80\x99s life all in a minute--particularly in the Jungle.\r\nThe first thing Mowgli did, when the disorderly Pack had slunk off, was\r\nto go to the home-cave, and sleep for a day and a night. Then he told\r\nMother Wolf and Father Wolf as much as they could understand of his\r\nadventures among men; and when he made the morning sun flicker up and\r\ndown the blade of his skinning-knife,--the same he had skinned Shere\r\nKhan with,--they said he had learned something. Then Akela and Gray\r\nBrother had to explain their share of the great buffalo-drive in the\r\nravine, and Baloo toiled up the hill to hear all about it, and Bagheera\r\nscratched himself all over with pure delight at the way in which Mowgli\r\nhad managed his war.\r\n\r\nIt was long after sunrise, but no one dreamed of going to sleep, and\r\nfrom time to time, during the talk, Mother Wolf would throw up her head,\r\nand sniff a deep snuff of satisfaction as the wind brought her the smell\r\nof the tiger-skin on the Council Rock.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut for Akela and Gray Brother here,\xe2\x80\x9d Mowgli said, at the end, \xe2\x80\x9cI could\r\nhave done nothing. Oh, mother, mother! if thou hadst seen the black\r\nherd-bulls pour down the ravine, or hurry through the gates when the\r\nMan-Pack flung stones at me!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am glad I did not see that last,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mother Wolf stiffly. \xe2\x80\x9cIt is\r\nnot MY custom to suffer my cubs to be driven to and fro like jackals.\r\n_I_ would have taken a price from the Man-Pack; but I would have spared\r\nthe woman who gave thee the milk. Yes, I would have spared her alone.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPeace, peace, Raksha!\xe2\x80\x9d said Father Wolf, lazily. \xe2\x80\x9cOur Frog has come\r\nback again--so wise that his own father must lick his feet; and what is\r\na cut, more or less, on the head? Leave Men alone.\xe2\x80\x9d Baloo and Bagheera\r\nboth echoed: \xe2\x80\x9cLeave Men alone.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMowgli, his head on Mother Wolf\xe2\x80\x99s side, smiled contentedly, and said\r\nthat, for his own part, he never wished to see, or hear, or smell Man\r\nagain.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut what,\xe2\x80\x9d said Akela, cocking one ear--\xe2\x80\x9cbut what if men do not leave\r\nthee alone, Little Brother?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe be FIVE,\xe2\x80\x9d said Gray Brother, looking round at the company, and\r\nsnapping his jaws on the last word.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe also might attend to that hunting,\xe2\x80\x9d said Bagheera, with a little\r\nswitch-switch of his tail, looking at Baloo. \xe2\x80\x9cBut why think of men now,\r\nAkela?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFor this reason,\xe2\x80\x9d the Lone Wolf answered: \xe2\x80\x9cwhen that yellow chief\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nhide was hung up on the rock, I went back along our trail to the\r\nvillage, stepping in my tracks, turning aside, and lying down, to make\r\na mixed trail in case one should follow us. But when I had fouled the\r\ntrail so that I myself hardly knew it again, Mang, the Bat, came hawking\r\nbetween the trees, and hung up above me.\xe2\x80\x9d Said Mang, \xe2\x80\x9cThe village of the\r\nMan-Pack, where they cast out the Man-cub, hums like a hornet\xe2\x80\x99s nest.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt was a big stone that I threw,\xe2\x80\x9d chuckled Mowgli, who had often amused\r\nhimself by throwing ripe paw-paws into a hornet\xe2\x80\x99s nest, and racing off\r\nto the nearest pool before the hornets caught him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI asked of Mang what he had seen.
