He said that the Red Flower blossomed\r\nat the gate of the village, and men sat about it carrying guns. Now _I_\r\nknow, for I have good cause,\xe2\x80\x9d--Akela looked down at the old dry scars\r\non his flank and side,--\xe2\x80\x9cthat men do not carry guns for pleasure.\r\nPresently, Little Brother, a man with a gun follows our trail--if,\r\nindeed, he be not already on it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut why should he? Men have cast me out. What more do they need?\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nMowgli angrily.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThou art a man, Little Brother,\xe2\x80\x9d Akela returned. \xe2\x80\x9cIt is not for US, the\r\nFree Hunters, to tell thee what thy brethren do, or why.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe had just time to snatch up his paw as the skinning-knife cut deep\r\ninto the ground below. Mowgli struck quicker than an average human\r\neye could follow but Akela was a wolf; and even a dog, who is very far\r\nremoved from the wild wolf, his ancestor, can be waked out of deep sleep\r\nby a cart-wheel touching his flank, and can spring away unharmed before\r\nthat wheel comes on.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnother time,\xe2\x80\x9d Mowgli said quietly, returning the knife to its sheath,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cspeak of the Man-Pack and of Mowgli in TWO breaths--not one.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPhff! That is a sharp tooth,\xe2\x80\x9d said Akela, snuffing at the blade\xe2\x80\x99s\r\ncut in the earth, \xe2\x80\x9cbut living with the Man-Pack has spoiled thine eye,\r\nLittle Brother. I could have killed a buck while thou wast striking.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBagheera sprang to his feet, thrust up his head as far as he could,\r\nsniffed, and stiffened through every curve in his body. Gray Brother\r\nfollowed his example quickly, keeping a little to his left to get the\r\nwind that was blowing from the right, while Akela bounded fifty yards up\r\nwind, and, half-crouching, stiffened too. Mowgli looked on enviously.\r\nHe could smell things as very few human beings could, but he had never\r\nreached the hair-trigger-like sensitiveness of a Jungle nose; and his\r\nthree months in the smoky village had set him back sadly. However, he\r\ndampened his finger, rubbed it on his nose, and stood erect to catch the\r\nupper scent, which, though it is the faintest, is the truest.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMan!\xe2\x80\x9d Akela growled, dropping on his haunches.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBuldeo!\xe2\x80\x9d said Mowgli, sitting down. \xe2\x80\x9cHe follows our trail, and yonder\r\nis the sunlight on his gun. Look!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIt was no more than a splash of sunlight, for a fraction of a second,\r\non the brass clamps of the old Tower musket, but nothing in the Jungle\r\nwinks with just that flash, except when the clouds race over the sky.\r\nThen a piece of mica, or a little pool, or even a highly-polished leaf\r\nwill flash like a heliograph. But that day was cloudless and still.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI knew men would follow,\xe2\x80\x9d said Akela triumphantly. \xe2\x80\x9cNot for nothing\r\nhave I led the Pack.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe four cubs said nothing, but ran down hill on their bellies, melting\r\ninto the thorn and under-brush as a mole melts into a lawn.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhere go ye, and without word?\xe2\x80\x9d Mowgli called.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cH\xe2\x80\x99sh! We roll his skull here before mid-day!\xe2\x80\x9d Gray Brother answered.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBack! Back and wait! Man does not eat Man!\xe2\x80\x9d Mowgli shrieked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWho was a wolf but now? Who drove the knife at me for thinking he might\r\nbe Man?\xe2\x80\x9d said Akela, as the four wolves turned back sullenly and dropped\r\nto heel.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAm I to give reason for all I choose to, do?\xe2\x80\x9d said Mowgli furiously.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is Man! There speaks Man!\xe2\x80\x9d Bagheera muttered under his whiskers.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cEven so did men talk round the King\xe2\x80\x99s cages at Oodeypore. We of the\r\nJungle know that Man is wisest of all. If we trusted our ears we should\r\nknow that of all things he is most foolish.\xe2\x80\x9d Raising his voice, he\r\nadded, \xe2\x80\x9cThe Man-cub is right in this. Men hunt in packs. To kill one,\r\nunless we know what the others will do, is bad hunting. Come, let us see\r\nwhat this Man means toward us.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe will not come,\xe2\x80\x9d Gray Brother growled. \xe2\x80\x9cHunt alone, Little Brother.\r\nWE know our own minds. The skull would have been ready to bring by now.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMowgli had been looking from one to the other of his friends, his chest\r\nheaving and his eyes full of tears. He strode forward to the wolves,\r\nand, dropping on one knee, said: \xe2\x80\x9cDo I not know my mind? Look at me!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThey looked uneasily, and when their eyes wandered, he called them back\r\nagain and again, till their hair stood up all over their bodies, and\r\nthey trembled in every limb, while Mowgli stared and stared.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow,\xe2\x80\x9d said he, \xe2\x80\x9cof us five, which is leader?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThou art leader, Little Brother,\xe2\x80\x9d said Gray Brother, and he licked\r\nMowgli\xe2\x80\x99s foot.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFollow, then,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mowgli, and the four followed at his heels with\r\ntheir tails between their legs.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThis comes of living with the Man-Pack,\xe2\x80\x9d said Bagheera, slipping down\r\nafter them. \xe2\x80\x9cThere is more in the Jungle now than Jungle Law, Baloo.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe old bear said nothing, but he thought many things.\r\n\r\nMowgli cut across noiselessly through the Jungle, at right angles to\r\nBuldeo\xe2\x80\x99s path, till, parting the undergrowth, he saw the old man, his\r\nmusket on his shoulder, running up the trail of overnight at a dog-trot.\r\n\r\nYou will remember that Mowgli had left the village with the heavy weight\r\nof Shere Khan\xe2\x80\x99s raw hide on his shoulders, while Akela and Gray Brother\r\ntrotted behind, so that the triple trail was very clearly marked.\r\nPresently Buldeo came to where Akela, as you know, had gone back and\r\nmixed it all up. Then he sat down, and coughed and grunted, and made\r\nlittle casts round and about into the Jungle to pick it up again, and,\r\nall the time he could have thrown a stone over those who were watching\r\nhim. No one can be so silent as a wolf when he does not care to be\r\nheard; and Mowgli, though the wolves thought he moved very clumsily,\r\ncould come and go like a shadow. They ringed the old man as a school\r\nof porpoises ring a steamer at full speed, and as they ringed him they\r\ntalked unconcernedly, for their speech began below the lowest end of the\r\nscale that untrained human beings can hear. [The other end is bounded by\r\nthe high squeak of Mang, the Bat, which very many people cannot catch at\r\nall. From that note all the bird and bat and insect talk takes on.]\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThis is better than any kill,\xe2\x80\x9d said Gray Brother, as Buldeo stooped\r\nand peered and puffed. \xe2\x80\x9cHe looks like a lost pig in the Jungles by the\r\nriver. What does he say?\xe2\x80\x9d Buldeo was muttering savagely.\r\n\r\nMowgli translated. \xe2\x80\x9cHe says that packs of wolves must have danced round\r\nme. He says that he never saw such a trail in his life. He says he is\r\ntired.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe will be rested before he picks it up again,\xe2\x80\x9d said Bagheera coolly,\r\nas he slipped round a tree-trunk, in the game of blindman\xe2\x80\x99s-buff that\r\nthey were playing. \xe2\x80\x9cNOW, what does the lean thing do?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cEat or blow smoke out of his mouth. Men always play with their mouths,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n said Mowgli; and the silent trailers saw the old man fill and light\r\nand puff at a water-pipe, and they took good note of the smell of the\r\ntobacco, so as to be sure of Buldeo in the darkest night, if necessary.\r\n\r\nThen a little knot of charcoal-burners came down the path, and naturally\r\nhalted to speak to Buldeo, whose fame as a hunter reached for at least\r\ntwenty miles round. They all sat down and smoked, and Bagheera and\r\nthe others came up and watched while Buldeo began to tell the story of\r\nMowgli, the Devil-child, from one end to another, with additions and\r\ninventions. How he himself had really killed Shere Khan; and how Mowgli\r\nhad turned himself into a wolf, and fought with him all the afternoon,\r\nand changed into a boy again and bewitched Buldeo\xe2\x80\x99s rifle, so that the\r\nbullet turned the corner, when he pointed it at Mowgli, and killed one\r\nof Buldeo\xe2\x80\x99s own buffaloes; and how the village, knowing him to be the\r\nbravest hunter in Seeonee, had sent him out to kill this Devil-child.\r\nBut meantime the village had got hold of Messua and her husband, who\r\nwere undoubtedly the father and mother of this Devil-child, and had\r\nbarricaded them in their own hut, and presently would torture them to\r\nmake them confess they were witch and wizard, and then they would be\r\nburned to death.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhen?\xe2\x80\x9d said the charcoal-burners, because they would very much like to\r\nbe present at the ceremony.\r\n\r\nBuldeo said that nothing would be done till he returned, because the\r\nvillage wished him to kill the Jungle Boy first. After that they would\r\ndispose of Messua and her husband, and divide their lands and buffaloes\r\namong the village. Messua\xe2\x80\x99s husband had some remarkably fine buffaloes,\r\ntoo. It was an excellent thing to destroy wizards, Buldeo thought; and\r\npeople who entertained Wolf-children out of the Jungle were clearly the\r\nworst kind of witches.\r\n\r\nBut, said the charcoal-burners, what would happen if the English heard\r\nof it? The English, they had heard, were a perfectly mad people, who\r\nwould not let honest farmers kill witches in peace.\r\n\r\nWhy, said Buldeo, the head-man of the village would report that Messua\r\nand her husband had died of snake-bite. THAT was all arranged, and the\r\nonly thing now was to kill the Wolf-child. They did not happen to have\r\nseen anything of such a creature?\r\n\r\nThe charcoal-burners looked round cautiously, and thanked their stars\r\nthey had not; but they had no doubt that so brave a man as Buldeo would\r\nfind him if any one could. The sun was getting rather low, and they had\r\nan idea that they would push on to Buldeo\xe2\x80\x99s village and see that wicked\r\nwitch. Buldeo said that, though it was his duty to kill the Devil-child,\r\nhe could not think of letting a party of unarmed men go through the\r\nJungle, which might produce the Wolf-demon at any minute, without his\r\nescort. He, therefore, would accompany them, and if the sorcerer\xe2\x80\x99s child\r\nappeared--well, he would show them how the best hunter in Seeonee dealt\r\nwith such things. The Brahmin, he said, had given him a charm against\r\nthe creature that made everything perfectly safe.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat says he? What says he? What says he?\xe2\x80\x9d the wolves repeated every\r\nfew minutes; and Mowgli translated until he came to the witch part of\r\nthe story, which was a little beyond him, and then he said that the man\r\nand woman who had been so kind to him were trapped.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDoes Man trap Man?\xe2\x80\x9d said Bagheera.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo he says. I cannot understand the talk. They are all mad together.\r\nWhat have Messua and her man to do with me that they should be put in\r\na trap; and what is all this talk about the Red Flower? I must look\r\nto this. Whatever they would do to Messua they will not do till Buldeo\r\nreturns. And so----\xe2\x80\x9d Mowgli thought hard, with his fingers playing round\r\nthe haft of the skinning-knife, while Buldeo and the charcoal-burners\r\nwent off very valiantly in single file.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI go hot-foot back to the Man-Pack,\xe2\x80\x9d Mowgli said at last.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd those?\xe2\x80\x9d said Gray Brother, looking hungrily after the brown backs\r\nof the charcoal-burners.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSing them home,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mowgli, with a grin; \xe2\x80\x9cI do not wish them to be at\r\nthe village gates till it is dark. Can ye hold them?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nGray Brother bared his white teeth in contempt. \xe2\x80\x9cWe can head them round\r\nand round in circles like tethered goats--if I know Man.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat I do not need. Sing to them a little, lest they be lonely on the\r\nroad, and, Gray Brother, the song need not be of the sweetest. Go with\r\nthem, Bagheera, and help make that song. When night is shut down, meet\r\nme by the village--Gray Brother knows the place.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is no light hunting to work for a Man-cub. When shall I sleep?\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nBagheera, yawning, though his eyes showed that he was delighted with the\r\namusement. \xe2\x80\x9cMe to sing to naked men! But let us try.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe lowered his head so that the sound would travel, and cried a long,\r\nlong, \xe2\x80\x9cGood hunting\xe2\x80\x9d--a midnight call in the afternoon, which was quite\r\nawful enough to begin with. Mowgli heard it rumble, and rise, and fall,\r\nand die off in a creepy sort of whine behind him, and laughed to himself\r\nas he ran through the Jungle. He could see the charcoal-burners huddled\r\nin a knot; old Buldeo\xe2\x80\x99s gun-barrel waving, like a banana-leaf, to every\r\npoint of the compass at once. Then Gray Brother gave the Ya-la-hi!\r\nYalaha! call for the buck-driving, when the Pack drives the nilghai, the\r\nbig blue cow, before them, and it seemed to come from the very ends of\r\nthe earth, nearer, and nearer, and nearer, till it ended in a shriek\r\nsnapped off short. The other three answered, till even Mowgli could have\r\nvowed that the full Pack was in full cry, and then they all broke\r\ninto the magnificent Morning-song in the Jungle, with every turn, and\r\nflourish, and grace-note that a deep-mouthed wolf of the Pack knows.\r\nThis is a rough rendering of the song, but you must imagine what it\r\nsounds like when it breaks the afternoon hush of the Jungle:--\r\n\r\n     One moment past our bodies cast\r\n       No shadow on the plain;\r\n     Now clear and black they stride our track,\r\n       And we run home again.\r\n     In morning hush, each rock and bush\r\n       Stands hard, and high, and raw:\r\n     Then give the Call: \xe2\x80\x9cGood rest to all\r\n       That keep The Jungle Law!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n     Now horn and pelt our peoples melt\r\n       In covert to abide;\r\n     Now, crouched and still, to cave and hill\r\n       Our Jungle Barons glide.\r\n     Now, stark and plain, Man\xe2\x80\x99s oxen strain,\r\n       That draw the new-yoked plough;\r\n     Now, stripped and dread, the dawn is red\r\n       Above the lit talao.\r\n\r\n     Ho! Get to lair! The sun\xe2\x80\x99s aflare\r\n       Behind the breathing grass:\r\n     And cracking through the young bamboo\r\n       The warning whispers pass.\r\n     By day made strange, the woods we range\r\n       With blinking eyes we scan;\r\n     While down the skies the wild duck cries\r\n       \xe2\x80\x9cThe Day--the Day to Man!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n     The dew is dried that drenched our hide\r\n       Or washed about our way;\r\n     And where we drank, the puddled bank\r\n       Is crisping into clay.\r\n     The traitor Dark gives up each mark\r\n       Of stretched or hooded claw;\r\n     Then hear the Call: \xe2\x80\x9cGood rest to all\r\n       That keep the Jungle Law!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBut no translation can give the effect of it, or the yelping scorn the\r\nFour threw into every word of it, as they heard the trees crash when\r\nthe men hastily climbed up into the branches, and Buldeo began repeating\r\nincantations and charms. Then they lay down and slept, for, like all who\r\nlive by their own exertions, they were of a methodical cast of mind; and\r\nno one can work well without sleep.\r\n\r\nMeantime, Mowgli was putting the miles behind him, nine to the hour,\r\nswinging on, delighted to find himself so fit after all his cramped\r\nmonths among men. The one idea in his head was to get Messua and her\r\nhusband out of the trap, whatever it was; for he had a natural mistrust\r\nof traps. Later on, he promised himself, he would pay his debts to the\r\nvillage at large.\r\n\r\nIt was at twilight when he saw the well-remembered grazing-grounds, and\r\nthe dhak-tree where Gray Brother had waited for him on the morning that\r\nhe killed Shere Khan. Angry as he was at the whole breed and community\r\nof Man, something jumped up in his throat and made him catch his breath\r\nwhen he looked at the village roofs. He noticed that every one had come\r\nin from the fields unusually early, and that, instead of getting to\r\ntheir evening cooking, they gathered in a crowd under the village tree,\r\nand chattered, and shouted.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMen must always be making traps for men, or they are not content,\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nMowgli. \xe2\x80\x9cLast night it was Mowgli--but that night seems many Rains ago.\r\nTo-night it is Messua and her man. To-morrow, and for very many nights\r\nafter, it will be Mowgli\xe2\x80\x99s turn again.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe crept along outside the wall till he came to Messua\xe2\x80\x99s hut, and looked\r\nthrough the window into the room. There lay Messua, gagged, and bound\r\nhand and foot, breathing hard, and groaning: her husband was tied to the\r\ngaily-painted bedstead. The door of the hut that opened into the street\r\nwas shut fast, and three or four people were sitting with their backs to\r\nit.\r\n\r\nMowgli knew the manners and customs of the villagers very fairly. He\r\nargued that so long as they could eat, and talk, and smoke, they would\r\nnot do anything else; but as soon as they had fed they would begin to be\r\ndangerous. Buldeo would be coming in before long, and if his escort had\r\ndone its duty, Buldeo would have a very interesting tale to tell. So he\r\nwent in through the window, and, stooping over the man and the woman,\r\ncut their thongs, pulling out the gags, and looked round the hut for\r\nsome milk.\r\n\r\nMessua was half wild with pain and fear (she had been beaten and stoned\r\nall the morning), and Mowgli put his hand over her mouth just in time\r\nto stop a scream. Her husband was only bewildered and angry, and sat\r\npicking dust and things out of his torn beard.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI knew--I knew he would come,\xe2\x80\x9d Messua sobbed at last. \xe2\x80\x9cNow do I KNOW\r\nthat he is my son!\xe2\x80\x9d and she hugged Mowgli to her heart. Up to that time\r\nMowgli had been perfectly steady, but now he began to tremble all over,\r\nand that surprised him immensely.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy are these thongs? Why have they tied thee?\xe2\x80\x9d he asked, after a\r\npause.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTo be put to the death for making a son of thee--what else?\xe2\x80\x9d said the\r\nman sullenly. \xe2\x80\x9cLook! I bleed.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMessua said nothing, but it was at her wounds that Mowgli looked, and\r\nthey heard him grit his teeth when he saw the blood.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhose work is this?\xe2\x80\x9d said he. \xe2\x80\x9cThere is a price to pay.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe work of all the village. I was too rich. I had too many cattle.\r\nTHEREFORE she and I are witches, because we gave thee shelter.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI do not understand. Let Messua tell the tale.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI gave thee milk, Nathoo; dost thou remember?\xe2\x80\x9d Messua said timidly.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBecause thou wast my son, whom the tiger took, and because I loved thee\r\nvery dearly. They said that I was thy mother, the mother of a devil, and\r\ntherefore worthy of death.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd what is a devil?\xe2\x80\x9d said Mowgli. \xe2\x80\x9cDeath I have seen.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe man looked up gloomily, but Messua laughed. \xe2\x80\x9cSee!\xe2\x80\x9d she said to her\r\nhusband, \xe2\x80\x9cI knew--I said that he was no sorcerer. He is my son--my son!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSon or sorcerer, what good will that do us?\xe2\x80\x9d the man answered. \xe2\x80\x9cWe be\r\nas dead already.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYonder is the road to the Jungle\xe2\x80\x9d--Mowgli pointed through the window.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYour hands and feet are free. Go now.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe do not know the Jungle, my son, as--as thou knowest,\xe2\x80\x9d Messua began.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI do not think that I could walk far.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd the men and women would be upon our backs and drag us here again,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n said the husband.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cH\xe2\x80\x99m!\xe2\x80\x9d said Mowgli, and he tickled the palm of his hand with the tip\r\nof his skinning-knife; \xe2\x80\x9cI have no wish to do harm to any one of this\r\nvillage--YET. But I do not think they will stay thee. In a little while\r\nthey will have much else to think upon. Ah!\xe2\x80\x9d he lifted his head and\r\nlistened to shouting and trampling outside. \xe2\x80\x9cSo they have let Buldeo\r\ncome home at last?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe was sent out this morning to kill thee,\xe2\x80\x9d Messua cried. \xe2\x80\x9cDidst thou\r\nmeet him?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes--we--I met him. He has a tale to tell and while he is telling it\r\nthere is time to do much. But first I will learn what they mean. Think\r\nwhere ye would go, and tell me when I come back.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe bounded through the window and ran along again outside the wall\r\nof the village till he came within ear-shot of the crowd round the\r\npeepul-tree. Buldeo was lying on the ground, coughing and groaning,\r\nand every one was asking him questions. His hair had fallen about his\r\nshoulders; his hands and legs were skinned from climbing up trees,\r\nand he could hardly speak, but he felt the importance of his position\r\nkeenly. From time to time he said something about devils and singing\r\ndevils, and magic enchantment, just to give the crowd a taste of what\r\nwas coming. Then he called for water.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBah!\xe2\x80\x9d said Mowgli. \xe2\x80\x9cChatter--chatter! Talk, talk! Men are\r\nblood-brothers of the Bandar-log. Now he must wash his mouth with water;\r\nnow he must blow smoke; and when all that is done he has still his story\r\nto tell. They are very wise people--men. They will leave no one to guard\r\nMessua till their ears are stuffed with Buldeo\xe2\x80\x99s tales. And--I grow as\r\nlazy as they!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe shook himself and glided back to the hut. Just as he was at the\r\nwindow he felt a touch on his foot.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMother,\xe2\x80\x9d said he, for he knew that tongue well, \xe2\x80\x9cwhat dost THOU here?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI heard my children singing through the woods, and I followed the one I\r\nloved best. Little Frog, I have a desire to see that woman who gave thee\r\nmilk,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mother Wolf, all wet with the dew.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey have bound and mean to kill her. I have cut those ties, and she\r\ngoes with her man through the Jungle.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI also will follow. I am old, but not yet toothless.\xe2\x80\x9d Mother Wolf\r\nreared herself up on end, and looked through the window into the dark of\r\nthe hut.\r\n\r\nIn a minute she dropped noiselessly, and all she said was: \xe2\x80\x9cI gave thee\r\nthy first milk; but Bagheera speaks truth: Man goes to Man at the last.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMaybe,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mowgli, with a very unpleasant look on his face; \xe2\x80\x9cbut\r\nto-night I am very far from that trail. Wait here, but do not let her\r\nsee.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTHOU wast never afraid of ME, Little Frog,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mother Wolf, backing\r\ninto the high grass, and blotting herself out, as she knew how.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd now,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mowgli cheerfully, as he swung into the hut again, \xe2\x80\x9cthey\r\nare all sitting round Buldeo, who is saying that which did not happen.\r\nWhen his talk is finished, they say they will assuredly come here with\r\nthe Red--with fire and burn you both. And then?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have spoken to my man,\xe2\x80\x9d said Messua. \xe2\x80\x9cKhanhiwara is thirty miles from\r\nhere, but at Khanhiwara we may find the English--\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd what Pack are they?\xe2\x80\x9d said Mowgli.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI do not know. They be white, and it is said that they govern all\r\nthe land, and do not suffer people to burn or beat each other without\r\nwitnesses. If we can get thither to-night, we live. Otherwise we die.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLive, then. No man passes the gates to-night. But what does HE do?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n Messua\xe2\x80\x99s husband was on his hands and knees digging up the earth in one\r\ncorner of the hut.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is his little money,\xe2\x80\x9d said Messua. \xe2\x80\x9cWe can take nothing else.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, yes. The stuff that passes from hand to hand and never grows\r\nwarmer. Do they need it outside this place also?\xe2\x80\x9d said Mowgli.\r\n\r\nThe man stared angrily. \xe2\x80\x9cHe is a fool, and no devil,\xe2\x80\x9d he muttered. \xe2\x80\x9cWith\r\nthe money I can buy a horse. We are too bruised to walk far, and the\r\nvillage will follow us in an hour.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI say they will NOT follow till I choose; but a horse is well thought\r\nof, for Messua is tired.\xe2\x80\x9d Her husband stood up and knotted the last\r\nof the rupees into his waist-cloth. Mowgli helped Messua through the\r\nwindow, and the cool night air revived her, but the Jungle in the\r\nstarlight looked very dark and terrible.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYe know the trail to Khanhiwara?\xe2\x80\x9d Mowgli whispered.\r\n\r\nThey nodded.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGood. Remember, now, not to be afraid. And there is no need to go\r\nquickly. Only--only there may be some small singing in the Jungle behind\r\nyou and before.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThink you we would have risked a night in the Jungle through anything\r\nless than the fear of burning? It is better to be killed by beasts than\r\nby men,\xe2\x80\x9d said Messua\xe2\x80\x99s husband; but Messua looked at Mowgli and smiled.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI say,\xe2\x80\x9d Mowgli went on, just as though he were Baloo repeating an old\r\nJungle Law for the hundredth time to a foolish cub--\xe2\x80\x9cI say that not a\r\ntooth in the Jungle is bared against you; not a foot in the Jungle is\r\nlifted against you. Neither man nor beast shall stay you till you come\r\nwithin eye-shot of Khanhiwara. There will be a watch about you.\xe2\x80\x9d He\r\nturned quickly to Messua, saying, \xe2\x80\x9cHE does not believe, but thou wilt\r\nbelieve?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAy, surely, my son. Man, ghost, or wolf of the Jungle, I believe.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHE will be afraid when he hears my people singing. Thou wilt know and\r\nunderstand. Go now, and slowly, for there is no need of any haste. The\r\ngates are shut.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMessua flung herself sobbing at Mowgli\xe2\x80\x99s feet, but he lifted her very\r\nquickly with a shiver. Then she hung about his neck and called him every\r\nname of blessing she could think of, but her husband looked enviously\r\nacross his fields, and said: \xe2\x80\x9cIF we reach Khanhiwara, and I get the ear\r\nof the English, I will bring such a lawsuit against the Brahmin and old\r\nBuldeo and the others as shall eat the village to the bone. They shall\r\npay me twice over for my crops untilled and my buffaloes unfed. I will\r\nhave a great justice.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMowgli laughed. \xe2\x80\x9cI do not know what justice is, but--come next Rains.\r\nand see what is left.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThey went off toward the Jungle, and Mother Wolf leaped from her place\r\nof hiding.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFollow!\xe2\x80\x9d said Mowgli; \xe2\x80\x9cand look to it that all the Jungle knows these\r\ntwo are safe. Give tongue a little. I would call Bagheera.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe long, low howl rose and fell, and Mowgli saw Messua\xe2\x80\x99s husband flinch\r\nand turn, half minded to run back to the hut.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGo on,\xe2\x80\x9d Mowgli called cheerfully. \xe2\x80\x9cI said there might be singing. That\r\ncall will follow up to Khanhiwara. It is Favour of the Jungle.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMessua urged her husband forward, and the darkness shut down on them and\r\nMother Wolf as Bagheera rose up almost under Mowgli\xe2\x80\x99s feet, trembling\r\nwith delight of the night that drives the Jungle People wild.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am ashamed of thy brethren,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, purring. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat? Did they not\r\nsing sweetly to Buldeo?\xe2\x80\x9d said Mowgli.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cToo well! Too well! They made even ME forget my pride, and, by the\r\nBroken Lock that freed me, I went singing through the Jungle as though I\r\nwere out wooing in the spring! Didst thou not hear us?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI had other game afoot. Ask Buldeo if he liked the song. But where\r\nare the Four? I do not wish one of the Man-Pack to leave the gates\r\nto-night.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat need of the Four, then?\xe2\x80\x9d said Bagheera, shifting from foot to\r\nfoot, his eyes ablaze, and purring louder than ever. \xe2\x80\x9cI can hold them,\r\nLittle Brother. Is it killing at last? The singing and the sight of the\r\nmen climbing up the trees have made me very ready. Who is Man that we\r\nshould care for him--the naked brown digger, the hairless and toothless,\r\nthe eater of earth? I have followed him all day--at noon--in the white\r\nsunlight. I herded him as the wolves herd buck. I am Bagheera! Bagheera!\r\nBagheera! As I dance with my shadow, so danced I with those men. Look!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n The great panther leaped as a kitten leaps at a dead leaf whirling\r\noverhead, struck left and right into the empty air, that sang under the\r\nstrokes, landed noiselessly, and leaped again and again, while the\r\nhalf purr, half growl gathered head as steam rumbles in a boiler. \xe2\x80\x9cI\r\nam Bagheera--in the jungle--in the night, and my strength is in me. Who\r\nshall stay my stroke? Man-cub, with one blow of my paw I could beat thy\r\nhead flat as a dead frog in the summer!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cStrike, then!\xe2\x80\x9d said Mowgli, in the dialect of the village, NOT the\r\ntalk of the Jungle, and the human words brought Bagheera to a full stop,\r\nflung back on haunches that quivered under him, his head just at the\r\nlevel of Mowgli\xe2\x80\x99s. Once more Mowgli stared, as he had stared at the\r\nrebellious cubs, full into the beryl-green eyes till the red glare\r\nbehind their green went out like the light of a lighthouse shut off\r\ntwenty miles across the sea; till the eyes dropped, and the big head\r\nwith them--dropped lower and lower, and the red rasp of a tongue grated\r\non Mowgli\xe2\x80\x99s instep.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBrother--Brother--Brother!\xe2\x80\x9d the boy whispered, stroking steadily and\r\nlightly from the neck along the heaving back. \xe2\x80\x9cBe still, be still! It is\r\nthe fault of the night, and no fault of thine.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt was the smells of the night,\xe2\x80\x9d said Bagheera penitently. \xe2\x80\x9cThis air\r\ncries aloud to me. But how dost THOU know?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nOf course the air round an Indian village is full of all kinds of\r\nsmells, and to any creature who does nearly all his thinking through his\r\nnose, smells are as maddening as music and drugs are to human beings.\r\nMowgli gentled the panther for a few minutes longer, and he lay down\r\nlike a cat before a fire, his paws tucked under his breast, and his eyes\r\nhalf shut.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThou art of the Jungle and NOT of the Jungle,\xe2\x80\x9d he said at last. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd I\r\nam only a black panther. But I love thee, Little Brother.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey are very long at their talk under the tree,\xe2\x80\x9d Mowgli said, without\r\nnoticing the last sentence. \xe2\x80\x9cBuldeo must have told many tales. They\r\nshould come soon to drag the woman and her man out of the trap and put\r\nthem into the Red Flower. They will find that trap sprung. Ho! ho!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNay, listen,\xe2\x80\x9d said Bagheera. \xe2\x80\x9cThe fever is out of my blood now. Let\r\nthem find ME there! Few would leave their houses after meeting me. It is\r\nnot the first time I have been in a cage; and I do not think they will\r\ntie ME with cords.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBe wise, then,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mowgli, laughing; for he was beginning to feel as\r\nreckless as the panther, who had glided into the hut.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPah!\xe2\x80\x9d Bagheera grunted. \xe2\x80\x9cThis place is rank with Man, but here is just\r\nsuch a bed as they gave me to lie upon in the King\xe2\x80\x99s cages at Oodeypore.\r\nNow I lie down.\xe2\x80\x9d Mowgli heard the strings of the cot crack under the\r\ngreat brute\xe2\x80\x99s weight. \xe2\x80\x9cBy the Broken Lock that freed me, they will think\r\nthey have caught big game! Come and sit beside me, Little Brother; we\r\nwill give them \xe2\x80\x98good hunting\xe2\x80\x99 together!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo; I have another thought in my stomach. The Man-Pack shall not know\r\nwhat share I have in the sport. Make thine own hunt. I do not wish to\r\nsee them.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBe it so,\xe2\x80\x9d said Bagheera. \xe2\x80\x9cAh, now they come!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe conference under the peepul-tree had been growing noisier and\r\nnoisier, at the far end of the village. It broke in wild yells, and\r\na rush up the street of men and women, waving clubs and bamboos and\r\nsickles and knives. Buldeo and the Brahmin were at the head of it, but\r\nthe mob was close at their heels, and they cried, \xe2\x80\x9cThe witch and the\r\nwizard! Let us see if hot coins will make them confess! Burn the hut\r\nover their heads! We will teach them to shelter wolf-devils! Nay, beat\r\nthem first! Torches! More torches! Buldeo, heat the gun-barrels!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHere was some little difficulty with the catch of the door. It had been\r\nvery firmly fastened, but the crowd tore it away bodily, and the light\r\nof the torches streamed into the room where, stretched at full length on\r\nthe bed, his paws crossed and lightly hung down over one end, black\r\nas the Pit, and terrible as a demon, was Bagheera. There was one\r\nhalf-minute of desperate silence, as the front ranks of the crowd clawed\r\nand tore their way back from the threshold, and in that minute\r\nBagheera raised his head and yawned--elaborately, carefully, and\r\nostentatiously--as he would yawn when he wished to insult an equal.\r\nThe fringed lips drew back and up; the red tongue curled; the lower jaw\r\ndropped and dropped till you could see half-way down the hot gullet; and\r\nthe gigantic dog-teeth stood clear to the pit of the gums till they rang\r\ntogether, upper and under, with the snick of steel-faced wards shooting\r\nhome round the edges of a safe. Next instant the street was empty;\r\nBagheera had leaped back through the window, and stood at Mowgli\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nside, while a yelling, screaming torrent scrambled and tumbled one over\r\nanother in their panic haste to get to their own huts.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey will not stir till day comes,\xe2\x80\x9d said Bagheera quietly. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd now?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe silence of the afternoon sleep seemed to have overtaken the village;\r\nbut, as they listened, they could hear the sound of heavy grain-boxes\r\nbeing dragged over earthen floors and set down against doors. Bagheera\r\nwas quite right; the village would not stir till daylight. Mowgli sat\r\nstill, and thought, and his face grew darker and darker.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat have I done?\xe2\x80\x9d said Bagheera, at last coming to his feet, fawning.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNothing but great good. Watch them now till the day. I sleep.\xe2\x80\x9d Mowgli\r\nran off into the Jungle, and dropped like a dead man across a rock, and\r\nslept and slept the day round, and the night back again.\r\n\r\nWhen he waked, Bagheera was at his side, and there was a newly-killed\r\nbuck at his feet. Bagheera watched curiously while Mowgli went to work\r\nwith his skinning-knife, ate and drank, and turned over with his chin in\r\nhis hands.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe man and the woman are come safe within eye-shot of Khanhiwara,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n Bagheera said. \xe2\x80\x9cThy lair mother sent the word back by Chil, the Kite.\r\nThey found a horse before midnight of the night they were freed, and\r\nwent very quickly. Is not that well?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is well,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mowgli.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd thy Man-Pack in the village did not stir till the sun was high this\r\nmorning. Then they ate their food and ran back quickly to their houses.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDid they, by chance, see thee?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt may have been. I was rolling in the dust before the gate at dawn,\r\nand I may have made also some small song to myself. Now, Little Brother,\r\nthere is nothing more to do. Come hunting with me and Baloo. He has new\r\nhives that he wishes to show, and we all desire thee back again as of\r\nold. Take off that look which makes even me afraid! The man and woman\r\nwill not be put into the Red Flower, and all goes well in the Jungle. Is\r\nit not true? Let us forget the Man-Pack.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey shall be forgotten in a little while. Where does Hathi feed\r\nto-night?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhere he chooses. Who can answer for the Silent One? But why? What is\r\nthere Hathi can do which we cannot?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBid him and his three sons come here to me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut, indeed, and truly, Little Brother, it is not--it is not seemly\r\nto say \xe2\x80\x98Come,\xe2\x80\x99 and \xe2\x80\x98Go,\xe2\x80\x99 to Hathi. Remember, he is the Master of the\r\nJungle, and before the Man-Pack changed the look on thy face, he taught\r\nthee the Master-words of the Jungle.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is all one. I have a Master-word for him now. Bid him come to\r\nMowgli, the Frog: and if he does not hear at first, bid him come because\r\nof the Sack of the Fields of Bhurtpore.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe Sack of the Fields of Bhurtpore,\xe2\x80\x9d Bagheera repeated two or three\r\ntimes to make sure. \xe2\x80\x9cI go. Hathi can but be angry at the worst, and\r\nI would give a moon\xe2\x80\x99s hunting to hear a Master-word that compels the\r\nSilent One.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe went away, leaving Mowgli stabbing furiously with his skinning-knife\r\ninto the earth. Mowgli had never seen human blood in his life before\r\ntill he had seen, and--what meant much more to him--smelled Messua\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nblood on the thongs that bound her. And Messua had been kind to\r\nhim, and, so far as he knew anything about love, he loved Messua as\r\ncompletely as he hated the rest of mankind. But deeply as he loathed\r\nthem, their talk, their cruelty, and their cowardice, not for anything\r\nthe Jungle had to offer could he bring himself to take a human life, and\r\nhave that terrible scent of blood back again in his nostrils. His plan\r\nwas simpler, but much more thorough; and he laughed to himself when he\r\nthought that it was one of old Buldeo\xe2\x80\x99s tales told under the peepul-tree\r\nin the evening that had put the idea into his head.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt WAS a Master-word,\xe2\x80\x9d Bagheera whispered in his ear. \xe2\x80\x9cThey were\r\nfeeding by the river, and they obeyed as though they were bullocks. Look\r\nwhere they come now!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHathi and his three sons had arrived, in their usual way, without a\r\nsound. The mud of the river was still fresh on their flanks, and Hathi\r\nwas thoughtfully chewing the green stem of a young plantain-tree that he\r\nhad gouged up with his tusks. But every line in his vast body showed to\r\nBagheera, who could see things when he came across them, that it was not\r\nthe Master of the Jungle speaking to a Man-cub, but one who was afraid\r\ncoming before one who was not. His three sons rolled side by side,\r\nbehind their father.\r\n\r\nMowgli hardly lifted his head as Hathi gave him \xe2\x80\x9cGood hunting.\xe2\x80\x9d He kept\r\nhim swinging and rocking, and shifting from one foot to another, for\r\na long time before he spoke; and when he opened his mouth it was to\r\nBagheera, not to the elephants.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI will tell a tale that was told to me by the hunter ye hunted to-day,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n said Mowgli. \xe2\x80\x9cIt concerns an elephant, old and wise, who fell into a\r\ntrap, and the sharpened stake in the pit scarred him from a little above\r\nhis heel to the crest of his shoulder, leaving a white mark.\xe2\x80\x9d Mowgli\r\nthrew out his hand, and as Hathi wheeled the moonlight showed a long\r\nwhite scar on his slaty side, as though he had been struck with a\r\nred-hot whip. \xe2\x80\x9cMen came to take him from the trap,\xe2\x80\x9d Mowgli continued,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cbut he broke his ropes, for he was strong, and went away till his\r\nwound was healed. Then came he, angry, by night to the fields of\r\nthose hunters. And I remember now that he had three sons. These things\r\nhappened many, many Rains ago, and very far away--among the fields of\r\nBhurtpore. What came to those fields at the next reaping, Hathi?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey were reaped by me and by my three sons,\xe2\x80\x9d said Hathi.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd to the ploughing that follows the reaping?\xe2\x80\x9d said Mowgli.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere was no ploughing,\xe2\x80\x9d said Hathi.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd to the men that live by the green crops on the ground?\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nMowgli.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey went away.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd to the huts in which the men slept?\xe2\x80\x9d said Mowgli.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe tore the roofs to pieces, and the Jungle swallowed up the walls,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n said Hathi.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd what more?\xe2\x80\x9d said Mowgli.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAs much good ground as I can walk over in two nights from the east to\r\nthe west, and from the north to the south as much as I can walk over in\r\nthree nights, the Jungle took. We let in the Jungle upon five villages;\r\nand in those villages, and in their lands, the grazing-ground and the\r\nsoft crop-grounds, there is not one man to-day who takes his food from\r\nthe ground. That was the Sack of the Fields of Bhurtpore, which I and\r\nmy three sons did; and now I ask, Man-cub, how the news of it came to\r\nthee?\xe2\x80\x9d said Hathi.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA man told me, and now I see even Buldeo can speak truth. It was well\r\ndone, Hathi with the white mark; but the second time it shall be done\r\nbetter, for the reason that there is a man to direct. Thou knowest the\r\nvillage of the Man-Pack that cast me out? They are idle, senseless, and\r\ncruel; they play with their mouths, and they do not kill the weaker for\r\nfood, but for sport. When they are full-fed they would throw their own\r\nbreed into the Red Flower. This I have seen. It is not well that they\r\nshould live here any more. I hate them!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cKill, then,\xe2\x80\x9d said the youngest of Hathi\xe2\x80\x99s three sons, picking up a tuft\r\nof grass, dusting it against his fore-legs, and throwing it away, while\r\nhis little red eyes glanced furtively from side to side.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat good are white bones to me?\xe2\x80\x9d Mowgli answered angrily. \xe2\x80\x9cAm I the\r\ncub of a wolf to play in the sun with a raw head? I have killed Shere\r\nKhan, and his hide rots on the Council Rock; but--but I do not know\r\nwhither Shere Khan is gone, and my stomach is still empty. Now I\r\nwill take that which I can see and touch. Let in the Jungle upon that\r\nvillage, Hathi!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBagheera shivered, and cowered down. He could understand, if the worst\r\ncame to the worst, a quick rush down the village street, and a right and\r\nleft blow into a crowd, or a crafty killing of men as they ploughed in\r\nthe twilight; but this scheme for deliberately blotting out an entire\r\nvillage from the eyes of man and beast frightened him. Now he saw why\r\nMowgli had sent for Hathi. No one but the long-lived elephant could plan\r\nand carry through such a war.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLet them run as the men ran from the fields of Bhurtpore, till we have\r\nthe rain-water for the only plough, and the noise of the rain on the\r\nthick leaves for the pattering of their spindles--till Bagheera and I\r\nlair in the house of the Brahmin, and the buck drink at the tank behind\r\nthe temple! Let in the Jungle, Hathi!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut I--but we have no quarrel with them, and it needs the red rage\r\nof great pain ere we tear down the places where men sleep,\xe2\x80\x9d said Hathi\r\ndoubtfully.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAre ye the only eaters of grass in the Jungle? Drive in your peoples.\r\nLet the deer and the pig and the nilghai look to it. Ye need never show\r\na hand\xe2\x80\x99s-breadth of hide till the fields are naked. Let in the Jungle,\r\nHathi!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere will be no killing? My tusks were red at the Sack of the Fields\r\nof Bhurtpore, and I would not wake that smell again.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNor I. I do not wish even their bones to lie on the clean earth. Let\r\nthem go and find a fresh lair. They cannot stay here. I have seen and\r\nsmelled the blood of the woman that gave me food--the woman whom they\r\nwould have killed but for me. Only the smell of the new grass on their\r\ndoor-steps can take away that smell. It burns in my mouth. Let in the\r\nJungle, Hathi!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh!\xe2\x80\x9d said Hathi. \xe2\x80\x9cSo did the scar of the stake burn on my hide till we\r\nwatched the villages die under in the spring growth. Now I see. Thy war\r\nshall be our war. We will let in the jungle!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMowgli had hardly time to catch his breath--he was shaking all over with\r\nrage and hate before the place where the elephants had stood was empty,\r\nand Bagheera was looking at him with terror.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBy the Broken Lock that freed me!\xe2\x80\x9d said the Black Panther at last. \xe2\x80\x9cArt\r\nTHOU the naked thing I spoke for in the Pack when all was young?\r\nMaster of the Jungle, when my strength goes, speak for me--speak for\r\nBaloo--speak for us all! We are cubs before thee! Snapped twigs under\r\nfoot! Fawns that have lost their doe!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe idea of Bagheera being a stray fawn upset Mowgli altogether, and he\r\nlaughed and caught his breath, and sobbed and laughed again, till he had\r\nto jump into a pool to make himself stop. Then he swam round and round,\r\nducking in and out of the bars of the moonlight like the frog, his\r\nnamesake.\r\n\r\nBy this time Hathi and his three sons had turned, each to one point of\r\nthe compass, and were striding silently down the valleys a mile away.\r\nThey went on and on for two days\xe2\x80\x99 march--that is to say, a long sixty\r\nmiles--through the Jungle; and every step they took, and every wave of\r\ntheir trunks, was known and noted and talked over by Mang and Chil and\r\nthe Monkey People and all the birds. Then they began to feed, and fed\r\nquietly for a week or so. Hathi and his sons are like Kaa, the Rock\r\nPython. They never hurry till they have to.\r\n\r\nAt the end of that time--and none knew who had started it--a rumour went\r\nthrough the Jungle that there was better food and water to be found in\r\nsuch and such a valley. The pig--who, of course, will go to the ends of\r\nthe earth for a full meal--moved first by companies, scuffling over the\r\nrocks, and the deer followed, with the small wild foxes that live on\r\nthe dead and dying of the herds; and the heavy-shouldered nilghai moved\r\nparallel with the deer, and the wild buffaloes of the swamps came after\r\nthe nilghai. The least little thing would have turned the scattered,\r\nstraggling droves that grazed and sauntered and drank and grazed again;\r\nbut whenever there was an alarm some one would rise up and soothe them.\r\nAt one time it would be Ikki the Porcupine, full of news of good feed\r\njust a little farther on; at another Mang would cry cheerily and flap\r\ndown a glade to show it was all empty; or Baloo, his mouth full of\r\nroots, would shamble alongside a wavering line and half frighten, half\r\nromp it clumsily back to the proper road. Very many creatures broke back\r\nor ran away or lost interest, but very many were left to go forward. At\r\nthe end of another ten days or so the situation was this. The deer and\r\nthe pig and the nilghai were milling round and round in a circle of\r\neight or ten miles radius, while the Eaters of Flesh skirmished round\r\nits edge. And the centre of that circle was the village, and round the\r\nvillage the crops were ripening, and in the crops sat men on what they\r\ncall machans--platforms like pigeon-perches, made of sticks at the top\r\nof four poles--to scare away birds and other stealers. Then the deer\r\nwere coaxed no more. The Eaters of Flesh were close behind them, and\r\nforced them forward and inward.\r\n\r\nIt was a dark night when Hathi and his three sons slipped down from the\r\nJungle, and broke off the poles of the machans with their trunks; they\r\nfell as a snapped stalk of hemlock in bloom falls, and the men that\r\ntumbled from them heard the deep gurgling of the elephants in their\r\nears. Then the vanguard of the bewildered armies of the deer broke down\r\nand flooded into the village grazing-grounds and the ploughed fields;\r\nand the sharp-hoofed, rooting wild pig came with them, and what the deer\r\nleft the pig spoiled, and from time to time an alarm of wolves would\r\nshake the herds, and they would rush to and fro desperately, treading\r\ndown the young barley, and cutting flat the banks of the irrigating\r\nchannels. Before the dawn broke the pressure on the outside of the\r\ncircle gave way at one point. The Eaters of Flesh had fallen back and\r\nleft an open path to the south, and drove upon drove of buck fled along\r\nit. Others, who were bolder, lay up in the thickets to finish their meal\r\nnext night.\r\n\r\nBut the work was practically done. When the villagers looked in the\r\nmorning they saw their crops were lost. And that meant death if they did\r\nnot get away, for they lived year in and year out as near to starvation\r\nas the Jungle was near to them. When the buffaloes were sent to graze\r\nthe hungry brutes found that the deer had cleared the grazing-grounds,\r\nand so wandered into the Jungle and drifted off with their wild mates;\r\nand when twilight fell the three or four ponies that belonged to the\r\nvillage lay in their stables with their heads beaten in. Only Bagheera\r\ncould have given those strokes, and only Bagheera would have thought of\r\ninsolently dragging the last carcass to the open street.\r\n\r\nThe villagers had no heart to make fires in the fields that night, so\r\nHathi and his three sons went gleaning among what was left; and where\r\nHathi gleans there is no need to follow. The men decided to live on\r\ntheir stored seed-corn until the rains had fallen, and then to take\r\nwork as servants till they could catch up with the lost year; but as\r\nthe grain-dealer was thinking of his well-filled crates of corn, and the\r\nprices he would levy at the sale of it, Hathi\xe2\x80\x99s sharp tusks were picking\r\nout the corner of his mud-house, and smashing open the big wicker chest,\r\nleeped with cow-dung, where the precious stuff lay.\r\n\r\nWhen that last loss was discovered, it was the Brahmin\xe2\x80\x99s turn to speak.\r\nHe had prayed to his own Gods without answer. It might be, he said,\r\nthat, unconsciously, the village had offended some one of the Gods of\r\nthe Jungle, for, beyond doubt, the Jungle was against them. So they sent\r\nfor the head-man of the nearest tribe of wandering Gonds--little, wise,\r\nand very black hunters, living in the deep Jungle, whose fathers came of\r\nthe oldest race in India--the aboriginal owners of the land. They made\r\nthe Gond welcome with what they had, and he stood on one leg, his bow in\r\nhis hand, and two or three poisoned arrows stuck through his top-knot,\r\nlooking half afraid and half contemptuously at the anxious villagers\r\nand their ruined fields. They wished to know whether his Gods--the Old\r\nGods--were angry with them and what sacrifices should be offered. The\r\nGond said nothing, but picked up a trail of the Karela, the vine that\r\nbears the bitter wild gourd, and laced it to and fro across the temple\r\ndoor in the face of the staring red Hindu image. Then he pushed with his\r\nhand in the open air along the road to Khanhiwara, and went back to his\r\nJungle, and watched the Jungle People drifting through it. He knew that\r\nwhen the Jungle moves only white men can hope to turn it aside.\r\n\r\nThere was no need to ask his meaning. The wild gourd would grow where\r\nthey had worshipped their God, and the sooner they saved themselves the\r\nbetter.\r\n\r\nBut it is hard to tear a village from its moorings. They stayed on as\r\nlong as any summer food was left to them, and they tried to gather nuts\r\nin the Jungle, but shadows with glaring eyes watched them, and rolled\r\nbefore them even at mid-day; and when they ran back afraid to their\r\nwalls, on the tree-trunks they had passed not five minutes before the\r\nbark would be stripped and chiselled with the stroke of some great\r\ntaloned paw. The more they kept to their village, the bolder grew the\r\nwild things that gambolled and bellowed on the grazing-grounds by the\r\nWaingunga. They had no time to patch and plaster the rear walls of the\r\nempty byres that backed on to the Jungle; the wild pig trampled them\r\ndown, and the knotty-rooted vines hurried after and threw their elbows\r\nover the new-won ground, and the coarse grass bristled behind the vines\r\nlike the lances of a goblin army following a retreat. The unmarried men\r\nran away first, and carried the news far and near that the village was\r\ndoomed. Who could fight, they said, against the Jungle, or the Gods\r\nof the Jungle, when the very village cobra had left his hole in the\r\nplatform under the peepul-tree? So their little commerce with the\r\noutside world shrunk as the trodden paths across the open grew fewer\r\nand fainter. At last the nightly trumpetings of Hathi and his three sons\r\nceased to trouble them; for they had no more to be robbed of. The crop\r\non the ground and the seed in the ground had been taken. The outlying\r\nfields were already losing their shape, and it was time to throw\r\nthemselves on the charity of the English at Khanhiwara.\r\n\r\nNative fashion, they delayed their departure from one day to another\r\ntill the first Rains caught them and the unmended roofs let in a flood,\r\nand the grazing-ground stood ankle deep, and all life came on with a\r\nrush after the heat of the summer. Then they waded out--men, women,\r\nand children--through the blinding hot rain of the morning, but turned\r\nnaturally for one farewell look at their homes.\r\n\r\nThey heard, as the last burdened family filed through the gate, a crash\r\nof falling beams and thatch behind the walls. They saw a shiny,\r\nsnaky black trunk lifted for an instant, scattering sodden thatch. It\r\ndisappeared, and there was another crash, followed by a squeal. Hathi\r\nhad been plucking off the roofs of the huts as you pluck water-lilies,\r\nand a rebounding beam had pricked him. He needed only this to unchain\r\nhis full strength, for of all things in the Jungle the wild elephant\r\nenraged is the most wantonly destructive. He kicked backward at a mud\r\nwall that crumbled at the stroke, and, crumbling, melted to yellow\r\nmud under the torrent of rain. Then he wheeled and squealed, and tore\r\nthrough the narrow streets, leaning against the huts right and left,\r\nshivering the crazy doors, and crumpling up the caves; while his\r\nthree sons raged behind as they had raged at the Sack of the Fields of\r\nBhurtpore.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe Jungle will swallow these shells,\xe2\x80\x9d said a quiet voice in the\r\nwreckage. \xe2\x80\x9cIt is the outer wall that must lie down,\xe2\x80\x9d and Mowgli, with\r\nthe rain sluicing over his bare shoulders and arms, leaped back from a\r\nwall that was settling like a tired buffalo.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAll in good time,\xe2\x80\x9d panted Hathi. \xe2\x80\x9cOh, but my tusks were red at\r\nBhurtpore; To the outer wall, children! With the head! Together! Now!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe four pushed side by side; the outer wall bulged, split, and fell,\r\nand the villagers, dumb with horror, saw the savage, clay-streaked\r\nheads of the wreckers in the ragged gap. Then they fled, houseless and\r\nfoodless, down the valley, as their village, shredded and tossed and\r\ntrampled, melted behind them.\r\n\r\nA month later the place was a dimpled mound, covered with soft, green\r\nyoung stuff; and by the end of the Rains there was the roaring jungle in\r\nfull blast on the spot that had been under plough not six months before.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nMOWGLI\xe2\x80\x99S SONG AGAINST PEOPLE\r\n\r\n     I will let loose against you the fleet-footed vines--\r\n     I will call in the Jungle to stamp out your lines!\r\n         The roofs shall fade before it,\r\n           The house-beams shall fall,\r\n         And the Karela, the bitter Karela,\r\n           Shall cover it all!\r\n\r\n     In the gates of these your councils my people shall sing,\r\n     In the doors of these your garners the Bat-folk shall cling;\r\n         And the snake shall be your watchman,\r\n           By a hearthstone unswept;\r\n         For the Karela, the bitter Karela,\r\n           Shall fruit where ye slept!\r\n\r\n     Ye shall not see my strikers; ye shall hear them and guess;\r\n     By night, before the moon-rise, I will send for my cess,\r\n         And the wolf shall be your herdsman\r\n           By a landmark removed,\r\n         For the Karela, the bitter Karela,\r\n           Shall seed where ye loved!\r\n\r\n     I will reap your fields before you at the hands of a host;\r\n     Ye shall glean behind my reapers, for the bread that is lost,\r\n         And the deer shall be your oxen\r\n           By a headland untilled,\r\n         For the Karela, the bitter Karela,\r\n           Shall leaf where ye build!\r\n\r\n     I have untied against you the club-footed vines,\r\n     I have sent in the Jungle to swamp out your lines.\r\n         The trees--the trees are on you!\r\n           The house-beams shall fall,\r\n         And the Karela, the bitter Karela,\r\n            Shall cover you all!\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nTHE UNDERTAKERS\r\n\r\n        When ye say to Tabaqui, \xe2\x80\x9cMy Brother!\xe2\x80\x9d when ye call the\r\n            Hyena to meat,\r\n        Ye may cry the Full Truce with Jacala--the Belly that runs\r\n            on four feet.\r\n                                                  Jungle Law\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cRespect the aged!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt was a thick voice--a muddy voice that would have made you shudder--a\r\nvoice like something soft breaking in two. There was a quaver in it, a\r\ncroak and a whine.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cRespect the aged! O Companions of the River--respect the aged!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nNothing could be seen on the broad reach of the river except a\r\nlittle fleet of square-sailed, wooden-pinned barges, loaded with\r\nbuilding-stone, that had just come under the railway bridge, and were\r\ndriving down-stream. They put their clumsy helms over to avoid the\r\nsand-bar made by the scour of the bridge-piers, and as they passed,\r\nthree abreast, the horrible voice began again:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cO Brahmins of the River--respect the aged and infirm!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nA boatman turned where he sat on the gunwale, lifted up his hand, said\r\nsomething that was not a blessing, and the boats creaked on through\r\nthe twilight. The broad Indian river, that looked more like a chain\r\nof little lakes than a stream, was as smooth as glass, reflecting the\r\nsandy-red sky in mid-channel, but splashed with patches of yellow and\r\ndusky purple near and under the low banks. Little creeks ran into the\r\nriver in the wet season, but now their dry mouths hung clear above\r\nwater-line. On the left shore, and almost under the railway bridge,\r\nstood a mud-and-brick and thatch-and-stick village, whose main street,\r\nfull of cattle going back to their byres, ran straight to the river, and\r\nended in a sort of rude brick pier-head, where people who wanted to\r\nwash could wade in step by step. That was the Ghaut of the village of\r\nMugger-Ghaut.\r\n\r\nNight was falling fast over the fields of lentils and rice and cotton\r\nin the low-lying ground yearly flooded by the river; over the reeds\r\nthat fringed the elbow of the bend, and the tangled jungle of the\r\ngrazing-grounds behind the still reeds. The parrots and crows, who had\r\nbeen chattering and shouting over their evening drink, had flown inland\r\nto roost, crossing the out-going battalions of the flying-foxes; and\r\ncloud upon cloud of water-birds came whistling and \xe2\x80\x9chonking\xe2\x80\x9d to\r\nthe cover of the reed-beds. There were geese, barrel-headed and\r\nblack-backed, teal, widgeon, mallard, and sheldrake, with curlews, and\r\nhere and there a flamingo.\r\n\r\nA lumbering Adjutant-crane brought up the rear, flying as though each\r\nslow stroke would be his last.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cRespect the aged! Brahmins of the River--respect the aged!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe Adjutant half turned his head, sheered a little in the direction of\r\nthe voice, and landed stiffly on the sand-bar below the bridge. Then you\r\nsaw what a ruffianly brute he really was. His back view was immensely\r\nrespectable, for he stood nearly six feet high, and looked rather like a\r\nvery proper bald-headed parson. In front it was different, for his Ally\r\nSloper-like head and neck had not a feather to them, and there was a\r\nhorrible raw-skin pouch on his neck under his chin--a hold-all for the\r\nthings his pick-axe beak might steal. His legs were long and thin and\r\nskinny, but he moved them delicately, and looked at them with pride as\r\nhe preened down his ashy-gray tail-feathers, glanced over the smooth of\r\nhis shoulder, and stiffened into \xe2\x80\x9cStand at attention.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nA mangy little Jackal, who had been yapping hungrily on a low bluff,\r\ncocked up his ears and tail, and scuttered across the shallows to join\r\nthe Adjutant.\r\n\r\nHe was the lowest of his caste--not that the best of jackals are good\r\nfor much, but this one was peculiarly low, being half a beggar, half a\r\ncriminal--a cleaner-up of village rubbish-heaps, desperately timid or\r\nwildly bold, everlastingly hungry, and full of cunning that never did\r\nhim any good.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cUgh!\xe2\x80\x9d he said, shaking himself dolefully as he landed. \xe2\x80\x9cMay the red\r\nmange destroy the dogs of this village! I have three bites for each flea\r\nupon me, and all because I looked--only looked, mark you--at an old shoe\r\nin a cow-byre. Can I eat mud?\xe2\x80\x9d He scratched himself under his left ear.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI heard,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Adjutant, in a voice like a blunt saw going through\r\na thick board--\xe2\x80\x9cI HEARD there was a new-born puppy in that same shoe.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTo hear is one thing; to know is another,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Jackal, who had a\r\nvery fair knowledge of proverbs, picked up by listening to men round the\r\nvillage fires of an evening.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cQuite true. So, to make sure, I took care of that puppy while the dogs\r\nwere busy elsewhere.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey were VERY busy,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Jackal. \xe2\x80\x9cWell, I must not go to the\r\nvillage hunting for scraps yet awhile. And so there truly was a blind\r\npuppy in that shoe?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is here,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Adjutant, squinting over his beak at his full\r\npouch. \xe2\x80\x9cA small thing, but acceptable now that charity is dead in the\r\nworld.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAhai! The world is iron in these days,\xe2\x80\x9d wailed the Jackal. Then his\r\nrestless eye caught the least possible ripple on the water, and he went\r\non quickly: \xe2\x80\x9cLife is hard for us all, and I doubt not that even our\r\nexcellent master, the Pride of the Ghaut and the Envy of the River----\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA liar, a flatterer, and a Jackal were all hatched out of the same\r\negg,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Adjutant to nobody in particular; for he was rather a\r\nfine sort of a liar on his own account when he took the trouble.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, the Envy of the River,\xe2\x80\x9d the Jackal repeated, raising his voice.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cEven he, I doubt not, finds that since the bridge has been built good\r\nfood is more scarce. But on the other hand, though I would by no means\r\nsay this to his noble face, he is so wise and so virtuous--as I, alas I\r\nam not----\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhen the Jackal owns he is gray, how black must the Jackal be!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n muttered the Adjutant. He could not see what was coming.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat his food never fails, and in consequence----\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThere was a soft grating sound, as though a boat had just touched in\r\nshoal water. The Jackal spun round quickly and faced (it is always\r\nbest to face) the creature he had been talking about. It was a\r\ntwenty-four-foot crocodile, cased in what looked like treble-riveted\r\nboiler-plate, studded and keeled and crested; the yellow points of his\r\nupper teeth just overhanging his beautifully fluted lower jaw. It\r\nwas the blunt-nosed Mugger of Mugger-Ghaut, older than any man in the\r\nvillage, who had given his name to the village; the demon of the ford\r\nbefore the railway bridge, came--murderer, man-eater, and local fetish\r\nin one. He lay with his chin in the shallows, keeping his place by an\r\nalmost invisible rippling of his tail, and well the Jackal knew that one\r\nstroke of that same tail in the water would carry the Mugger up the bank\r\nwith the rush of a steam-engine.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAuspiciously met, Protector of the Poor!\xe2\x80\x9d he fawned, backing at every\r\nword. \xe2\x80\x9cA delectable voice was heard, and we came in the hopes of sweet\r\nconversation. My tailless presumption, while waiting here, led me,\r\nindeed, to speak of thee. It is my hope that nothing was overheard.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nNow the Jackal had spoken just to be listened to, for he knew flattery\r\nwas the best way of getting things to eat, and the Mugger knew that\r\nthe Jackal had spoken for this end, and the Jackal knew that the Mugger\r\nknew, and the Mugger knew that the Jackal knew that the Mugger knew, and\r\nso they were all very contented together.\r\n\r\nThe old brute pushed and panted and grunted up the bank, mumbling,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cRespect the aged and infirm!\xe2\x80\x9d and all the time his little eyes burned\r\nlike coals under the heavy, horny eyelids on the top of his triangular\r\nhead, as he shoved his bloated barrel-body along between his crutched\r\nlegs. Then he settled down, and, accustomed as the Jackal was to his\r\nways, he could not help starting, for the hundredth time, when he saw\r\nhow exactly the Mugger imitated a log adrift on the bar. He had even\r\ntaken pains to lie at the exact angle a naturally stranded log would\r\nmake with the water, having regard to the current of the season at the\r\ntime and place. All this was only a matter of habit, of course, because\r\nthe Mugger had come ashore for pleasure; but a crocodile is never quite\r\nfull, and if the Jackal had been deceived by the likeness he would not\r\nhave lived to philosophise over it.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy child, I heard nothing,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Mugger, shutting one eye. \xe2\x80\x9cThe\r\nwater was in my ears, and also I was faint with hunger. Since the\r\nrailway bridge was built my people at my village have ceased to love me;\r\nand that is breaking my heart.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, shame!\xe2\x80\x9d said the Jackal. \xe2\x80\x9cSo noble a heart, too! But men are all\r\nalike, to my mind.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNay, there are very great differences indeed,\xe2\x80\x9d the Mugger answered\r\ngently. \xe2\x80\x9cSome are as lean as boat-poles. Others again are fat as\r\nyoung ja--dogs. Never would I causelessly revile men. They are of all\r\nfashions, but the long years have shown me that, one with another, they\r\nare very good. Men, women, and children--I have no fault to find with\r\nthem. And remember, child, he who rebukes the World is rebuked by the\r\nWorld.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFlattery is worse than an empty tin can in the belly. But that which we\r\nhave just heard is wisdom,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Adjutant, bringing down one foot.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cConsider, though, their ingratitude to this excellent one,\xe2\x80\x9d began the\r\nJackal tenderly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNay, nay, not ingratitude!\xe2\x80\x9d the Mugger said. \xe2\x80\x9cThey do not think for\r\nothers; that is all. But I have noticed, lying at my station below the\r\nford, that the stairs of the new bridge are cruelly hard to climb, both\r\nfor old people and young children. The old, indeed, are not so worthy of\r\nconsideration, but I am grieved--I am truly grieved--on account of the\r\nfat children. Still, I think, in a little while, when the newness of the\r\nbridge has worn away, we shall see my people\xe2\x80\x99s bare brown legs bravely\r\nsplashing through the ford as before. Then the old Mugger will be\r\nhonoured again.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut surely I saw Marigold wreaths floating off the edge of the Ghaut\r\nonly this noon,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Adjutant.\r\n\r\nMarigold wreaths are a sign of reverence all India over.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAn error--an error. It was the wife of the sweetmeat-seller. She loses\r\nher eyesight year by year, and cannot tell a log from me--the Mugger of\r\nthe Ghaut. I saw the mistake when she threw the garland, for I was lying\r\nat the very foot of the Ghaut, and had she taken another step I might\r\nhave shown her some little difference. Yet she meant well, and we must\r\nconsider the spirit of the offering.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat good are marigold wreaths when one is on the rubbish-heap?\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nthe Jackal, hunting for fleas, but keeping one wary eye on his Protector\r\nof the Poor.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTrue, but they have not yet begun to make the rubbish-heap that shall\r\ncarry ME. Five times have I seen the river draw back from the village\r\nand make new land at the foot of the street. Five times have I seen the\r\nvillage rebuilt on the banks, and I shall see it built yet five times\r\nmore. I am no faithless, fish-hunting Gavial, I, at Kasi to-day and\r\nPrayag to-morrow, as the saying is, but the true and constant watcher of\r\nthe ford. It is not for nothing, child, that the village bears my name,\r\nand \xe2\x80\x98he who watches long,\xe2\x80\x99 as the saying is, \xe2\x80\x98shall at last have his\r\nreward.\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_I_ have watched long--very long--nearly all my life, and my reward has\r\nbeen bites and blows,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Jackal.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHo! ho! ho!\xe2\x80\x9d roared the Adjutant.\r\n\r\n     \xe2\x80\x9cIn August was the Jackal born;\r\n        The Rains fell in September;\r\n     \xe2\x80\x98Now such a fearful flood as this,\xe2\x80\x99\r\n        Says he, \xe2\x80\x98I can\xe2\x80\x99t remember!\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThere is one very unpleasant peculiarity about the Adjutant. At\r\nuncertain times he suffers from acute attacks of the fidgets or cramp\r\nin his legs, and though he is more virtuous to behold than any of the\r\ncranes, who are all immensely respectable, he flies off into wild,\r\ncripple-stilt war-dances, half opening his wings and bobbing his bald\r\nhead up and down; while for reasons best known to himself he is very\r\ncareful to time his worst attacks with his nastiest remarks. At the last\r\nword of his song he came to attention again, ten times adjutaunter than\r\nbefore.\r\n\r\nThe Jackal winced, though he was full three seasons old, but you cannot\r\nresent an insult from a person with a beak a yard long, and the power of\r\ndriving it like a javelin. The Adjutant was a most notorious coward, but\r\nthe Jackal was worse.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe must live before we can learn,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Mugger, \xe2\x80\x9cand there is this\r\nto say: Little jackals are very common, child, but such a mugger as I am\r\nis not common. For all that, I am not proud, since pride is destruction;\r\nbut take notice, it is Fate, and against his Fate no one who swims or\r\nwalks or runs should say anything at all. I am well contented with Fate.\r\nWith good luck, a keen eye, and the custom of considering whether a\r\ncreek or a backwater has an outlet to it ere you ascend, much may be\r\ndone.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOnce I heard that even the Protector of the Poor made a mistake,\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nthe Jackal viciously.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTrue; but there my Fate helped me. It was before I had come to my\r\nfull growth--before the last famine but three (by the Right and Left of\r\nGunga, how full used the streams to be in those days!). Yes, I was young\r\nand unthinking, and when the flood came, who so pleased as I? A little\r\nmade me very happy then. The village was deep in flood, and I swam above\r\nthe Ghaut and went far inland, up to the rice-fields, and they were deep\r\nin good mud. I remember also a pair of bracelets (glass they were,\r\nand troubled me not a little) that I found that evening. Yes, glass\r\nbracelets; and, if my memory serves me well, a shoe. I should have\r\nshaken off both shoes, but I was hungry. I learned better later. Yes.\r\nAnd so I fed and rested me; but when I was ready to go to the river\r\nagain the flood had fallen, and I walked through the mud of the main\r\nstreet. Who but I? Came out all my people, priests and women and\r\nchildren, and I looked upon them with benevolence. The mud is not a good\r\nplace to fight in. Said a boatman, \xe2\x80\x98Get axes and kill him, for he is the\r\nMugger of the ford.\xe2\x80\x99 \xe2\x80\x98Not so,\xe2\x80\x99 said the Brahmin. \xe2\x80\x98Look, he is driving\r\nthe flood before him! He is the godling of the village.\xe2\x80\x99 Then they\r\nthrew many flowers at me, and by happy thought one led a goat across the\r\nroad.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow good--how very good is goat!\xe2\x80\x9d said the Jackal.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHairy--too hairy, and when found in the water more than likely to hide\r\na cross-shaped hook. But that goat I accepted, and went down to the\r\nGhaut in great honour. Later, my Fate sent me the boatman who had\r\ndesired to cut off my tail with an axe. His boat grounded upon an old\r\nshoal which you would not remember.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe are not ALL jackals here,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Adjutant. \xe2\x80\x9cWas it the shoal made\r\nwhere the stone-boats sank in the year of the great drouth--a long shoal\r\nthat lasted three floods?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere were two,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Mugger; \xe2\x80\x9can upper and a lower shoal.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAy, I forgot. A channel divided them, and later dried up again,\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nthe Adjutant, who prided himself on his memory.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOn the lower shoal my well-wisher\xe2\x80\x99s craft grounded. He was sleeping in\r\nthe bows, and, half awake, leaped over to his waist--no, it was no more\r\nthan to his knees--to push off. His empty boat went on and touched again\r\nbelow the next reach, as the river ran then. I followed, because I knew\r\nmen would come out to drag it ashore.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd did they do so?\xe2\x80\x9d said the Jackal, a little awe-stricken. This was\r\nhunting on a scale that impressed him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere and lower down they did. I went no farther, but that gave me\r\nthree in one day--well-fed manjis (boatmen) all, and, except in the\r\ncase of the last (then I was careless), never a cry to warn those on the\r\nbank.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, noble sport! But what cleverness and great judgment it requires!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n said the Jackal.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot cleverness, child, but only thought. A little thought in life\r\nis like salt upon rice, as the boatmen say, and I have thought deeply\r\nalways. The Gavial, my cousin, the fish-eater, has told me how hard it\r\nis for him to follow his fish, and how one fish differs from the other,\r\nand how he must know them all, both together and apart. I say that is\r\nwisdom; but, on the other hand, my cousin, the Gavial, lives among his\r\npeople. MY people do not swim in companies, with their mouths out of the\r\nwater, as Rewa does; nor do they constantly rise to the surface of the\r\nwater, and turn over on their sides, like Mohoo and little Chapta; nor\r\ndo they gather in shoals after flood, like Batchua and Chilwa.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAll are very good eating,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Adjutant, clattering his beak.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo my cousin says, and makes a great to-do over hunting them, but\r\nthey do not climb the banks to escape his sharp nose. MY people are\r\notherwise. Their life is on the land, in the houses, among the cattle.\r\nI must know what they do, and what they are about to do; and adding the\r\ntail to the trunk, as the saying is, I make up the whole elephant. Is\r\nthere a green branch and an iron ring hanging over a doorway? The old\r\nMugger knows that a boy has been born in that house, and must some\r\nday come down to the Ghaut to play. Is a maiden to be married? The old\r\nMugger knows, for he sees the men carry gifts back and forth; and she,\r\ntoo, comes down to the Ghaut to bathe before her wedding, and--he is\r\nthere. Has the river changed its channel, and made new land where there\r\nwas only sand before? The Mugger knows.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow, of what use is that knowledge?\xe2\x80\x9d said the Jackal. \xe2\x80\x9cThe river has\r\nshifted even in my little life.\xe2\x80\x9d Indian rivers are nearly always moving\r\nabout in their beds, and will shift, sometimes, as much as two or three\r\nmiles in a season, drowning the fields on one bank, and spreading good\r\nsilt on the other.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere is no knowledge so useful,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Mugger, \xe2\x80\x9cfor new land means\r\nnew quarrels. The Mugger knows. Oho! the Mugger knows. As soon as the\r\nwater has drained off, he creeps up the little creeks that men think\r\nwould not hide a dog, and there he waits. Presently comes a farmer\r\nsaying he will plant cucumbers here, and melons there, in the new land\r\nthat the river has given him. He feels the good mud with his bare\r\ntoes. Anon comes another, saying he will put onions, and carrots, and\r\nsugar-cane in such and such places.
