They meet as boats adrift meet,\r\nand each rolls his eye at the other under the big blue turban. The old\r\nMugger sees and hears. Each calls the other \xe2\x80\x98Brother,\xe2\x80\x99 and they go to\r\nmark out the boundaries of the new land. The Mugger hurries with them\r\nfrom point to point, shuffling very low through the mud. Now they begin\r\nto quarrel! Now they say hot words! Now they pull turbans! Now they lift\r\nup their lathis (clubs), and, at last, one falls backward into the mud,\r\nand the other runs away. When he comes back the dispute is settled, as\r\nthe iron-bound bamboo of the loser witnesses. Yet they are not grateful\r\nto the Mugger. No, they cry \xe2\x80\x98Murder!\xe2\x80\x99 and their families fight with\r\nsticks, twenty a-side. My people are good people--upland Jats--Malwais\r\nof the Bet. They do not give blows for sport, and, when the fight is\r\ndone, the old Mugger waits far down the river, out of sight of the\r\nvillage, behind the kikar-scrub yonder. Then come they down, my\r\nbroad-shouldered Jats--eight or nine together under the stars, bearing\r\nthe dead man upon a bed. They are old men with gray beards, and voices\r\nas deep as mine. They light a little fire--ah! how well I know that\r\nfire!--and they drink tobacco, and they nod their heads together forward\r\nin a ring, or sideways toward the dead man upon the bank. They say the\r\nEnglish Law will come with a rope for this matter, and that such a man\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nfamily will be ashamed, because such a man must be hanged in the great\r\nsquare of the Jail. Then say the friends of the dead, \xe2\x80\x98Let him hang!\xe2\x80\x99\r\nand the talk is all to do over again--once, twice, twenty times in the\r\nlong night. Then says one, at last, \xe2\x80\x98The fight was a fair fight. Let us\r\ntake blood-money, a little more than is offered by the slayer, and we\r\nwill say no more about it.\xe2\x80\x99 Then do they haggle over the blood-money,\r\nfor the dead was a strong man, leaving many sons. Yet before amratvela\r\n(sunrise) they put the fire to him a little, as the custom is, and the\r\ndead man comes to me, and HE says no more about it. Aha! my children,\r\nthe Mugger knows--the Mugger knows--and my Malwah Jats are a good\r\npeople!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey are too close--too narrow in the hand for my crop,\xe2\x80\x9d croaked the\r\nAdjutant. \xe2\x80\x9cThey waste not the polish on the cow\xe2\x80\x99s horn, as the saying\r\nis; and, again, who can glean after a Malwai?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, I--glean--THEM,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Mugger.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow, in Calcutta of the South, in the old days,\xe2\x80\x9d the Adjutant went on,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9ceverything was thrown into the streets, and we picked and chose. Those\r\nwore dainty seasons. But to-day they keep their streets as clean as the\r\noutside of an egg, and my people fly away. To be clean is one thing;\r\nto dust, sweep, and sprinkle seven times a day wearies the very Gods\r\nthemselves.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere was a down-country jackal had it from a brother, who told me,\r\nthat in Calcutta of the South all the jackals were as fat as otters in\r\nthe Rains,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Jackal, his mouth watering at the bare thought of\r\nit.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, but the white-faces are there--the English, and they bring dogs\r\nfrom somewhere down the river in boats--big fat dogs--to keep those same\r\njackals lean,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Adjutant.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey are, then, as hard-hearted as these people? I might have known.\r\nNeither earth, sky, nor water shows charity to a jackal. I saw the tents\r\nof a white-face last season, after the Rains, and I also took a new\r\nyellow bridle to eat. The white-faces do not dress their leather in the\r\nproper way. It made me very sick.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat was better than my case,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Adjutant. \xe2\x80\x9cWhen I was in my\r\nthird season, a young and a bold bird, I went down to the river where\r\nthe big boats come in. The boats of the English are thrice as big as\r\nthis village.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe has been as far as Delhi, and says all the people there walk on\r\ntheir heads,\xe2\x80\x9d muttered the Jackal. The Mugger opened his left eye, and\r\nlooked keenly at the Adjutant.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is true,\xe2\x80\x9d the big bird insisted. \xe2\x80\x9cA liar only lies when he hopes\r\nto be believed. No one who had not seen those boats COULD believe this\r\ntruth.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTHAT is more reasonable,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Mugger. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd then?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFrom the insides of this boat they were taking out great pieces of\r\nwhite stuff, which, in a little while, turned to water. Much split off,\r\nand fell about on the shore, and the rest they swiftly put into a house\r\nwith thick walls. But a boatman, who laughed, took a piece no larger\r\nthan a small dog, and threw it to me. I--all my people--swallow without\r\nreflection, and that piece I swallowed as is our custom. Immediately I\r\nwas afflicted with an excessive cold which, beginning in my crop, ran\r\ndown to the extreme end of my toes, and deprived me even of speech,\r\nwhile the boatmen laughed at me. Never have I felt such cold. I danced\r\nin my grief and amazement till I could recover my breath and then\r\nI danced and cried out against the falseness of this world; and the\r\nboatmen derided me till they fell down. The chief wonder of the matter,\r\nsetting aside that marvellous coldness, was that there was nothing at\r\nall in my crop when I had finished my lamentings!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe Adjutant had done his very best to describe his feelings after\r\nswallowing a seven-pound lump of Wenham Lake ice, off an American\r\nice-ship, in the days before Calcutta made her ice by machinery; but\r\nas he did not know what ice was, and as the Mugger and the Jackal knew\r\nrather less, the tale missed fire.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnything,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Mugger, shutting his left eye again--\xe2\x80\x9cANYTHING is\r\npossible that comes out of a boat thrice the size of Mugger-Ghaut. My\r\nvillage is not a small one.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThere was a whistle overhead on the bridge, and the Delhi Mail\r\nslid across, all the carriages gleaming with light, and the shadows\r\nfaithfully following along the river. It clanked away into the dark\r\nagain; but the Mugger and the Jackal were so well used to it that they\r\nnever turned their heads.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs that anything less wonderful than a boat thrice the size of\r\nMugger-Ghaut?\xe2\x80\x9d said the bird, looking up.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI saw that built, child. Stone by stone I saw the bridge-piers rise,\r\nand when the men fell off (they were wondrous sure-footed for the most\r\npart--but WHEN they fell) I was ready. After the first pier was made\r\nthey never thought to look down the stream for the body to burn. There,\r\nagain, I saved much trouble. There was nothing strange in the building\r\nof the bridge,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Mugger.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut that which goes across, pulling the roofed carts! That is strange,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n the Adjutant repeated. \xe2\x80\x9cIt is, past any doubt, a new breed of bullock.\r\nSome day it will not be able to keep its foothold up yonder, and will\r\nfall as the men did. The old Mugger will then be ready.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe Jackal looked at the Adjutant and the Adjutant looked at the Jackal.\r\nIf there was one thing they were more certain of than another, it was\r\nthat the engine was everything in the wide world except a bullock. The\r\nJackal had watched it time and again from the aloe hedges by the side of\r\nthe line, and the Adjutant had seen engines since the first locomotive\r\nran in India. But the Mugger had only looked up at the thing from below,\r\nwhere the brass dome seemed rather like a bullock\xe2\x80\x99s hump.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cM--yes, a new kind of bullock,\xe2\x80\x9d the Mugger repeated ponderously,\r\nto make himself quite sure in his own mind; and \xe2\x80\x9cCertainly it is a\r\nbullock,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Jackal.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd again it might be----\xe2\x80\x9d began the Mugger pettishly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCertainly--most certainly,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Jackal, without waiting for the\r\nother to finish.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat?\xe2\x80\x9d said the Mugger angrily, for he could feel that the others knew\r\nmore than he did. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat might it be? _I_ never finished my words. You\r\nsaid it was a bullock.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is anything the Protector of the Poor pleases. I am HIS servant--not\r\nthe servant of the thing that crosses the river.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhatever it is, it is white-face work,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Adjutant; \xe2\x80\x9cand for my\r\nown part, I would not lie out upon a place so near to it as this bar.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou do not know the English as I do,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Mugger. \xe2\x80\x9cThere was a\r\nwhite-face here when the bridge was built, and he would take a boat\r\nin the evenings and shuffle with his feet on the bottom-boards, and\r\nwhisper: \xe2\x80\x98Is he here? Is he there? Bring me my gun.\xe2\x80\x99 I could hear him\r\nbefore I could see him--each sound that he made--creaking and puffing\r\nand rattling his gun, up and down the river. As surely as I had picked\r\nup one of his workmen, and thus saved great expense in wood for the\r\nburning, so surely would he come down to the Ghaut, and shout in a loud\r\nvoice that he would hunt me, and rid the river of me--the Mugger of\r\nMugger-Ghaut! ME! Children, I have swum under the bottom of his boat for\r\nhour after hour, and heard him fire his gun at logs; and when I was well\r\nsure he was wearied, I have risen by his side and snapped my jaws in his\r\nface. When the bridge was finished he went away. All the English hunt in\r\nthat fashion, except when they are hunted.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWho hunts the white-faces?\xe2\x80\x9d yapped the Jackal excitedly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo one now, but I have hunted them in my time.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI remember a little of that Hunting. I was young then,\xe2\x80\x9d said the\r\nAdjutant, clattering his beak significantly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI was well established here. My village was being builded for the third\r\ntime, as I remember, when my cousin, the Gavial, brought me word of rich\r\nwaters above Benares. At first I would not go, for my cousin, who is a\r\nfish-eater, does not always know the good from the bad; but I heard my\r\npeople talking in the evenings, and what they said made me certain.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd what did they say?\xe2\x80\x9d the Jackal asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey said enough to make me, the Mugger of Mugger-Ghaut, leave water\r\nand take to my feet. I went by night, using the littlest streams as they\r\nserved me; but it was the beginning of the hot weather, and all streams\r\nwere low. I crossed dusty roads; I went through tall grass; I climbed\r\nhills in the moonlight. Even rocks did I climb, children--consider this\r\nwell. I crossed the tail of Sirhind, the waterless, before I could\r\nfind the set of the little rivers that flow Gungaward. I was a month\xe2\x80\x99s\r\njourney from my own people and the river that I knew. That was very\r\nmarvellous!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat food on the way?\xe2\x80\x9d said the Jackal, who kept his soul in his little\r\nstomach, and was not a bit impressed by the Mugger\xe2\x80\x99s land travels.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat which I could find--COUSIN,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Mugger slowly, dragging each\r\nword.\r\n\r\nNow you do not call a man a cousin in India unless you think you can\r\nestablish some kind of blood-relationship, and as it is only in old\r\nfairy-tales that the Mugger ever marries a jackal, the Jackal knew for\r\nwhat reason he had been suddenly lifted into the Mugger\xe2\x80\x99s family circle.\r\nIf they had been alone he would not have cared, but the Adjutant\xe2\x80\x99s eyes\r\ntwinkled with mirth at the ugly jest.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAssuredly, Father, I might have known,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Jackal. A mugger\r\ndoes not care to be called a father of jackals, and the Mugger of\r\nMugger-Ghaut said as much--and a great deal more which there is no use\r\nin repeating here.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe Protector of the Poor has claimed kinship. How can I remember the\r\nprecise degree? Moreover, we eat the same food. He has said it,\xe2\x80\x9d was the\r\nJackal\xe2\x80\x99s reply.\r\n\r\nThat made matters rather worse, for what the Jackal hinted at was that\r\nthe Mugger must have eaten his food on that land-march fresh and fresh\r\nevery day, instead of keeping it by him till it was in a fit and proper\r\ncondition, as every self-respecting mugger and most wild beasts do when\r\nthey can. Indeed, one of the worst terms of contempt along the River-bed\r\nis \xe2\x80\x9ceater of fresh meat.\xe2\x80\x9d It is nearly as bad as calling a man a\r\ncannibal.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat food was eaten thirty seasons ago,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Adjutant quietly. \xe2\x80\x9cIf\r\nwe talk for thirty seasons more it will never come back. Tell us, now,\r\nwhat happened when the good waters were reached after thy most wonderful\r\nland journey. If we listened to the howling of every jackal the business\r\nof the town would stop, as the saying is.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe Mugger must have been grateful for the interruption, because he went\r\non, with a rush:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBy the Right and Left of Gunga! when I came there never did I see such\r\nwaters!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWere they better, then, than the big flood of last season?\xe2\x80\x9d said the\r\nJackal.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBetter! That flood was no more than comes every five years--a handful\r\nof drowned strangers, some chickens, and a dead bullock in muddy water\r\nwith cross-currents. But the season I think of, the river was low,\r\nsmooth, and even, and, as the Gavial had warned me, the dead English\r\ncame down, touching each other. I got my girth in that season--my\r\ngirth and my depth. From Agra, by Etawah and the broad waters by\r\nAllahabad----\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, the eddy that set under the walls of the fort at Allahabad!\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nthe Adjutant. \xe2\x80\x9cThey came in there like widgeon to the reeds, and round\r\nand round they swung--thus!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe went off into his horrible dance again, while the Jackal looked on\r\nenviously. He naturally could not remember the terrible year of the\r\nMutiny they were talking about. The Mugger continued:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, by Allahabad one lay still in the slack-water and let twenty go\r\nby to pick one; and, above all, the English were not cumbered with\r\njewellery and nose-rings and anklets as my women are nowadays. To\r\ndelight in ornaments is to end with a rope for a necklace, as the saying\r\nis. All the muggers of all the rivers grew fat then, but it was my Fate\r\nto be fatter than them all. The news was that the English were being\r\nhunted into the rivers, and by the Right and Left of Gunga! we believed\r\nit was true. So far as I went south I believed it to be true; and I went\r\ndown-stream beyond Monghyr and the tombs that look over the river.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI know that place,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Adjutant. \xe2\x80\x9cSince those days Monghyr is a\r\nlost city. Very few live there now.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThereafter I worked up-stream very slowly and lazily, and a little\r\nabove Monghyr there came down a boatful of white-faces--alive! They\r\nwere, as I remember, women, lying under a cloth spread over sticks, and\r\ncrying aloud. There was never a gun fired at us, the watchers of the\r\nfords in those days. All the guns were busy elsewhere. We could hear\r\nthem day and night inland, coming and going as the wind shifted. I rose\r\nup full before the boat, because I had never seen white-faces alive,\r\nthough I knew them well--otherwise. A naked white child kneeled by the\r\nside of the boat, and, stooping over, must needs try to trail his hands\r\nin the river. It is a pretty thing to see how a child loves running\r\nwater. I had fed that day, but there was yet a little unfilled space\r\nwithin me. Still, it was for sport and not for food that I rose at the\r\nchild\xe2\x80\x99s hands. They were so clear a mark that I did not even look when I\r\nclosed; but they were so small that though my jaws rang true--I am sure\r\nof that--the child drew them up swiftly, unhurt. They must have passed\r\nbetween tooth and tooth--those small white hands. I should have caught\r\nhim cross-wise at the elbows; but, as I said, it was only for sport and\r\ndesire to see new things that I rose at all. They cried out one after\r\nanother in the boat, and presently I rose again to watch them. The boat\r\nwas too heavy to push over. They were only women, but he who trusts\r\na woman will walk on duckweed in a pool, as the saying is: and by the\r\nRight and Left of Gunga, that is truth!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOnce a woman gave me some dried skin from a fish,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Jackal. \xe2\x80\x9cI\r\nhad hoped to get her baby, but horse-food is better than the kick of a\r\nhorse, as the saying is. What did thy woman do?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe fired at me with a short gun of a kind I have never seen before or\r\nsince. Five times, one after another\xe2\x80\x9d (the Mugger must have met with an\r\nold-fashioned revolver); \xe2\x80\x9cand I stayed open-mouthed and gaping, my head\r\nin the smoke. Never did I see such a thing. Five times, as swiftly as I\r\nwave my tail--thus!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe Jackal, who had been growing more and more interested in the story,\r\nhad just time to leap back as the huge tail swung by like a scythe.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot before the fifth shot,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Mugger, as though he had never\r\ndreamed of stunning one of his listeners--\xe2\x80\x9cnot before the fifth shot\r\ndid I sink, and I rose in time to hear a boatman telling all those\r\nwhite women that I was most certainly dead. One bullet had gone under\r\na neck-plate of mine. I know not if it is there still, for the reason I\r\ncannot turn my head. Look and see, child. It will show that my tale is\r\ntrue.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI?\xe2\x80\x9d said the Jackal. \xe2\x80\x9cShall an eater of old shoes, a bone-cracker,\r\npresume, to doubt the word of the Envy of the River? May my tail be\r\nbitten off by blind puppies if the shadow of such a thought has crossed\r\nmy humble mind! The Protector of the Poor has condescended to inform me,\r\nhis slave, that once in his life he has been wounded by a woman. That is\r\nsufficient, and I will tell the tale to all my children, asking for no\r\nproof.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOver-much civility is sometimes no better than over-much discourtesy,\r\nfor, as the saying is, one can choke a guest with curds. I do NOT desire\r\nthat any children of thine should know that the Mugger of Mugger-Ghaut\r\ntook his only wound from a woman. They will have much else to think of\r\nif they get their meat as miserably as does their father.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is forgotten long ago! It was never said! There never was a white\r\nwoman! There was no boat! Nothing whatever happened at all.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe Jackal waved his brush to show how completely everything was wiped\r\nout of his memory, and sat down with an air.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIndeed, very many things happened,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Mugger, beaten in his\r\nsecond attempt that night to get the better of his friend. (Neither bore\r\nmalice, however. Eat and be eaten was fair law along the river, and the\r\nJackal came in for his share of plunder when the Mugger had finished\r\na meal.) \xe2\x80\x9cI left that boat and went up-stream, and, when I had reached\r\nArrah and the back-waters behind it, there were no more dead English.\r\nThe river was empty for a while. Then came one or two dead, in red\r\ncoats, not English, but of one kind all--Hindus and Purbeeahs--then five\r\nand six abreast, and at last, from Arrah to the North beyond Agra, it\r\nwas as though whole villages had walked into the water. They came out\r\nof little creeks one after another, as the logs come down in the Rains.\r\nWhen the river rose they rose also in companies from the shoals they\r\nhad rested upon; and the falling flood dragged them with it across the\r\nfields and through the Jungle by the long hair. All night, too, going\r\nNorth, I heard the guns, and by day the shod feet of men crossing fords,\r\nand that noise which a heavy cart-wheel makes on sand under water; and\r\nevery ripple brought more dead. At last even I was afraid, for I said:\r\n\xe2\x80\x98If this thing happen to men, how shall the Mugger of Mugger-Ghaut\r\nescape?\xe2\x80\x99 There were boats, too, that came up behind me without sails,\r\nburning continually, as the cotton-boats sometimes burn, but never\r\nsinking.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh!\xe2\x80\x9d said the Adjutant. \xe2\x80\x9cBoats like those come to Calcutta of the\r\nSouth. They are tall and black, they beat up the water behind them with\r\na tail, and they----\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAre thrice as big as my village. MY boats were low and white; they beat\r\nup the water on either side of them and were no larger than the boats\r\nof one who speaks truth should be. They made me very afraid, and I\r\nleft water and went back to this my river, hiding by day and walking\r\nby night, when I could not find little streams to help me. I came to\r\nmy village again, but I did not hope to see any of my people there.\r\nYet they were ploughing and sowing and reaping, and going to and fro in\r\ntheir fields, as quietly as their own cattle.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWas there still good food in the river?\xe2\x80\x9d said the Jackal.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMore than I had any desire for. Even I--and I do not eat mud--even\r\nI was tired, and, as I remember, a little frightened of this constant\r\ncoming down of the silent ones. I heard my people say in my village\r\nthat all the English were dead; but those that came, face down, with the\r\ncurrent were NOT English, as my people saw. Then my people said that it\r\nwas best to say nothing at all, but to pay the tax and plough the land.\r\nAfter a long time the river cleared, and those that came down it had\r\nbeen clearly drowned by the floods, as I could well see; and though it\r\nwas not so easy then to get food, I was heartily glad of it. A little\r\nkilling here and there is no bad thing--but even the Mugger is sometimes\r\nsatisfied, as the saying is.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMarvellous! Most truly marvellous!\xe2\x80\x9d said the Jackal. \xe2\x80\x9cI am become fat\r\nthrough merely hearing about so much good eating. And afterward what, if\r\nit be permitted to ask, did the Protector of the Poor do?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI said to myself--and by the Right and Left of Gunga! I locked my jaws\r\non that vow--I said I would never go roving any more. So I lived by the\r\nGhaut, very close to my own people, and I watched over them year after\r\nyear; and they loved me so much that they threw marigold wreaths at my\r\nhead whenever they saw it lift. Yes, and my Fate has been very kind to\r\nme, and the river is good enough to respect my poor and infirm presence;\r\nonly----\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo one is all happy from his beak to his tail,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Adjutant\r\nsympathetically. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat does the Mugger of Mugger-Ghaut need more?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat little white child which I did not get,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Mugger, with a\r\ndeep sigh. \xe2\x80\x9cHe was very small, but I have not forgotten. I am old now,\r\nbut before I die it is my desire to try one new thing. It is true they\r\nare a heavy-footed, noisy, and foolish people, and the sport would be\r\nsmall, but I remember the old days above Benares, and, if the child\r\nlives, he will remember still. It may be he goes up and down the bank\r\nof some river, telling how he once passed his hands between the teeth of\r\nthe Mugger of Mugger-Ghaut, and lived to make a tale of it. My Fate has\r\nbeen very kind, but that plagues me sometimes in my dreams--the thought\r\nof the little white child in the bows of that boat.\xe2\x80\x9d He yawned, and\r\nclosed his jaws. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd now I will rest and think. Keep silent, my\r\nchildren, and respect the aged.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe turned stiffly, and shuffled to the top of the sand-bar, while the\r\nJackal drew back with the Adjutant to the shelter of a tree stranded on\r\nthe end nearest the railway bridge.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat was a pleasant and profitable life,\xe2\x80\x9d he grinned, looking up\r\ninquiringly at the bird who towered above him. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd not once, mark you,\r\ndid he think fit to tell me where a morsel might have been left along\r\nthe banks. Yet I have told HIM a hundred times of good things wallowing\r\ndown-stream. How true is the saying, \xe2\x80\x98All the world forgets the Jackal\r\nand the Barber when the news has been told!\xe2\x80\x99 Now he is going to sleep!\r\nArrh!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow can a jackal hunt with a Mugger?\xe2\x80\x9d said the Adjutant coolly. \xe2\x80\x9cBig\r\nthief and little thief; it is easy to say who gets the pickings.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe Jackal turned, whining impatiently, and was going to curl himself\r\nup under the tree-trunk, when suddenly he cowered, and looked up through\r\nthe draggled branches at the bridge almost above his head.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat now?\xe2\x80\x9d said the Adjutant, opening his wings uneasily.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWait till we see. The wind blows from us to them, but they are not\r\nlooking for us--those two men.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMen, is it? My office protects me. All India knows I am holy.\xe2\x80\x9d The\r\nAdjutant, being a first-class scavenger, is allowed to go where he\r\npleases, and so this one never flinched.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am not worth a blow from anything better than an old shoe,\xe2\x80\x9d said the\r\nJackal, and listened again. \xe2\x80\x9cHark to that footfall!\xe2\x80\x9d he went on. \xe2\x80\x9cThat\r\nwas no country leather, but the shod foot of a white-face. Listen\r\nagain! Iron hits iron up there! It is a gun! Friend, those heavy-footed,\r\nfoolish English are coming to speak with the Mugger.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWarn him, then. He was called Protector of the Poor by some one not\r\nunlike a starving Jackal but a little time ago.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLet my cousin protect his own hide. He has told me again and again\r\nthere is nothing to fear from the white-faces. They must be white-faces.\r\nNot a villager of Mugger-Ghaut would dare to come after him. See, I said\r\nit was a gun! Now, with good luck, we shall feed before daylight. He\r\ncannot hear well out of water, and--this time it is not a woman!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nA shiny barrel glittered for a minute in the moonlight on the girders.\r\nThe Mugger was lying on the sand-bar as still as his own shadow, his\r\nfore-feet spread out a little, his head dropped between them, snoring\r\nlike a--mugger.\r\n\r\nA voice on the bridge whispered: \xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s an odd shot--straight down\r\nalmost--but as safe as houses. Better try behind the neck. Golly! what\r\na brute! The villagers will be wild if he\xe2\x80\x99s shot, though. He\xe2\x80\x99s the deota\r\n[godling] of these parts.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t care a rap,\xe2\x80\x9d another voice answered; \xe2\x80\x9che took about fifteen of my\r\nbest coolies while the bridge was building, and it\xe2\x80\x99s time he was put\r\na stop to. I\xe2\x80\x99ve been after him in a boat for weeks. Stand by with the\r\nMartini as soon as I\xe2\x80\x99ve given him both barrels of this.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMind the kick, then. A double four-bore\xe2\x80\x99s no joke.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s for him to decide. Here goes!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThere was a roar like the sound of a small cannon (the biggest sort of\r\nelephant-rifle is not very different from some artillery), and a double\r\nstreak of flame, followed by the stinging crack of a Martini, whose long\r\nbullet makes nothing of a crocodile\xe2\x80\x99s plates. But the explosive bullets\r\ndid the work. One of them struck just behind the Mugger\xe2\x80\x99s neck, a\r\nhand\xe2\x80\x99s-breadth to the left of the backbone, while the other burst a\r\nlittle lower down, at the beginning of the tail. In ninety-nine cases\r\nout of a hundred a mortally-wounded crocodile can scramble to deep water\r\nand get away; but the Mugger of Mugger-Ghaut was literally broken into\r\nthree pieces. He hardly moved his head before the life went out of him,\r\nand he lay as flat as the Jackal.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThunder and lightning! Lightning and thunder!\xe2\x80\x9d said that miserable\r\nlittle beast. \xe2\x80\x9cHas the thing that pulls the covered carts over the\r\nbridge tumbled at last?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is no more than a gun,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Adjutant, though his very\r\ntail-feathers quivered. \xe2\x80\x9cNothing more than a gun. He is certainly dead.\r\nHere come the white-faces.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe two Englishmen had hurried down from the bridge and across to the\r\nsand-bar, where they stood admiring the length of the Mugger. Then a\r\nnative with an axe cut off the big head, and four men dragged it across\r\nthe spit.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe last time that I had my hand in a Mugger\xe2\x80\x99s mouth,\xe2\x80\x9d said one of the\r\nEnglishmen, stooping down (he was the man who had built the bridge), \xe2\x80\x9cit\r\nwas when I was about five years old--coming down the river by boat to\r\nMonghyr. I was a Mutiny baby, as they call it. Poor mother was in the\r\nboat, too, and she often told me how she fired dad\xe2\x80\x99s old pistol at the\r\nbeast\xe2\x80\x99s head.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, you\xe2\x80\x99ve certainly had your revenge on the chief of the clan--even\r\nif the gun has made your nose bleed. Hi, you boatmen! Haul that head up\r\nthe bank, and we\xe2\x80\x99ll boil it for the skull. The skin\xe2\x80\x99s too knocked about\r\nto keep. Come along to bed now. This was worth sitting up all night for,\r\nwasn\xe2\x80\x99t it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n*****\r\n\r\nCuriously enough, the Jackal and the Adjutant made the very same remark\r\nnot three minutes after the men had left.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nA RIPPLE SONG\r\n\r\n     Once a ripple came to land\r\n       In the golden sunset burning--\r\n     Lapped against a maiden\xe2\x80\x99s hand,\r\n       By the ford returning.\r\n\r\n     Dainty foot and gentle breast--\r\n       Here, across, be glad and rest.\r\n     \xe2\x80\x9cMaiden, wait,\xe2\x80\x9d the ripple saith.\r\n       \xe2\x80\x9cWait awhile, for I am Death!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n     \xe2\x80\x9cWhere my lover calls I go--\r\n       Shame it were to treat him coldly--\r\n     \xe2\x80\x98Twas a fish that circled so,\r\n       Turning over boldly.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n     Dainty foot and tender heart,\r\n       Wait the loaded ferry-cart.\r\n     \xe2\x80\x9cWait, ah, wait!\xe2\x80\x9d the ripple saith;\r\n       \xe2\x80\x9cMaiden, wait, for I am Death!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n     \xe2\x80\x9cWhen my lover calls I haste--\r\n       Dame Disdain was never wedded!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n      Ripple-ripple round her waist,\r\n       Clear the current eddied.\r\n\r\n     Foolish heart and faithful hand,\r\n       Little feet that touched no land.\r\n     Far away the ripple sped,\r\n       Ripple--ripple--running red!\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nTHE KING\xe2\x80\x99S ANKUS\r\n\r\n     These are the Four that are never content, that have never\r\n         been filled since the Dews began--\r\n     Jacala\xe2\x80\x99s mouth, and the glut of the Kite, and the hands of the\r\n         Ape, and the Eyes of Man.\r\n                                 Jungle Saying.\r\n\r\nKaa, the big Rock Python, had changed his skin for perhaps the\r\ntwo-hundredth time since his birth; and Mowgli, who never forgot that\r\nhe owed his life to Kaa for a night\xe2\x80\x99s work at Cold Lairs, which you may\r\nperhaps remember, went to congratulate him. Skin-changing always makes\r\na snake moody and depressed till the new skin begins to shine and look\r\nbeautiful. Kaa never made fun of Mowgli any more, but accepted him, as\r\nthe other Jungle People did, for the Master of the Jungle, and brought\r\nhim all the news that a python of his size would naturally hear. What\r\nKaa did not know about the Middle Jungle, as they call it,--the life\r\nthat runs close to the earth or under it, the boulder, burrow, and\r\nthe tree-bole life,--might have been written upon the smallest of his\r\nscales.\r\n\r\nThat afternoon Mowgli was sitting in the circle of Kaa\xe2\x80\x99s great coils,\r\nfingering the flaked and broken old skin that lay all looped and twisted\r\namong the rocks just as Kaa had left it. Kaa had very courteously packed\r\nhimself under Mowgli\xe2\x80\x99s broad, bare shoulders, so that the boy was really\r\nresting in a living arm-chair.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cEven to the scales of the eyes it is perfect,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mowgli, under his\r\nbreath, playing with the old skin. \xe2\x80\x9cStrange to see the covering of one\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nown head at one\xe2\x80\x99s own feet!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAy, but I lack feet,\xe2\x80\x9d said Kaa; \xe2\x80\x9cand since this is the custom of all\r\nmy people, I do not find it strange. Does thy skin never feel old and\r\nharsh?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen go I and wash, Flathead; but, it is true, in the great heats I\r\nhave wished I could slough my skin without pain, and run skinless.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI wash, and ALSO I take off my skin. How looks the new coat?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMowgli ran his hand down the diagonal checkerings of the immense back.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe Turtle is harder-backed, but not so gay,\xe2\x80\x9d he said judgmatically.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe Frog, my name-bearer, is more gay, but not so hard. It is very\r\nbeautiful to see--like the mottling in the mouth of a lily.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt needs water. A new skin never comes to full colour before the first\r\nbath. Let us go bathe.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI will carry thee,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mowgli; and he stooped down, laughing, to\r\nlift the middle section of Kaa\xe2\x80\x99s great body, just where the barrel was\r\nthickest. A man might just, as well have tried to heave up a two-foot\r\nwater-main; and Kaa lay still, puffing with quiet amusement. Then the\r\nregular evening game began--the Boy in the flush of his great strength,\r\nand the Python in his sumptuous new skin, standing up one against the\r\nother for a wrestling match--a trial of eye and strength. Of course,\r\nKaa could have crushed a dozen Mowglis if he had let himself go; but he\r\nplayed carefully, and never loosed one-tenth of his power. Ever since\r\nMowgli was strong enough to endure a little rough handling, Kaa had\r\ntaught him this game, and it suppled his limbs as nothing else could.\r\nSometimes Mowgli would stand lapped almost to his throat in Kaa\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nshifting coils, striving to get one arm free and catch him by\r\nthe throat. Then Kaa would give way limply, and Mowgli, with both\r\nquick-moving feet, would try to cramp the purchase of that huge tail as\r\nit flung backward feeling for a rock or a stump. They would rock to\r\nand fro, head to head, each waiting for his chance, till the beautiful,\r\nstatue-like group melted in a whirl of black-and-yellow coils and\r\nstruggling legs and arms, to rise up again and again. \xe2\x80\x9cNow! now! now!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n said Kaa, making feints with his head that even Mowgli\xe2\x80\x99s quick hand\r\ncould not turn aside. \xe2\x80\x9cLook! I touch thee here, Little Brother! Here,\r\nand here! Are thy hands numb? Here again!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe game always ended in one way--with a straight, driving blow of the\r\nhead that knocked the boy over and over. Mowgli could never learn the\r\nguard for that lightning lunge, and, as Kaa said, there was not the\r\nleast use in trying.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGood hunting!\xe2\x80\x9d Kaa grunted at last; and Mowgli, as usual, was shot away\r\nhalf a dozen yards, gasping and laughing. He rose with his fingers full\r\nof grass, and followed Kaa to the wise snake\xe2\x80\x99s pet bathing-place--a\r\ndeep, pitchy-black pool surrounded with rocks, and made interesting by\r\nsunken tree-stumps. The boy slipped in, Jungle-fashion, without a sound,\r\nand dived across; rose, too, without a sound, and turned on his back,\r\nhis arms behind his head, watching the moon rising above the rocks,\r\nand breaking up her reflection in the water with his toes. Kaa\xe2\x80\x99s\r\ndiamond-shaped head cut the pool like a razor, and came out to rest\r\non Mowgli\xe2\x80\x99s shoulder. They lay still, soaking luxuriously in the cool\r\nwater.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is VERY good,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mowgli at last, sleepily. \xe2\x80\x9cNow, in the Man-Pack,\r\nat this hour, as I remember, they laid them down upon hard pieces of\r\nwood in the inside of a mud-trap, and, having carefully shut out all the\r\nclean winds, drew foul cloth over their heavy heads and made evil songs\r\nthrough their noses. It is better in the Jungle.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nA hurrying cobra slipped down over a rock and drank, gave them \xe2\x80\x9cGood\r\nhunting!\xe2\x80\x9d and went away.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSssh!\xe2\x80\x9d said Kaa, as though he had suddenly remembered something. \xe2\x80\x9cSo\r\nthe Jungle gives thee all that thou hast ever desired, Little Brother?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot all,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mowgli, laughing; \xe2\x80\x9celse there would be a new and strong\r\nShere Khan to kill once a moon. Now, I could kill with my own hands,\r\nasking no help of buffaloes. And also I have wished the sun to shine in\r\nthe middle of the Rains, and the Rains to cover the sun in the deep of\r\nsummer; and also I have never gone empty but I wished that I had killed\r\na goat; and also I have never killed a goat but I wished it had been\r\nbuck; nor buck but I wished it had been nilghai. But thus do we feel,\r\nall of us.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThou hast no other desire?\xe2\x80\x9d the big snake demanded.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat more can I wish? I have the Jungle, and the favour of the Jungle!\r\nIs there more anywhere between sunrise and sunset?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow, the Cobra said----\xe2\x80\x9d Kaa began. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat cobra? He that went away just\r\nnow said nothing. He was hunting.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt was another.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHast thou many dealings with the Poison People? I give them their own\r\npath. They carry death in the fore-tooth, and that is not good--for they\r\nare so small. But what hood is this thou hast spoken with?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nKaa rolled slowly in the water like a steamer in a beam sea. \xe2\x80\x9cThree or\r\nfour moons since,\xe2\x80\x9d said he, \xe2\x80\x9cI hunted in Cold Lairs, which place thou\r\nhast not forgotten. And the thing I hunted fled shrieking past the tanks\r\nand to that house whose side I once broke for thy sake, and ran into the\r\nground.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut the people of Cold Lairs do not live in burrows.\xe2\x80\x9d Mowgli knew that\r\nKaa was telling of the Monkey People.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThis thing was not living, but seeking to live,\xe2\x80\x9d Kaa replied, with\r\na quiver of his tongue. \xe2\x80\x9cHe ran into a burrow that led very far. I\r\nfollowed, and having killed, I slept. When I waked I went forward.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cUnder the earth?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cEven so, coming at last upon a White Hood [a white cobra], who spoke of\r\nthings beyond my knowledge, and showed me many things I had never before\r\nseen.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNew game? Was it good hunting?\xe2\x80\x9d Mowgli turned quickly on his side.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt was no game, and would have broken all my teeth; but the White Hood\r\nsaid that a man--he spoke as one that knew the breed--that a man would\r\ngive the breath under his ribs for only the sight of those things.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe will look,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mowgli. \xe2\x80\x9cI now remember that I was once a man.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSlowly--slowly. It was haste killed the Yellow Snake that ate the sun.\r\nWe two spoke together under the earth, and I spoke of thee, naming thee\r\nas a man. Said the White Hood (and he is indeed as old as the Jungle):\r\n\xe2\x80\x98It is long since I have seen a man. Let him come, and he shall see all\r\nthese things, for the least of which very many men would die.\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat MUST be new game. And yet the Poison People do not tell us when\r\ngame is afoot. They are an unfriendly folk.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is NOT game. It is--it is--I cannot say what it is.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe will go there. I have never seen a White Hood, and I wish to see the\r\nother things. Did he kill them?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey are all dead things. He says he is the keeper of them all.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh! As a wolf stands above meat he has taken to his own lair. Let us\r\ngo.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMowgli swam to bank, rolled on the grass to dry himself, and the two\r\nset off for Cold Lairs, the deserted city of which you may have heard.\r\nMowgli was not the least afraid of the Monkey People in those days,\r\nbut the Monkey People had the liveliest horror of Mowgli. Their tribes,\r\nhowever, were raiding in the Jungle, and so Cold Lairs stood empty and\r\nsilent in the moonlight. Kaa led up to the ruins of the queens\xe2\x80\x99 pavilion\r\nthat stood on the terrace, slipped over the rubbish, and dived down\r\nthe half-choked staircase that went underground from the centre of\r\nthe pavilion. Mowgli gave the snake-call,--\xe2\x80\x9cWe be of one blood, ye and\r\nI,\xe2\x80\x9d--and followed on his hands and knees. They crawled a long distance\r\ndown a sloping passage that turned and twisted several times, and at\r\nlast came to where the root of some great tree, growing thirty feet\r\noverhead, had forced out a solid stone in the wall. They crept through\r\nthe gap, and found themselves in a large vault, whose domed roof had\r\nbeen also broken away by tree-roots so that a few streaks of light\r\ndropped down into the darkness.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA safe lair,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mowgli, rising to his firm feet, \xe2\x80\x9cbut over-far to\r\nvisit daily. And now what do we see?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAm I nothing?\xe2\x80\x9d said a voice in the middle of the vault; and Mowgli saw\r\nsomething white move till, little by little, there stood up the hugest\r\ncobra he had ever set eyes on--a creature nearly eight feet long,\r\nand bleached by being in darkness to an old ivory-white. Even the\r\nspectacle-marks of his spread hood had faded to faint yellow. His eyes\r\nwere as red as rubies, and altogether he was most wonderful.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGood hunting!\xe2\x80\x9d said Mowgli, who carried his manners with his knife, and\r\nthat never left him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat of the city?\xe2\x80\x9d said the White Cobra, without answering the\r\ngreeting. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat of the great, the walled city--the city of a hundred\r\nelephants and twenty thousand horses, and cattle past counting--the city\r\nof the King of Twenty Kings? I grow deaf here, and it is long since I\r\nheard their war-gongs.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe Jungle is above our heads,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mowgli. \xe2\x80\x9cI know only Hathi and his\r\nsons among elephants. Bagheera has slain all the horses in one village,\r\nand--what is a King?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI told thee,\xe2\x80\x9d said Kaa softly to the Cobra,--\xe2\x80\x9cI told thee, four moons\r\nago, that thy city was not.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe city--the great city of the forest whose gates are guarded by the\r\nKing\xe2\x80\x99s towers--can never pass. They builded it before my father\xe2\x80\x99s father\r\ncame from the egg, and it shall endure when my son\xe2\x80\x99s sons are as white\r\nas I! Salomdhi, son of Chandrabija, son of Viyeja, son of Yegasuri, made\r\nit in the days of Bappa Rawal. Whose cattle are YE?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is a lost trail,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mowgli, turning to Kaa. \xe2\x80\x9cI know not his\r\ntalk.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNor I. He is very old. Father of Cobras, there is only the Jungle here,\r\nas it has been since the beginning.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen who is HE,\xe2\x80\x9d said the White Cobra, \xe2\x80\x9csitting down before me,\r\nunafraid, knowing not the name of the King, talking our talk through a\r\nman\xe2\x80\x99s lips? Who is he with the knife and the snake\xe2\x80\x99s tongue?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMowgli they call me,\xe2\x80\x9d was the answer. \xe2\x80\x9cI am of the Jungle. The wolves\r\nare my people, and Kaa here is my brother. Father of Cobras, who art\r\nthou?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am the Warden of the King\xe2\x80\x99s Treasure. Kurrun Raja builded the stone\r\nabove me, in the days when my skin was dark, that I might teach death\r\nto those who came to steal. Then they let down the treasure through the\r\nstone, and I heard the song of the Brahmins my masters.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cUmm!\xe2\x80\x9d said Mowgli to himself. \xe2\x80\x9cI have dealt with one Brahmin already,\r\nin the Man-Pack, and--I know what I know. Evil comes here in a little.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFive times since I came here has the stone been lifted, but always to\r\nlet down more, and never to take away. There are no riches like these\r\nriches--the treasures of a hundred kings. But it is long and long since\r\nthe stone was last moved, and I think that my city has forgotten.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere is no city. Look up. Yonder are roots of the great trees tearing\r\nthe stones apart. Trees and men do not grow together,\xe2\x80\x9d Kaa insisted.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTwice and thrice have men found their way here,\xe2\x80\x9d the White Cobra\r\nanswered savagely; \xe2\x80\x9cbut they never spoke till I came upon them groping\r\nin the dark, and then they cried only a little time. But ye come with\r\nlies, Man and Snake both, and would have me believe the city is not, and\r\nthat my wardship ends. Little do men change in the years. But I change\r\nnever! Till the stone is lifted, and the Brahmins come down singing the\r\nsongs that I know, and feed me with warm milk, and take me to the light\r\nagain, I--I--_I_, and no other, am the Warden of the King\xe2\x80\x99s Treasure!\r\nThe city is dead, ye say, and here are the roots of the trees? Stoop\r\ndown, then, and take what ye will. Earth has no treasure like to these.\r\nMan with the snake\xe2\x80\x99s tongue, if thou canst go alive by the way that thou\r\nhast entered it, the lesser Kings will be thy servants!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAgain the trail is lost,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mowgli coolly. \xe2\x80\x9cCan any jackal have\r\nburrowed so deep and bitten this great White Hood? He is surely mad.\r\nFather of Cobras, I see nothing here to take away.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBy the Gods of the Sun and Moon, it is the madness of death upon the\r\nboy!\xe2\x80\x9d hissed the Cobra. \xe2\x80\x9cBefore thine eyes close I will allow thee this\r\nfavour. Look thou, and see what man has never seen before!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey do not well in the Jungle who speak to Mowgli of favours,\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nthe boy, between his teeth; \xe2\x80\x9cbut the dark changes all, as I know. I will\r\nlook, if that please thee.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe stared with puckered-up eyes round the vault, and then lifted up from\r\nthe floor a handful of something that glittered.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOho!\xe2\x80\x9d said he, \xe2\x80\x9cthis is like the stuff they play with in the Man-Pack:\r\nonly this is yellow and the other was brown.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe let the gold pieces fall, and move forward. The floor of the vault\r\nwas buried some five or six feet deep in coined gold and silver that had\r\nburst from the sacks it had been originally stored in, and, in the long\r\nyears, the metal had packed and settled as sand packs at low tide. On it\r\nand in it and rising through it, as wrecks lift through the sand, were\r\njewelled elephant-howdahs of embossed silver, studded with plates of\r\nhammered gold, and adorned with carbuncles and turquoises. There were\r\npalanquins and litters for carrying queens, framed and braced with\r\nsilver and enamel, with jade-handled poles and amber curtain-rings;\r\nthere were golden candlesticks hung with pierced emeralds that quivered\r\non the branches; there were studded images, five feet high, of forgotten\r\ngods, silver with jewelled eyes; there were coats of mail, gold inlaid\r\non steel, and fringed with rotted and blackened seed-pearls; there\r\nwere helmets, crested and beaded with pigeon\xe2\x80\x99s-blood rubies; there were\r\nshields of lacquer, of tortoise-shell and rhinoceros-hide, strapped\r\nand bossed with red gold and set with emeralds at the edge; there were\r\nsheaves of diamond-hilted swords, daggers, and hunting-knives; there\r\nwere golden sacrificial bowls and ladles, and portable altars of a shape\r\nthat never sees the light of day; there were jade cups and bracelets;\r\nthere were incense-burners, combs, and pots for perfume, henna, and\r\neye-powder, all in embossed gold; there were nose-rings, armlets,\r\nhead-bands, finger-rings, and girdles past any counting; there were\r\nbelts, seven fingers broad, of square-cut diamonds and rubies, and\r\nwooden boxes, trebly clamped with iron, from which the wood had fallen\r\naway in powder, showing the pile of uncut star-sapphires, opals,\r\ncat\xe2\x80\x99s-eyes, sapphires, rubies, diamonds, emeralds, and garnets within.\r\n\r\nThe White Cobra was right. No mere money would begin to pay the value of\r\nthis treasure, the sifted pickings of centuries of war, plunder, trade,\r\nand taxation. The coins alone were priceless, leaving out of count all\r\nthe precious stones; and the dead weight of the gold and silver alone\r\nmight be two or three hundred tons. Every native ruler in India to-day,\r\nhowever poor, has a hoard to which he is always adding; and though, once\r\nin a long while, some enlightened prince may send off forty or fifty\r\nbullock-cart loads of silver to be exchanged for Government securities,\r\nthe bulk of them keep their treasure and the knowledge of it very\r\nclosely to themselves.\r\n\r\nBut Mowgli naturally did not understand what these things meant. The\r\nknives interested him a little, but they did not balance so well as\r\nhis own, and so he dropped them. At last he found something really\r\nfascinating laid on the front of a howdah half buried in the coins.\r\nIt was a three-foot ankus, or elephant-goad--something like a small\r\nboat-hook. The top was one round, shining ruby, and eight inches of\r\nthe handle below it were studded with rough turquoises close together,\r\ngiving a most satisfactory grip. Below them was a rim of jade with a\r\nflower-pattern running round it--only the leaves were emeralds, and\r\nthe blossoms were rubies sunk in the cool, green stone. The rest of\r\nthe handle was a shaft of pure ivory, while the point--the spike and\r\nhook--was gold-inlaid steel with pictures of elephant-catching; and the\r\npictures attracted Mowgli, who saw that they had something to do with\r\nhis friend Hathi the Silent.\r\n\r\nThe White Cobra had been following him closely.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs this not worth dying to behold?\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cHave I not done thee a\r\ngreat favour?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI do not understand,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mowgli. \xe2\x80\x9cThe things are hard and cold, and\r\nby no means good to eat. But this\xe2\x80\x9d--he lifted the ankus--\xe2\x80\x9cI desire to\r\ntake away, that I may see it in the sun. Thou sayest they are all thine?\r\nWilt thou give it to me, and I will bring thee frogs to eat?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe White Cobra fairly shook with evil delight. \xe2\x80\x9cAssuredly I will give\r\nit,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cAll that is here I will give thee--till thou goest away.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut I go now. This place is dark and cold, and I wish to take the\r\nthorn-pointed thing to the Jungle.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLook by thy foot! What is that there?\xe2\x80\x9d Mowgli picked up something white\r\nand smooth. \xe2\x80\x9cIt is the bone of a man\xe2\x80\x99s head,\xe2\x80\x9d he said quietly. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd here\r\nare two more.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey came to take the treasure away many years ago. I spoke to them in\r\nthe dark, and they lay still.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut what do I need of this that is called treasure? If thou wilt\r\ngive me the ankus to take away, it is good hunting. If not, it is good\r\nhunting none the less. I do not fight with the Poison People, and I was\r\nalso taught the Master-word of thy tribe.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere is but one Master-word here. It is mine!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nKaa flung himself forward with blazing eyes. \xe2\x80\x9cWho bade me bring the\r\nMan?\xe2\x80\x9d he hissed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI surely,\xe2\x80\x9d the old Cobra lisped. \xe2\x80\x9cIt is long since I have seen Man, and\r\nthis Man speaks our tongue.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut there was no talk of killing. How can I go to the Jungle and say\r\nthat I have led him to his death?\xe2\x80\x9d said Kaa.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI talk not of killing till the time. And as to thy going or not going,\r\nthere is the hole in the wall. Peace, now, thou fat monkey-killer! I\r\nhave but to touch thy neck, and the Jungle will know thee no longer.\r\nNever Man came here that went away with the breath under his ribs. I am\r\nthe Warden of the Treasure of the King\xe2\x80\x99s City!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut, thou white worm of the dark, I tell thee there is neither king nor\r\ncity! The Jungle is all about us!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Kaa.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere is still the Treasure. But this can be done. Wait awhile, Kaa of\r\nthe Rocks, and see the boy run. There is room for great sport here. Life\r\nis good. Run to and fro awhile, and make sport, boy!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMowgli put his hand on Kaa\xe2\x80\x99s head quietly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe white thing has dealt with men of the Man-Pack until now. He does\r\nnot know me,\xe2\x80\x9d he whispered. \xe2\x80\x9cHe has asked for this hunting. Let him have\r\nit.\xe2\x80\x9d Mowgli had been standing with the ankus held point down. He flung\r\nit from him quickly and it dropped crossways just behind the great\r\nsnake\xe2\x80\x99s hood, pinning him to the floor. In a flash, Kaa\xe2\x80\x99s weight was\r\nupon the writhing body, paralysing it from hood to tail. The red eyes\r\nburned, and the six spare inches of the head struck furiously right and\r\nleft.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cKill!\xe2\x80\x9d said Kaa, as Mowgli\xe2\x80\x99s hand went to his knife.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, as he drew the blade; \xe2\x80\x9cI will never kill again save for\r\nfood. But look you, Kaa!\xe2\x80\x9d He caught the snake behind the hood, forced\r\nthe mouth open with the blade of the knife, and showed the terrible\r\npoison-fangs of the upper jaw lying black and withered in the gum. The\r\nWhite Cobra had outlived his poison, as a snake will.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTHUU\xe2\x80\x9d (\xe2\x80\x9cIt is dried up\xe2\x80\x9d--Literally, a rotted out tree-stump), said\r\nMowgli; and motioning Kaa away, he picked up the ankus, setting the\r\nWhite Cobra free.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe King\xe2\x80\x99s Treasure needs a new Warden,\xe2\x80\x9d he said gravely. \xe2\x80\x9cThuu, thou\r\nhast not done well. Run to and fro and make sport, Thuu!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am ashamed. Kill me!\xe2\x80\x9d hissed the White Cobra.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere has been too much talk of killing. We will go now. I take the\r\nthorn-pointed thing, Thuu, because I have fought and worsted thee.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSee, then, that the thing does not kill thee at last. It is Death!\r\nRemember, it is Death! There is enough in that thing to kill the men of\r\nall my city. Not long wilt thou hold it, Jungle Man, nor he who takes it\r\nfrom thee. They will kill, and kill, and kill for its sake! My strength\r\nis dried up, but the ankus will do my work. It is Death! It is Death! It\r\nis Death!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMowgli crawled out through the hole into the passage again, and the last\r\nthat he saw was the White Cobra striking furiously with his harmless\r\nfangs at the stolid golden faces of the gods that lay on the floor, and\r\nhissing, \xe2\x80\x9cIt is Death!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThey were glad to get to the light of day once more; and when they\r\nwere back in their own Jungle and Mowgli made the ankus glitter in the\r\nmorning light, he was almost as pleased as though he had found a bunch\r\nof new flowers to stick in his hair.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThis is brighter than Bagheera\xe2\x80\x99s eyes,\xe2\x80\x9d he said delightedly, as he\r\ntwirled the ruby. \xe2\x80\x9cI will show it to him; but what did the Thuu mean\r\nwhen he talked of death?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI cannot say. I am sorrowful to my tail\xe2\x80\x99s tail that he felt not thy\r\nknife. There is always evil at Cold Lairs--above ground or below. But\r\nnow I am hungry. Dost thou hunt with me this dawn?\xe2\x80\x9d said Kaa.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo; Bagheera must see this thing. Good hunting!\xe2\x80\x9d Mowgli danced off,\r\nflourishing the great ankus, and stopping from time to time to admire\r\nit, till he came to that part of the Jungle Bagheera chiefly used,\r\nand found him drinking after a heavy kill. Mowgli told him all his\r\nadventures from beginning to end, and Bagheera sniffed at the ankus\r\nbetween whiles. When Mowgli came to the White Cobra\xe2\x80\x99s last words, the\r\nPanther purred approvingly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen the White Hood spoke the thing which is?\xe2\x80\x9d Mowgli asked quickly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI was born in the King\xe2\x80\x99s cages at Oodeypore, and it is in my stomach\r\nthat I know some little of Man. Very many men would kill thrice in a\r\nnight for the sake of that one big red stone alone.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut the stone makes it heavy to the hand. My little bright knife is\r\nbetter; and--see! the red stone is not good to eat. Then WHY would they\r\nkill?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMowgli, go thou and sleep. Thou hast lived among men, and----\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI remember. Men kill because they are not hunting;--for idleness and\r\npleasure. Wake again, Bagheera. For what use was this thorn-pointed\r\nthing made?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBagheera half opened his eyes--he was very sleepy--with a malicious\r\ntwinkle.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt was made by men to thrust into the head of the sons of Hathi, so\r\nthat the blood should pour out. I have seen the like in the street of\r\nOodeypore, before our cages. That thing has tasted the blood of many\r\nsuch as Hathi.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut why do they thrust into the heads of elephants?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTo teach them Man\xe2\x80\x99s Law. Having neither claws nor teeth, men make these\r\nthings--and worse.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAlways more blood when I come near, even to the things the Man-Pack\r\nhave made,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mowgli disgustedly. He was getting a little tired of\r\nthe weight of the ankus. \xe2\x80\x9cIf I had known this, I would not have taken\r\nit. First it was Messua\xe2\x80\x99s blood on the thongs, and now it is Hathi\xe2\x80\x99s. I\r\nwill use it no more. Look!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe ankus flew sparkling, and buried itself point down thirty yards\r\naway, between the trees. \xe2\x80\x9cSo my hands are clean of Death,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mowgli,\r\nrubbing his palms on the fresh, moist earth. \xe2\x80\x9cThe Thuu said Death would\r\nfollow me. He is old and white and mad.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhite or black, or death or life, _I_ am going to sleep, Little\r\nBrother. I cannot hunt all night and howl all day, as do some folk.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBagheera went off to a hunting-lair that he knew, about two miles off.\r\nMowgli made an easy way for himself up a convenient tree, knotted three\r\nor four creepers together, and in less time than it takes to tell was\r\nswinging in a hammock fifty feet above ground. Though he had no positive\r\nobjection to strong daylight, Mowgli followed the custom of his\r\nfriends, and used it as little as he could. When he waked among the very\r\nloud-voiced peoples that live in the trees, it was twilight once more,\r\nand he had been dreaming of the beautiful pebbles he had thrown away.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAt least I will look at the thing again,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, and slid down a\r\ncreeper to the earth; but Bagheera was before him. Mowgli could hear him\r\nsnuffing in the half light.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhere is the thorn-pointed thing?\xe2\x80\x9d cried Mowgli.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA man has taken it. Here is the trail.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow we shall see whether the Thuu spoke truth. If the pointed thing is\r\nDeath, that man will die. Let us follow.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cKill first,\xe2\x80\x9d said Bagheera. \xe2\x80\x9cAn empty stomach makes a careless eye. Men\r\ngo very slowly, and the Jungle is wet enough to hold the lightest mark.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThey killed as soon as they could, but it was nearly three hours before\r\nthey finished their meat and drink and buckled down to the trail. The\r\nJungle People know that nothing makes up for being hurried over your\r\nmeals.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThink you the pointed thing will turn in the man\xe2\x80\x99s hand and kill him?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n Mowgli asked. \xe2\x80\x9cThe Thuu said it was Death.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe shall see when we find,\xe2\x80\x9d said Bagheera, trotting with his head low.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is single-foot\xe2\x80\x9d (he meant that there was only one man), \xe2\x80\x9cand the\r\nweight of the thing has pressed his heel far into the ground.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHai! This is as clear as summer lightning,\xe2\x80\x9d Mowgli answered; and they\r\nfell into the quick, choppy trail-trot in and out through the checkers\r\nof the moonlight, following the marks of those two bare feet.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow he runs swiftly,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mowgli. \xe2\x80\x9cThe toes are spread apart.\xe2\x80\x9d They\r\nwent on over some wet ground. \xe2\x80\x9cNow why does he turn aside here?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWait!\xe2\x80\x9d said Bagheera, and flung himself forward with one superb bound\r\nas far as ever he could. The first thing to do when a trail ceases to\r\nexplain itself is to cast forward without leaving, your own confusing\r\nfoot-marks on the ground. Bagheera turned as he landed, and faced\r\nMowgli, crying, \xe2\x80\x9cHere comes another trail to meet him. It is a smaller\r\nfoot, this second trail, and the toes turn inward.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThen Mowgli ran up and looked. \xe2\x80\x9cIt is the foot of a Gond hunter,\xe2\x80\x9d he\r\nsaid. \xe2\x80\x9cLook! Here he dragged his bow on the grass. That is why the first\r\ntrail turned aside so quickly. Big Foot hid from Little Foot.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is true,\xe2\x80\x9d said Bagheera. \xe2\x80\x9cNow, lest by crossing each other\xe2\x80\x99s\r\ntracks we foul the signs, let each take one trail. I am Big Foot, Little\r\nBrother, and thou art Little Foot, the Gond.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBagheera leaped back to the original trail, leaving Mowgli stooping\r\nabove the curious narrow track of the wild little man of the woods.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow,\xe2\x80\x9d said Bagheera, moving step by step along the chain of footprints,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI, Big Foot, turn aside here. Now I hide me behind a rock and stand\r\nstill, not daring to shift my feet. Cry thy trail, Little Brother.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow, I, Little Foot, come to the rock,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mowgli, running up his\r\ntrail. \xe2\x80\x9cNow, I sit down under the rock, leaning upon my right hand, and\r\nresting my bow between my toes. I wait long, for the mark of my feet is\r\ndeep here.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI also,\xe2\x80\x9d said Bagheera, hidden behind the rock. \xe2\x80\x9cI wait, resting the end\r\nof the thorn-pointed thing upon a stone. It slips, for here is a scratch\r\nupon the stone. Cry thy trail, Little Brother.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOne, two twigs and a big branch are broken here,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mowgli, in an\r\nundertone. \xe2\x80\x9cNow, how shall I cry THAT? Ah! It is plain now. I, Little\r\nFoot, go away making noises and tramplings so that Big Foot may hear\r\nme.\xe2\x80\x9d He moved away from the rock pace by pace among the trees, his\r\nvoice rising in the distance as he approached a little cascade. \xe2\x80\x9cI--go,\r\nfar--away--to--where--the--noise--of--falling-water--covers--my--noise;\r\nand--here--I--wait. Cry thy trail, Bagheera, Big Foot!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe panther had been casting in every direction to see how Big Foot\xe2\x80\x99s\r\ntrail led away from behind the rock. Then he gave tongue:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI come from behind the rock upon my knees, dragging the thorn-pointed\r\nthing. Seeing no one, I run. I, Big Foot, run swiftly. The trail is\r\nclear. Let each follow his own. I run!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBagheera swept on along the clearly-marked trail, and Mowgli followed\r\nthe steps of the Gond. For some time there was silence in the Jungle.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhere art thou, Little Foot?\xe2\x80\x9d cried Bagheera. Mowgli\xe2\x80\x99s voice answered\r\nhim not fifty yards to the right.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cUm!\xe2\x80\x9d said the Panther, with a deep cough. \xe2\x80\x9cThe two run side by side,\r\ndrawing nearer!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThey raced on another half-mile, always keeping about the same distance,\r\ntill Mowgli, whose head was not so close to the ground as Bagheera\xe2\x80\x99s,\r\ncried: \xe2\x80\x9cThey have met. Good hunting--look! Here stood Little Foot, with\r\nhis knee on a rock--and yonder is Big Foot indeed!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nNot ten yards in front of them, stretched across a pile of broken rocks,\r\nlay the body of a villager of the district, a long, small-feathered Gond\r\narrow through his back and breast.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWas the Thuu so old and so mad, Little Brother?\xe2\x80\x9d said Bagheera gently.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHere is one death, at least.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFollow on. But where is the drinker of elephant\xe2\x80\x99s blood--the red-eyed\r\nthorn?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLittle Foot has it--perhaps. It is single-foot again now.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe single trail of a light man who had been running quickly and bearing\r\na burden on his left shoulder held on round a long, low spur of dried\r\ngrass, where each footfall seemed, to the sharp eyes of the trackers,\r\nmarked in hot iron.\r\n\r\nNeither spoke till the trail ran up to the ashes of a camp-fire hidden\r\nin a ravine.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAgain!\xe2\x80\x9d said Bagheera, checking as though he had been turned into\r\nstone.\r\n\r\nThe body of a little wizened Gond lay with its feet in the ashes, and\r\nBagheera looked inquiringly at Mowgli.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat was done with a bamboo,\xe2\x80\x9d said the boy, after one glance. \xe2\x80\x9cI have\r\nused such a thing among the buffaloes when I served in the Man-Pack.\r\nThe Father of Cobras--I am sorrowful that I made a jest of him--knew\r\nthe breed well, as I might have known. Said I not that men kill for\r\nidleness?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIndeed, they killed for the sake of the red and blue stones,\xe2\x80\x9d Bagheera\r\nanswered. \xe2\x80\x9cRemember, I was in the King\xe2\x80\x99s cages at Oodeypore.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOne, two, three, four tracks,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mowgli, stooping over the ashes.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFour tracks of men with shod feet. They do not go so quickly as Gonds.\r\nNow, what evil had the little woodman done to them? See, they talked\r\ntogether, all five, standing up, before they killed him. Bagheera, let\r\nus go back. My stomach is heavy in me, and yet it heaves up and down\r\nlike an oriole\xe2\x80\x99s nest at the end of a branch.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is not good hunting to leave game afoot. Follow!\xe2\x80\x9d said the panther.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThose eight shod feet have not gone far.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nNo more was said for fully an hour, as they worked up the broad trail of\r\nthe four men with shod feet.\r\n\r\nIt was clear, hot daylight now, and Bagheera said, \xe2\x80\x9cI smell smoke.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMen are always more ready to eat than to run, Mowgli answered, trotting\r\nin and out between the low scrub bushes of the new Jungle they were\r\nexploring. Bagheera, a little to his left, made an indescribable noise\r\nin his throat.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHere is one that has done with feeding,\xe2\x80\x9d said he. A tumbled bundle\r\nof gay-coloured clothes lay under a bush, and round it was some spilt\r\nflour.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat was done by the bamboo again,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mowgli. \xe2\x80\x9cSee! that white dust\r\nis what men eat. They have taken the kill from this one,--he carried\r\ntheir food,--and given him for a kill to Chil, the Kite.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is the third,\xe2\x80\x9d said Bagheera.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI will go with new, big frogs to the Father of Cobras, and feed him\r\nfat,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mowgli to himself. \xe2\x80\x9cThe drinker of elephant\xe2\x80\x99s blood is Death\r\nhimself--but still I do not understand!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFollow!\xe2\x80\x9d said Bagheera.\r\n\r\nThey had not gone half a mile farther when they heard Ko, the Crow,\r\nsinging the death-song in the top of a tamarisk under whose shade three\r\nmen were lying. A half-dead fire smoked in the centre of the circle,\r\nunder an iron plate which held a blackened and burned cake of unleavened\r\nbread. Close to the fire, and blazing in the sunshine, lay the\r\nruby-and-turquoise ankus.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe thing works quickly; all ends here,\xe2\x80\x9d said Bagheera. \xe2\x80\x9cHow did THESE\r\ndie, Mowgli? There is no mark on any.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nA Jungle-dweller gets to learn by experience as much as many doctors\r\nknow of poisonous plants and berries. Mowgli sniffed the smoke that came\r\nup from the fire, broke off a morsel of the blackened bread, tasted it,\r\nand spat it out again.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cApple of Death,\xe2\x80\x9d he coughed. \xe2\x80\x9cThe first must have made it ready in the\r\nfood for THESE, who killed him, having first killed the Gond.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGood hunting, indeed! The kills follow close,\xe2\x80\x9d said Bagheera.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cApple of Death\xe2\x80\x9d is what the Jungle call thorn-apple or dhatura, the\r\nreadiest poison in all India.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat now?\xe2\x80\x9d said the panther. \xe2\x80\x9cMust thou and I kill each other for\r\nyonder red-eyed slayer?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCan it speak?\xe2\x80\x9d said Mowgli in a whisper. \xe2\x80\x9cDid I do it a wrong when I\r\nthrew it away? Between us two it can do no wrong, for we do not desire\r\nwhat men desire. If it be left here, it will assuredly continue to kill\r\nmen one after another as fast as nuts fall in a high wind. I have no\r\nlove to men, but even I would not have them die six in a night.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat matter? They are only men. They killed one another, and were well\r\npleased,\xe2\x80\x9d said Bagheera. \xe2\x80\x9cThat first little woodman hunted well.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey are cubs none the less; and a cub will drown himself to bite the\r\nmoon\xe2\x80\x99s light on the water. The fault was mine,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mowgli, who spoke\r\nas though he knew all about everything. \xe2\x80\x9cI will never again bring into\r\nthe Jungle strange things--not though they be as beautiful as flowers.\r\nThis\xe2\x80\x9d--he handled the ankus gingerly--\xe2\x80\x9cgoes back to the Father of\r\nCobras. But first we must sleep, and we cannot sleep near these\r\nsleepers. Also we must bury HIM, lest he run away and kill another six.\r\nDig me a hole under that tree.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut, Little Brother,\xe2\x80\x9d said Bagheera, moving off to the spot, \xe2\x80\x9cI tell\r\nthee it is no fault of the blood-drinker. The trouble is with the men.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAll one,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mowgli. \xe2\x80\x9cDig the hole deep. When we wake I will take him\r\nup and carry him back.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n*****\r\n\r\nTwo nights later, as the White Cobra sat mourning in the darkness of\r\nthe vault, ashamed, and robbed, and alone, the turquoise ankus whirled\r\nthrough the hole in the wall, and clashed on the floor of golden coins.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFather of Cobras,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mowgli (he was careful to keep the other side\r\nof the wall), \xe2\x80\x9cget thee a young and ripe one of thine own people to help\r\nthee guard the King\xe2\x80\x99s Treasure, so that no man may come away alive any\r\nmore.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh-ha! It returns, then. I said the thing was Death. How comes it that\r\nthou art still alive?\xe2\x80\x9d the old Cobra mumbled, twining lovingly round the\r\nankus-haft.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBy the Bull that bought me, I do not know! That thing has killed six\r\ntimes in a night. Let him go out no more.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nTHE SONG OF THE LITTLE HUNTER\r\n\r\n     Ere Mor the Peacock flutters, ere the Monkey People cry,\r\n       Ere Chil the Kite swoops down a furlong sheer,\r\n     Through the Jungle very softly flits a shadow and a sigh--\r\n       He is Fear, O Little Hunter, he is Fear!\r\n\r\n     Very softly down the glade runs a waiting, watching shade,\r\n       And the whisper spreads and widens far and near;\r\n     And the sweat is on thy brow, for he passes even now--\r\n       He is Fear, O Little Hunter, he is Fear!\r\n\r\n     Ere the moon has climbed the mountain, ere the rocks\r\n           are ribbed with light,\r\n       When the downward-dipping trails are dank and drear,\r\n     Comes a breathing hard behind thee--snuffle-snuffle\r\n           through the night--\r\n       It is Fear, O Little Hunter, it is Fear!\r\n\r\n     On thy knees and draw the bow; bid the shrilling arrow go;\r\n       In the empty, mocking thicket plunge the spear;\r\n     But thy hands are loosed and weak, and the blood has left\r\n           thy cheek--\r\n       It is Fear, O Little Hunter, it is Fear!\r\n\r\n     When the heat-cloud sucks the tempest, when the slivered\r\n           pine-trees fall,\r\n       When the blinding, blaring rain-squalls lash and veer;\r\n     Through the war-gongs of the thunder rings a voice more\r\n           loud than all--\r\n       It is Fear, O Little Hunter, it is Fear!\r\n\r\n     Now the spates are banked and deep; now the footless\r\n           boulders leap--\r\n       Now the lightning shows each littlest leaf-rib clear--\r\n     But thy throat is shut and dried, and thy heart against\r\n           thy side\r\n       Hammers: Fear, O Little Hunter--this is Fear!\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nQUIQUERN\r\n\r\n     The People of the Eastern Ice, they are melting like the snow--\r\n     They beg for coffee and sugar; they go where the white men go.\r\n     The People of the Western Ice, they learn to steal and fight;\r\n     \xe2\x80\x9cThey sell their furs to the trading-post: they sell their souls\r\n         to the white.\r\n     The People of the Southern Ice, they trade with the whaler\xe2\x80\x99s\r\n     crew;\r\n     Their women have many ribbons, but their tents are torn and few.\r\n     But the People of the Elder Ice, beyond the white man\xe2\x80\x99s ken--\r\n     Their spears are made of the narwhal-horn, and they are the\r\n         last of the Men!\r\n                                         Translation.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe has opened his eyes. Look!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPut him in the skin again. He will be a strong dog. On the fourth month\r\nwe will name him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFor whom?\xe2\x80\x9d said Amoraq.\r\n\r\nKadlu\xe2\x80\x99s eye rolled round the skin-lined snow-house till it fell on\r\nfourteen-year-old Kotuko sitting on the sleeping-bench, making a button\r\nout of walrus ivory. \xe2\x80\x9cName him for me,\xe2\x80\x9d said Kotuko, with a grin. \xe2\x80\x9cI\r\nshall need him one day.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nKadlu grinned back till his eyes were almost buried in the fat of his\r\nflat cheeks, and nodded to Amoraq, while the puppy\xe2\x80\x99s fierce mother\r\nwhined to see her baby wriggling far out of reach in the little sealskin\r\npouch hung above the warmth of the blubber-lamp. Kotuko went on with his\r\ncarving, and Kadlu threw a rolled bundle of leather dog-harnesses into a\r\ntiny little room that opened from one side of the house, slipped off his\r\nheavy deerskin hunting-suit, put it into a whalebone-net that hung above\r\nanother lamp, and dropped down on the sleeping-bench to whittle at\r\na piece of frozen seal-meat till Amoraq, his wife, should bring the\r\nregular dinner of boiled meat and blood-soup. He had been out since\r\nearly dawn at the seal-holes, eight miles away, and had come home with\r\nthree big seal. Half-way down the long, low snow passage or tunnel\r\nthat led to the inner door of the house you could hear snappings and\r\nyelpings, as the dogs of his sleigh-team, released from the day\xe2\x80\x99s work,\r\nscuffled for warm places.\r\n\r\nWhen the yelpings grew too loud Kotuko lazily rolled off the\r\nsleeping-bench, and picked up a whip with an eighteen-inch handle of\r\nspringy whalebone, and twenty-five feet of heavy, plaited thong. He\r\ndived into the passage, where it sounded as though all the dogs were\r\neating him alive; but that was no more than their regular grace before\r\nmeals. When he crawled out at the far end, half a dozen furry heads\r\nfollowed him with their eyes as he went to a sort of gallows of\r\nwhale-jawbones, from which the dog\xe2\x80\x99s meat was hung; split off the frozen\r\nstuff in big lumps with a broad-headed spear; and stood, his whip in\r\none hand and the meat in the other. Each beast was called by name, the\r\nweakest first, and woe betide any dog that moved out of his turn; for\r\nthe tapering lash would shoot out like thonged lightning, and flick away\r\nan inch or so of hair and hide. Each beast growled, snapped, choked once\r\nover his portion, and hurried back to the protection of the passage,\r\nwhile the boy stood upon the snow under the blazing Northern Lights and\r\ndealt out justice.
