The last to be served was the big black leader of\r\nthe team, who kept order when the dogs were harnessed; and to him Kotuko\r\ngave a double allowance of meat as well as an extra crack of the whip.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh!\xe2\x80\x9d said Kotuko, coiling up the lash, \xe2\x80\x9cI have a little one over the\r\nlamp that will make a great many howlings. SARPOK! Get in!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe crawled back over the huddled dogs, dusted the dry snow from his\r\nfurs with the whalebone beater that Amoraq kept by the door, tapped the\r\nskin-lined roof of the house to shake off any icicles that might have\r\nfallen from the dome of snow above, and curled up on the bench. The\r\ndogs in the passage snored and whined in their sleep, the boy-baby in\r\nAmoraq\xe2\x80\x99s deep fur hood kicked and choked and gurgled, and the mother of\r\nthe newly-named puppy lay at Kotuko\xe2\x80\x99s side, her eyes fixed on the bundle\r\nof sealskin, warm and safe above the broad yellow flame of the lamp.\r\n\r\nAnd all this happened far away to the north, beyond Labrador, beyond\r\nHudson\xe2\x80\x99s Strait, where the great tides heave the ice about, north of\r\nMelville Peninsula--north even of the narrow Fury and Hecla Straits--on\r\nthe north shore of Baffin Land, where Bylot\xe2\x80\x99s Island stands above the\r\nice of Lancaster Sound like a pudding-bowl wrong side up. North of\r\nLancaster Sound there is little we know anything about, except North\r\nDevon and Ellesmere Land; but even there live a few scattered people,\r\nnext door, as it were, to the very Pole.\r\n\r\nKadlu was an Inuit,--what you call an Esquimau,--and his tribe, some\r\nthirty persons all told, belonged to the Tununirmiut--\xe2\x80\x9cthe country lying\r\nat the back of something.\xe2\x80\x9d In the maps that desolate coast is written\r\nNavy Board Inlet, but the Inuit name is best, because the country lies\r\nat the very back of everything in the world. For nine months of the year\r\nthere is only ice and snow, and gale after gale, with a cold that no\r\none can realise who has never seen the thermometer even at zero. For six\r\nmonths of those nine it is dark; and that is what makes it so horrible.\r\nIn the three months of the summer it only freezes every other day and\r\nevery night, and then the snow begins to weep off on the southerly\r\nslopes, and a few ground-willows put out their woolly buds, a tiny\r\nstonecrop or so makes believe to blossom, beaches of fine gravel and\r\nrounded stones run down to the open sea, and polished boulders and\r\nstreaked rocks lift up above the granulated snow. But all that is gone\r\nin a few weeks, and the wild winter locks down again on the land; while\r\nat sea the ice tears up and down the offing, jamming and ramming, and\r\nsplitting and hitting, and pounding and grounding, till it all freezes\r\ntogether, ten feet thick, from the land outward to deep water.\r\n\r\nIn the winter Kadlu would follow the seal to the edge of this land-ice,\r\nand spear them as they came up to breathe at their blow-holes. The\r\nseal must have open water to live and catch fish in, and in the deep of\r\nwinter the ice would sometimes run eighty miles without a break from the\r\nnearest shore. In the spring he and his people retreated from the floes\r\nto the rocky mainland, where they put up tents of skins, and snared the\r\nsea-birds, or speared the young seal basking on the beaches. Later, they\r\nwould go south into Baffin Land after the reindeer, and to get their\r\nyear\xe2\x80\x99s store of salmon from the hundreds of streams and lakes of the\r\ninterior; coming back north in September or October for the musk-ox\r\nhunting and the regular winter sealery. This travelling was done with\r\ndog-sleighs, twenty and thirty miles a day, or sometimes down the coast\r\nin big skin \xe2\x80\x9cwoman-boats,\xe2\x80\x9d when the dogs and the babies lay among the\r\nfeet of the rowers, and the women sang songs as they glided from cape to\r\ncape over the glassy, cold waters. All the luxuries that the Tununirmiut\r\nknew came from the south--driftwood for sleigh-runners, rod-iron for\r\nharpoon-tips, steel knives, tin kettles that cooked food much better\r\nthan the old soap-stone affairs, flint and steel, and even matches, as\r\nwell as coloured ribbons for the women\xe2\x80\x99s hair, little cheap mirrors,\r\nand red cloth for the edging of deerskin dress-jackets. Kadlu traded the\r\nrich, creamy, twisted narwhal horn and musk-ox teeth (these are just\r\nas valuable as pearls) to the Southern Inuit, and they, in turn, traded\r\nwith the whalers and the missionary-posts of Exeter and Cumberland\r\nSounds; and so the chain went on, till a kettle picked up by a ship\xe2\x80\x99s\r\ncook in the Bhendy Bazaar might end its days over a blubber-lamp\r\nsomewhere on the cool side of the Arctic Circle.\r\n\r\nKadlu, being a good hunter, was rich in iron harpoons, snow-knives,\r\nbird-darts, and all the other things that make life easy up there in the\r\ngreat cold; and he was the head of his tribe, or, as they say, \xe2\x80\x9cthe\r\nman who knows all about it by practice.\xe2\x80\x9d This did not give him any\r\nauthority, except now and then he could advise his friends to change\r\ntheir hunting-grounds; but Kotuko used it to domineer a little, in the\r\nlazy, fat Inuit fashion, over the other boys, when they came out at\r\nnight to play ball in the moonlight, or to sing the Child\xe2\x80\x99s Song to the\r\nAurora Borealis.\r\n\r\nBut at fourteen an Inuit feels himself a man, and Kotuko was tired of\r\nmaking snares for wild-fowl and kit-foxes, and most tired of all of\r\nhelping the women to chew seal-and deer-skins (that supples them as\r\nnothing else can) the long day through, while the men were out hunting.\r\nHe wanted to go into the quaggi, the Singing-House, when the hunters\r\ngathered there for their mysteries, and the angekok, the sorcerer,\r\nfrightened them into the most delightful fits after the lamps were put\r\nout, and you could hear the Spirit of the Reindeer stamping on the roof;\r\nand when a spear was thrust out into the open black night it came back\r\ncovered with hot blood. He wanted to throw his big boots into the net\r\nwith the tired air of the head of a family, and to gamble with the\r\nhunters when they dropped in of an evening and played a sort of\r\nhome-made roulette with a tin pot and a nail. There were hundreds of\r\nthings that he wanted to do, but the grown men laughed at him and said,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWait till you have been in the buckle, Kotuko. Hunting is not ALL\r\ncatching.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nNow that his father had named a puppy for him, things looked brighter.\r\nAn Inuit does not waste a good dog on his son till the boy knows\r\nsomething of dog-driving; and Kotuko was more than sure that he knew\r\nmore than everything.\r\n\r\nIf the puppy had not had an iron constitution he would have died from\r\nover-stuffing and over-handling. Kotuko made him a tiny harness with a\r\ntrace to it, and hauled him all over the house-floor, shouting: \xe2\x80\x9cAua!\r\nJa aua!\xe2\x80\x9d (Go to the right). \xe2\x80\x9cChoiachoi! Ja choiachoi!\xe2\x80\x9d (Go to the left).\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOhaha!\xe2\x80\x9d (Stop). The puppy did not like it at all, but being fished for\r\nin this way was pure happiness beside being put to the sleigh for the\r\nfirst time. He just sat down on the snow, and played with the seal-hide\r\ntrace that ran from his harness to the pitu, the big thong in the bows\r\nof the sleigh. Then the team started, and the puppy found the heavy\r\nten-foot sleigh running up his back, and dragging him along the snow,\r\nwhile Kotuko laughed till the tears ran down his face. There followed\r\ndays and days of the cruel whip that hisses like the wind over ice, and\r\nhis companions all bit him because he did not know his work, and the\r\nharness chafed him, and he was dot allowed to sleep with Kotuko any\r\nmore, but had to take the coldest place in the passage. It was a sad\r\ntime for the puppy.\r\n\r\nThe boy learned, too, as fast as the dog; though a dog-sleigh is a\r\nheart-breaking thing to manage. Each beast is harnessed, the weakest\r\nnearest to the driver, by his own separate trace, which runs under\r\nhis left fore-leg to the main thong, where it is fastened by a sort\r\nof button and loop which can be slipped by a turn of the wrist, thus\r\nfreeing one dog at a time. This is very necessary, because young dogs\r\noften get the trace between their hind legs, where it cuts to the bone.\r\nAnd they one and all WILL go visiting their friends as they run, jumping\r\nin and out among the traces. Then they fight, and the result is more\r\nmixed than a wet fishing-line next morning. A great deal of trouble can\r\nbe avoided by scientific use of the whip. Every Inuit boy prides himself\r\nas being a master of the long lash; but it is easy to flick at a mark on\r\nthe ground, and difficult to lean forward and catch a shirking dog just\r\nbehind the shoulders when the sleigh is going at full speed. If you call\r\none dog\xe2\x80\x99s name for \xe2\x80\x9cvisiting,\xe2\x80\x9d and accidentally lash another, the two\r\nwill fight it out at once, and stop all the others. Again, if you travel\r\nwith a companion and begin to talk, or by yourself and sing, the dogs\r\nwill halt, turn round, and sit down to hear what you have to say. Kotuko\r\nwas run away from once or twice through forgetting to block the sleigh\r\nwhen he stopped; and he broke many lashings, and ruined a few thongs\r\nbefore he could be trusted with a full team of eight and the light\r\nsleigh. Then he felt himself a person of consequence, and on smooth,\r\nblack ice, with a bold heart and a quick elbow, he smoked along over\r\nthe levels as fast as a pack in full cry. He would go ten miles to the\r\nseal-holes, and when he was on the hunting-grounds he would twitch a\r\ntrace loose from the pitu, and free the big black leader, who was\r\nthe cleverest dog in the team. As soon as the dog had scented a\r\nbreathing-hole, Kotuko would reverse the sleigh, driving a couple of\r\nsawed-off antlers, that stuck up like perambulator-handles from the\r\nback-rest, deep into the snow, so that the team could not get away. Then\r\nhe would crawl forward inch by inch, and wait till the seal came up\r\nto breathe. Then he would stab down swiftly with his spear and\r\nrunning-line, and presently would haul his seal up to the lip of the\r\nice, while the black leader came up and helped to pull the carcass\r\nacross the ice to the sleigh. That was the time when the harnessed dogs\r\nyelled and foamed with excitement, and Kotuko laid the long lash like a\r\nred-hot bar across all their faces, till the carcass froze stiff. Going\r\nhome was the heavy work. The loaded sleigh had to be humoured among the\r\nrough ice, and the dogs sat down and looked hungrily at the seal instead\r\nof pulling. At last they would strike the well-worn sleigh-road to the\r\nvillage, and toodle-kiyi along the ringing ice, heads down and tails\r\nup, while Kotuko struck up the \xe2\x80\x9cAn-gutivaun tai-na tau-na-ne taina\xe2\x80\x9d (The\r\nSong of the Returning Hunter), and voices hailed him from house to house\r\nunder all that dim, star-littern sky.\r\n\r\nWhen Kotuko the dog came to his full growth he enjoyed himself too. He\r\nfought his way up the team steadily, fight after fight, till one fine\r\nevening, over their food, he tackled the big, black leader (Kotuko the\r\nboy saw fair play), and made second dog of him, as they say. So he was\r\npromoted to the long thong of the leading dog, running five feet in\r\nadvance of all the others: it was his bounden duty to stop all fighting,\r\nin harness or out of it, and he wore a collar of copper wire, very thick\r\nand heavy. On special occasions he was fed with cooked food inside the\r\nhouse, and sometimes was allowed to sleep on the bench with Kotuko. He\r\nwas a good seal-dog, and would keep a musk-ox at bay by running round\r\nhim and snapping at his heels. He would even--and this for a sleigh-dog\r\nis the last proof of bravery--he would even stand up to the gaunt Arctic\r\nwolf, whom all dogs of the North, as a rule, fear beyond anything\r\nthat walks the snow. He and his master--they did not count the team of\r\nordinary dogs as company--hunted together, day after day and night\r\nafter night, fur-wrapped boy and savage, long-haired, narrow-eyed,\r\nwhite-fanged, yellow brute. All an Inuit has to do is to get food and\r\nskins for himself and his family. The women-folk make the skins into\r\nclothing, and occasionally help in trapping small game; but the bulk\r\nof the food--and they eat enormously--must be found by the men. If the\r\nsupply fails there is no one up there to buy or beg or borrow from. The\r\npeople must die.\r\n\r\nAn Inuit does not think of these chances till he is forced to. Kadlu,\r\nKotuko, Amoraq, and the boy-baby who kicked about in Amoraq\xe2\x80\x99s fur hood\r\nand chewed pieces of blubber all day, were as happy together as any\r\nfamily in the world. They came of a very gentle race--an Inuit seldom\r\nloses his temper, and almost never strikes a child--who did not know\r\nexactly what telling a real lie meant, still less how to steal. They\r\nwere content to spear their living out of the heart of the bitter,\r\nhopeless cold; to smile oily smiles, and tell queer ghost and fairy\r\ntales of evenings, and eat till they could eat no more, and sing the\r\nendless woman\xe2\x80\x99s song: \xe2\x80\x9cAmna aya, aya amna, ah! ah!\xe2\x80\x9d through the long\r\nlamp-lighted days as they mended their clothes and their hunting-gear.\r\n\r\nBut one terrible winter everything betrayed them. The Tununirmiut\r\nreturned from the yearly salmon-fishing, and made their houses on the\r\nearly ice to the north of Bylot\xe2\x80\x99s Island, ready to go after the seal\r\nas soon as the sea froze. But it was an early and savage autumn. All\r\nthrough September there were continuous gales that broke up the smooth\r\nseal-ice when it was only four or five feet thick, and forced it inland,\r\nand piled a great barrier, some twenty miles broad, of lumped and ragged\r\nand needly ice, over which it was impossible to draw the dog-sleighs.\r\nThe edge of the floe off which the seal were used to fish in winter\r\nlay perhaps twenty miles beyond this barrier, and out of reach of the\r\nTununirmiut. Even so, they might have managed to scrape through the\r\nwinter on their stock of frozen salmon and stored blubber, and what\r\nthe traps gave them, but in December one of their hunters came across a\r\ntupik (a skin-tent) of three women and a girl nearly dead, whose men\r\nhad come down from the far North and been crushed in their little skin\r\nhunting-boats while they were out after the long-horned narwhal. Kadlu,\r\nof course, could only distribute the women among the huts of the winter\r\nvillage, for no Inuit dare refuse a meal to a stranger. He never knows\r\nwhen his own turn may come to beg. Amoraq took the girl, who was about\r\nfourteen, into her own house as a sort of servant. From the cut of her\r\nsharp-pointed hood, and the long diamond pattern of her white deer-skin\r\nleggings, they supposed she came from Ellesmere Land. She had never seen\r\ntin cooking-pots or wooden-shod sleighs before; but Kotuko the boy and\r\nKotuko the dog were rather fond of her.\r\n\r\nThen all the foxes went south, and even the wolverine, that growling,\r\nblunt-headed little thief of the snow, did not take the trouble to\r\nfollow the line of empty traps that Kotuko set. The tribe lost a\r\ncouple of their best hunters, who were badly crippled in a fight with\r\na musk-ox, and this threw more work on the others. Kotuko went out, day\r\nafter day, with a light hunting-sleigh and six or seven of the strongest\r\ndogs, looking till his eyes ached for some patch of clear ice where\r\na seal might perhaps have scratched a breathing-hole. Kotuko the dog\r\nranged far and wide, and in the dead stillness of the ice-fields\r\nKotuko the boy could hear his half-choked whine of excitement, above a\r\nseal-hole three miles away, as plainly as though he were at his elbow.\r\nWhen the dog found a hole the boy would build himself a little, low snow\r\nwall to keep off the worst of the bitter wind, and there he would wait\r\nten, twelve, twenty hours for the seal to come up to breathe, his eyes\r\nglued to the tiny mark he had made above the hole to guide the downward\r\nthrust of his harpoon, a little seal-skin mat under his feet, and his\r\nlegs tied together in the tutareang (the buckle that the old hunters\r\nhad talked about). This helps to keep a man\xe2\x80\x99s legs from twitching as he\r\nwaits and waits and waits for the quick-eared seal to rise. Though there\r\nis no excitement in it, you can easily believe that the sitting still in\r\nthe buckle with the thermometer perhaps forty degrees below zero is\r\nthe hardest work an Inuit knows. When a seal was caught, Kotuko the dog\r\nwould bound forward, his trace trailing behind him, and help to pull the\r\nbody to the sleigh, where the tired and hungry dogs lay sullenly under\r\nthe lee of the broken ice.\r\n\r\nA seal did not go very far, for each mouth in the little village had a\r\nright to be filled, and neither bone, hide, nor sinew was wasted. The\r\ndogs\xe2\x80\x99 meat was taken for human use, and Amoraq fed the team with pieces\r\nof old summer skin-tents raked out from under the sleeping-bench, and\r\nthey howled and howled again, and waked to howl hungrily. One could\r\ntell by the soap-stone lamps in the huts that famine was near. In good\r\nseasons, when blubber was plentiful, the light in the boat-shaped lamps\r\nwould be two feet high--cheerful, oily, and yellow. Now it was a\r\nbare six inches: Amoraq carefully pricked down the moss wick, when an\r\nunwatched flame brightened for a moment, and the eyes of all the family\r\nfollowed her hand. The horror of famine up there in the great cold is\r\nnot so much dying, as dying in the dark. All the Inuit dread the dark\r\nthat presses on them without a break for six months in each year; and\r\nwhen the lamps are low in the houses the minds of people begin to be\r\nshaken and confused.\r\n\r\nBut worse was to come.\r\n\r\nThe underfed dogs snapped and growled in the passages, glaring at the\r\ncold stars, and snuffing into the bitter wind, night after night. When\r\nthey stopped howling the silence fell down again as solid and heavy as a\r\nsnowdrift against a door, and men could hear the beating of their blood\r\nin the thin passages of the ear, and the thumping of their own hearts,\r\nthat sounded as loud as the noise of sorcerers\xe2\x80\x99 drums beaten across\r\nthe snow. One night Kotuko the dog, who had been unusually sullen in\r\nharness, leaped up and pushed his head against Kotuko\xe2\x80\x99s knee. Kotuko\r\npatted him, but the dog still pushed blindly forward, fawning. Then\r\nKadlu waked, and gripped the heavy wolf-like head, and stared into the\r\nglassy eyes. The dog whimpered and shivered between Kadlu\xe2\x80\x99s knees. The\r\nhair rose about his neck, and he growled as though a stranger were at\r\nthe door; then he barked joyously, and rolled on the ground, and bit at\r\nKotuko\xe2\x80\x99s boot like a puppy.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat is it?\xe2\x80\x9d said Kotuko; for he was beginning to be afraid.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe sickness,\xe2\x80\x9d Kadlu answered. \xe2\x80\x9cIt is the dog sickness.\xe2\x80\x9d Kotuko the dog\r\nlifted his nose and howled and howled again.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have not seen this before. What will he do?\xe2\x80\x9d said Kotuko.\r\n\r\nKadlu shrugged one shoulder a little, and crossed the hut for his short\r\nstabbing-harpoon. The big dog looked at him, howled again, and slunk\r\naway down the passage, while the other dogs drew aside right and left to\r\ngive him ample room. When he was out on the snow he barked furiously, as\r\nthough on the trail of a musk-ox, and, barking and leaping and frisking,\r\npassed out of sight. His trouble was not hydrophobia, but simple, plain\r\nmadness. The cold and the hunger, and, above all, the dark, had turned\r\nhis head; and when the terrible dog-sickness once shows itself in a\r\nteam, it spreads like wild-fire. Next hunting-day another dog sickened,\r\nand was killed then and there by Kotuko as he bit and struggled among\r\nthe traces. Then the black second dog, who had been the leader in the\r\nold days, suddenly gave tongue on an imaginary reindeer-track, and when\r\nthey slipped him from the pitu he flew at the throat of an ice-cliff,\r\nand ran away as his leader had done, his harness on his back. After that\r\nno one would take the dogs out again. They needed them for something\r\nelse, and the dogs knew it; and though they were tied down and fed by\r\nhand, their eyes were full of despair and fear. To make things worse,\r\nthe old women began to tell ghost-tales, and to say that they had met\r\nthe spirits of the dead hunters lost that autumn, who prophesied all\r\nsorts of horrible things.\r\n\r\nKotuko grieved more for the loss of his dog than anything else; for\r\nthough an Inuit eats enormously he also knows how to starve. But the\r\nhunger, the darkness, the cold, and the exposure told on his strength,\r\nand he began to hear voices inside his head, and to see people who\r\nwere not there, out of the tail of his eye. One night--he had unbuckled\r\nhimself after ten hours\xe2\x80\x99 waiting above a \xe2\x80\x9cblind\xe2\x80\x9d seal-hole, and was\r\nstaggering back to the village faint and dizzy--he halted to lean\r\nhis back against a boulder which happened to be supported like a\r\nrocking-stone on a single jutting point of ice. His weight disturbed the\r\nbalance of the thing, it rolled over ponderously, and as Kotuko\r\nsprang aside to avoid it, slid after him, squeaking and hissing on the\r\nice-slope.\r\n\r\nThat was enough for Kotuko. He had been brought up to believe that every\r\nrock and boulder had its owner (its inua), who was generally a one-eyed\r\nkind of a Woman-Thing called a tornaq, and that when a tornaq meant to\r\nhelp a man she rolled after him inside her stone house, and asked him\r\nwhether he would take her for a guardian spirit. (In summer thaws the\r\nice-propped rocks and boulders roll and slip all over the face of the\r\nland, so you can easily see how the idea of live stones arose.) Kotuko\r\nheard the blood beating in his ears as he had heard it all day, and\r\nhe thought that was the tornaq of the stone speaking to him. Before he\r\nreached home he was quite certain that he had held a long conversation\r\nwith her, and as all his people believed that this was quite possible,\r\nno one contradicted him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe said to me, \xe2\x80\x98I jump down, I jump down from my place on the snow,\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n cried Kotuko, with hollow eyes, leaning forward in the half-lighted hut.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe said, \xe2\x80\x98I will be a guide.\xe2\x80\x99 She said, \xe2\x80\x98I will guide you to the good\r\nseal-holes.\xe2\x80\x99 To-morrow I go out, and the tornaq will guide me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThen the angekok, the village sorcerer, came in, and Kotuko told him the\r\ntale a second time. It lost nothing in the telling.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFollow the tornait [the spirits of the stones], and they will bring us\r\nfood again,\xe2\x80\x9d said the angekok.\r\n\r\nNow the girl from the North had been lying near the lamp, eating very\r\nlittle and saying less for days past; but when Amoraq and Kadlu next\r\nmorning packed and lashed a little hand-sleigh for Kotuko, and loaded it\r\nwith his hunting-gear and as much blubber and frozen seal-meat as they\r\ncould spare, she took the pulling-rope, and stepped out boldly at the\r\nboy\xe2\x80\x99s side.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYour house is my house,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, as the little bone-shod sleigh\r\nsqueaked and bumped behind them in the awful Arctic night.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy house is your house,\xe2\x80\x9d said Kotuko; \xe2\x80\x9cbut I think that we shall both\r\ngo to Sedna together.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nNow Sedna is the Mistress of the Underworld, and the Inuit believe that\r\nevery one who dies must spend a year in her horrible country before\r\ngoing to Quadliparmiut, the Happy Place, where it never freezes and the\r\nfat reindeer trot up when you call.\r\n\r\nThrough the village people were shouting: \xe2\x80\x9cThe tornait have spoken to\r\nKotuko. They will show him open ice. He will bring us the seal again!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n Their voices were soon swallowed up by the cold, empty dark, and\r\nKotuko and the girl shouldered close together as they strained on the\r\npulling-rope or humoured the sleigh through the ice in the direction of\r\nthe Polar Sea. Kotuko insisted that the tornaq of the stone had told him\r\nto go north, and north they went under Tuktuqdjung the Reindeer--those\r\nstars that we call the Great Bear.\r\n\r\nNo European could have made five miles a day over the ice-rubbish and\r\nthe sharp-edged drifts; but those two knew exactly the turn of the wrist\r\nthat coaxes a sleigh round a hummock, the jerk that nearly lifts it\r\nout of an ice-crack, and the exact strength that goes to the few quiet\r\nstrokes of the spear-head that make a path possible when everything\r\nlooks hopeless.\r\n\r\nThe girl said nothing, but bowed her head, and the long wolverine-fur\r\nfringe of her ermine hood blew across her broad, dark face. The sky\r\nabove them was an intense velvety black, changing to bands of Indian\r\nred on the horizon, where the great stars burned like street-lamps. From\r\ntime to time a greenish wave of the Northern Lights would roll across\r\nthe hollow of the high heavens, flick like a flag, and disappear; or\r\na meteor would crackle from darkness to darkness, trailing a shower of\r\nsparks behind. Then they could see the ridged and furrowed surface of\r\nthe floe tipped and laced with strange colours--red, copper, and bluish;\r\nbut in the ordinary starlight everything turned to one frost-bitten\r\ngray. The floe, as you will remember, had been battered and tormented by\r\nthe autumn gales till it was one frozen earthquake. There were gullies\r\nand ravines, and holes like gravel-pits, cut in ice; lumps and scattered\r\npieces frozen down to the original floor of the floe; blotches of old\r\nblack ice that had been thrust under the floe in some gale and heaved\r\nup again; roundish boulders of ice; saw-like edges of ice carved by the\r\nsnow that flies before the wind; and sunken pits where thirty or forty\r\nacres lay below the level of the rest of the field. From a little\r\ndistance you might have taken the lumps for seal or walrus, overturned\r\nsleighs or men on a hunting expedition, or even the great Ten-legged\r\nWhite Spirit-Bear himself; but in spite of these fantastic shapes, all\r\non the very edge of starting into life, there was neither sound nor the\r\nleast faint echo of sound. And through this silence and through this\r\nwaste, where the sudden lights flapped and went out again, the sleigh\r\nand the two that pulled it crawled like things in a nightmare--a\r\nnightmare of the end of the world at the end of the world.\r\n\r\nWhen they were tired Kotuko would make what the hunters call a\r\n\xe2\x80\x9chalf-house,\xe2\x80\x9d a very small snow hut, into which they would huddle with\r\nthe travelling-lamp, and try to thaw out the frozen seal-meat. When they\r\nhad slept, the march began again--thirty miles a day to get ten miles\r\nnorthward. The girl was always very silent, but Kotuko muttered\r\nto himself and broke out into songs he had learned in the\r\nSinging-House--summer songs, and reindeer and salmon songs--all horribly\r\nout of place at that season. He would declare that he heard the tornaq\r\ngrowling to him, and would run wildly up a hummock, tossing his arms and\r\nspeaking in loud, threatening tones. To tell the truth, Kotuko was very\r\nnearly crazy for the time being; but the girl was sure that he was being\r\nguided by his guardian spirit, and that everything would come right.\r\nShe was not surprised, therefore, when at the end of the fourth march\r\nKotuko, whose eyes were burning like fire-balls in his head, told her\r\nthat his tornaq was following them across the snow in the shape of a\r\ntwo-headed dog. The girl looked where Kotuko pointed, and something\r\nseemed to slip into a ravine. It was certainly not human, but everybody\r\nknew that the tornait preferred to appear in the shape of bear and seal,\r\nand such like.\r\n\r\nIt might have been the Ten-legged White Spirit-Bear himself, or it might\r\nhave been anything, for Kotuko and the girl were so starved that their\r\neyes were untrustworthy. They had trapped nothing, and seen no trace of\r\ngame since they had left the village; their food would not hold out for\r\nanother week, and there was a gale coming. A Polar storm can blow for\r\nten days without a break, and all that while it is certain death to\r\nbe abroad. Kotuko laid up a snow-house large enough to take in the\r\nhand-sleigh (never be separated from your meat), and while he was\r\nshaping the last irregular block of ice that makes the key-stone of the\r\nroof, he saw a Thing looking at him from a little cliff of ice half a\r\nmile away. The air was hazy, and the Thing seemed to be forty feet long\r\nand ten feet high, with twenty feet of tail and a shape that quivered\r\nall along the outlines. The girl saw it too, but instead of crying aloud\r\nwith terror, said quietly, \xe2\x80\x9cThat is Quiquern. What comes after?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe will speak to me,\xe2\x80\x9d said Kotuko; but the snow-knife trembled in his\r\nhand as he spoke, because however much a man may believe that he is a\r\nfriend of strange and ugly spirits, he seldom likes to be taken quite\r\nat his word. Quiquern, too, is the phantom of a gigantic toothless\r\ndog without any hair, who is supposed to live in the far North, and to\r\nwander about the country just before things are going to happen. They\r\nmay be pleasant or unpleasant things, but not even the sorcerers care to\r\nspeak about Quiquern. He makes the dogs go mad. Like the Spirit-Bear, he\r\nhas several extra pairs of legs,--six or eight,--and this Thing jumping\r\nup and down in the haze had more legs than any real dog needed. Kotuko\r\nand the girl huddled into their hut quickly. Of course if Quiquern had\r\nwanted them, he could have torn it to pieces above their heads, but the\r\nsense of a foot-thick snow-wall between themselves and the wicked dark\r\nwas great comfort. The gale broke with a shriek of wind like the shriek\r\nof a train, and for three days and three nights it held, never varying\r\none point, and never lulling even for a minute. They fed the stone lamp\r\nbetween their knees, and nibbled at the half-warm seal-meat, and watched\r\nthe black soot gather on the roof for seventy-two long hours. The girl\r\ncounted up the food in the sleigh; there was not more than two days\xe2\x80\x99\r\nsupply, and Kotuko looked over the iron heads and the deer-sinew\r\nfastenings of his harpoon and his seal-lance and his bird-dart. There\r\nwas nothing else to do.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe shall go to Sedna soon--very soon,\xe2\x80\x9d the girl whispered. \xe2\x80\x9cIn three\r\ndays we shall lie down and go. Will your tornaq do nothing? Sing her an\r\nangekok\xe2\x80\x99s song to make her come here.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe began to sing in the high-pitched howl of the magic songs, and the\r\ngale went down slowly. In the middle of his song the girl started, laid\r\nher mittened hand and then her head to the ice floor of the hut. Kotuko\r\nfollowed her example, and the two kneeled, staring into each other\xe2\x80\x99s\r\neyes, and listening with every nerve. He ripped a thin sliver of\r\nwhalebone from the rim of a bird-snare that lay on the sleigh, and,\r\nafter straightening, set it upright in a little hole in the ice, firming\r\nit down with his mitten. It was almost as delicately adjusted as a\r\ncompass-needle, and now instead of listening they watched. The thin rod\r\nquivered a little--the least little jar in the world; then it vibrated\r\nsteadily for a few seconds, came to rest, and vibrated again, this time\r\nnodding to another point of the compass.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cToo soon!\xe2\x80\x9d said Kotuko. \xe2\x80\x9cSome big floe has broken far away outside.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe girl pointed at the rod, and shook her head. \xe2\x80\x9cIt is the big\r\nbreaking,\xe2\x80\x9d she said. \xe2\x80\x9cListen to the ground-ice. It knocks.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWhen they kneeled this time they heard the most curious muffled grunts\r\nand knockings, apparently under their feet. Sometimes it sounded as\r\nthough a blind puppy were squeaking above the lamp; then as if a stone\r\nwere being ground on hard ice; and again, like muffled blows on a drum;\r\nbut all dragged out and made small, as though they travelled through a\r\nlittle horn a weary distance away.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe shall not go to Sedna lying down,\xe2\x80\x9d said Kotuko. \xe2\x80\x9cIt is the breaking.\r\nThe tornaq has cheated us. We shall die.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAll this may sound absurd enough, but the two were face to face with\r\na very real danger. The three days\xe2\x80\x99 gale had driven the deep water of\r\nBaffin\xe2\x80\x99s Bay southerly, and piled it on to the edge of the far-reaching\r\nland-ice that stretches from Bylot\xe2\x80\x99s Island to the west. Also, the\r\nstrong current which sets east out of Lancaster Sound carried with it\r\nmile upon mile of what they call pack-ice--rough ice that has not frozen\r\ninto fields; and this pack was bombarding the floe at the same time\r\nthat the swell and heave of the storm-worked sea was weakening and\r\nundermining it. What Kotuko and the girl had been listening to were the\r\nfaint echoes of that fight thirty or forty miles away, and the little\r\ntell-tale rod quivered to the shock of it.\r\n\r\nNow, as the Inuit say, when the ice once wakes after its long winter\r\nsleep, there is no knowing what may happen, for solid floe-ice changes\r\nshape almost as quickly as a cloud. The gale was evidently a spring gale\r\nsent out of time, and anything was possible.\r\n\r\nYet the two were happier in their minds than before. If the floe broke\r\nup there would be no more waiting and suffering. Spirits, goblins, and\r\nwitch-people were moving about on the racking ice, and they might find\r\nthemselves stepping into Sedna\xe2\x80\x99s country side by side with all sorts of\r\nwild Things, the flush of excitement still on them. When they left the\r\nhut after the gale, the noise on the horizon was steadily growing, and\r\nthe tough ice moaned and buzzed all round them.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is still waiting,\xe2\x80\x9d said Kotuko.\r\n\r\nOn the top of a hummock sat or crouched the eight-legged Thing that they\r\nhad seen three days before--and it howled horribly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLet us follow,\xe2\x80\x9d said the girl. \xe2\x80\x9cIt may know some way that does not lead\r\nto Sedna\xe2\x80\x9d; but she reeled from weakness as she took the pulling-rope.\r\nThe Thing moved off slowly and clumsily across the ridges, heading\r\nalways toward the westward and the land, and they followed, while the\r\ngrowling thunder at the edge of the floe rolled nearer and nearer. The\r\nfloe\xe2\x80\x99s lip was split and cracked in every direction for three or four\r\nmiles inland, and great pans of ten-foot-thick ice, from a few yards\r\nto twenty acres square, were jolting and ducking and surging into one\r\nanother, and into the yet unbroken floe, as the heavy swell took and\r\nshook and spouted between them. This battering-ram ice was, so to speak,\r\nthe first army that the sea was flinging against the floe. The incessant\r\ncrash and jar of these cakes almost drowned the ripping sound of sheets\r\nof pack-ice driven bodily under the floe as cards are hastily pushed\r\nunder a tablecloth. Where the water was shallow these sheets would be\r\npiled one atop of the other till the bottommost touched mud fifty feet\r\ndown, and the discoloured sea banked behind the muddy ice till the\r\nincreasing pressure drove all forward again. In addition to the floe and\r\nthe pack-ice, the gale and the currents were bringing down true bergs,\r\nsailing mountains of ice, snapped off from the Greenland side of the\r\nwater or the north shore of Melville Bay. They pounded in solemnly,\r\nthe waves breaking white round them, and advanced on the floe like an\r\nold-time fleet under full sail. A berg that seemed ready to carry the\r\nworld before it would ground helplessly in deep water, reel over, and\r\nwallow in a lather of foam and mud and flying frozen spray, while a much\r\nsmaller and lower one would rip and ride into the flat floe, flinging\r\ntons of ice on either side, and cutting a track half a mile long before\r\nit was stopped. Some fell like swords, shearing a raw-edged canal;\r\nand others splintered into a shower of blocks, weighing scores of tons\r\napiece, that whirled and skirted among the hummocks. Others, again, rose\r\nup bodily out of the water when they shoaled, twisted as though in\r\npain, and fell solidly on their sides, while the sea threshed over their\r\nshoulders. This trampling and crowding and bending and buckling and\r\narching of the ice into every possible shape was going on as far as the\r\neye could reach all along the north line of the floe. From where\r\nKotuko and the girl were, the confusion looked no more than an uneasy,\r\nrippling, crawling movement under the horizon; but it came toward them\r\neach moment, and they could hear, far away to landward a heavy booming,\r\nas it might have been the boom of artillery through a fog. That showed\r\nthat the floe was being jammed home against the iron cliffs of Bylot\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nIsland, the land to the southward behind them.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThis has never been before,\xe2\x80\x9d said Kotuko, staring stupidly. \xe2\x80\x9cThis is\r\nnot the time. How can the floe break NOW?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFollow THAT!\xe2\x80\x9d the girl cried, pointing to the Thing half limping,\r\nhalf running distractedly before them. They followed, tugging at the\r\nhand-sleigh, while nearer and nearer came the roaring march of the ice.\r\nAt last the fields round them cracked and starred in every direction,\r\nand the cracks opened and snapped like the teeth of wolves. But where\r\nthe Thing rested, on a mound of old and scattered ice-blocks some fifty\r\nfeet high, there was no motion. Kotuko leaped forward wildly, dragging\r\nthe girl after him, and crawled to the bottom of the mound. The talking\r\nof the ice grew louder and louder round them, but the mound stayed fast,\r\nand, as the girl looked at him, he threw his right elbow upward and\r\noutward, making the Inuit sign for land in the shape of an island. And\r\nland it was that the eight-legged, limping Thing had led them to--some\r\ngranite-tipped, sand-beached islet off the coast, shod and sheathed and\r\nmasked with ice so that no man could have told it from the floe, but at\r\nthe bottom solid earth, and not shifting ice! The smashing and rebound\r\nof the floes as they grounded and splintered marked the borders of it,\r\nand a friendly shoal ran out to the northward, and turned aside the rush\r\nof the heaviest ice, exactly as a ploughshare turns over loam. There was\r\ndanger, of course, that some heavily squeezed ice-field might shoot up\r\nthe beach, and plane off the top of the islet bodily; but that did not\r\ntrouble Kotuko and the girl when they made their snow-house and began\r\nto eat, and heard the ice hammer and skid along the beach. The Thing\r\nhad disappeared, and Kotuko was talking excitedly about his power over\r\nspirits as he crouched round the lamp. In the middle of his wild sayings\r\nthe girl began to laugh, and rock herself backward and forward.\r\n\r\nBehind her shoulder, crawling into the hut crawl by crawl, there were\r\ntwo heads, one yellow and one black, that belonged to two of the most\r\nsorrowful and ashamed dogs that ever you saw. Kotuko the dog was one,\r\nand the black leader was the other. Both were now fat, well-looking, and\r\nquite restored to their proper minds, but coupled to each other in an\r\nextraordinary fashion. When the black leader ran off, you remember, his\r\nharness was still on him. He must have met Kotuko the dog, and played or\r\nfought with him, for his shoulder-loop had caught in the plaited copper\r\nwire of Kotuko\xe2\x80\x99s collar, and had drawn tight, so that neither could get\r\nat the trace to gnaw it apart, but each was fastened sidelong to\r\nhis neighbour\xe2\x80\x99s neck. That, with the freedom of hunting on their own\r\naccount, must have helped to cure their madness. They were very sober.\r\n\r\nThe girl pushed the two shamefaced creatures towards Kotuko, and,\r\nsobbing with laughter, cried, \xe2\x80\x9cThat is Quiquern, who led us to safe\r\nground. Look at his eight legs and double head!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nKotuko cut them free, and they fell into his arms, yellow and black\r\ntogether, trying to explain how they had got their senses back again.\r\nKotuko ran a hand down their ribs, which were round and well clothed.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey have found food,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, with a grin. \xe2\x80\x9cI do not think we shall\r\ngo to Sedna so soon. My tornaq sent these. The sickness has left them.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAs soon as they had greeted Kotuko, these two, who had been forced to\r\nsleep and eat and hunt together for the past few weeks, flew at each\r\nother\xe2\x80\x99s throat, and there was a beautiful battle in the snow-house.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cEmpty dogs do not fight,\xe2\x80\x9d Kotuko said. \xe2\x80\x9cThey have found the seal. Let\r\nus sleep. We shall find food.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWhen they waked there was open water on the north beach of the island,\r\nand all the loosened ice had been driven landward. The first sound of\r\nthe surf is one of the most delightful that the Inuit can hear, for it\r\nmeans that spring is on the road. Kotuko and the girl took hold of hands\r\nand smiled, for the clear, full roar of the surge among the ice\r\nreminded them of salmon and reindeer time and the smell of blossoming\r\nground-willows. Even as they looked, the sea began to skim over between\r\nthe floating cakes of ice, so intense was the cold; but on the horizon\r\nthere was a vast red glare, and that was the light of the sunken sun.\r\nIt was more like hearing him yawn in his sleep than seeing him rise, and\r\nthe glare lasted for only a few minutes, but it marked the turn of the\r\nyear. Nothing, they felt, could alter that.\r\n\r\nKotuko found the dogs fighting over a fresh-killed seal who was\r\nfollowing the fish that a gale always disturbs. He was the first of some\r\ntwenty or thirty seal that landed on the island in the course of the\r\nday, and till the sea froze hard there were hundreds of keen black heads\r\nrejoicing in the shallow free water and floating about with the floating\r\nice.\r\n\r\nIt was good to eat seal-liver again; to fill the lamps recklessly with\r\nblubber, and watch the flame blaze three feet in the air; but as soon\r\nas the new sea-ice bore, Kotuko and the girl loaded the hand-sleigh, and\r\nmade the two dogs pull as they had never pulled in their lives, for they\r\nfeared what might have happened in their village. The weather was as\r\npitiless as usual; but it is easier to draw a sleigh loaded with good\r\nfood than to hunt starving. They left five-and-twenty seal carcasses\r\nburied in the ice of the beach, all ready for use, and hurried back to\r\ntheir people. The dogs showed them the way as soon as Kotuko told them\r\nwhat was expected, and though there was no sign of a landmark, in two\r\ndays they were giving tongue outside Kadlu\xe2\x80\x99s house. Only three dogs\r\nanswered them; the others had been eaten, and the houses were all dark.\r\nBut when Kotuko shouted, \xe2\x80\x9cOjo!\xe2\x80\x9d (boiled meat), weak voices replied, and\r\nwhen he called the muster of the village name by name, very distinctly,\r\nthere were no gaps in it.\r\n\r\nAn hour later the lamps blazed in Kadlu\xe2\x80\x99s house; snow-water was heating;\r\nthe pots were beginning to simmer, and the snow was dripping from the\r\nroof, as Amoraq made ready a meal for all the village, and the boy-baby\r\nin the hood chewed at a strip of rich nutty blubber, and the hunters\r\nslowly and methodically filled themselves to the very brim with\r\nseal-meat. Kotuko and the girl told their tale. The two dogs sat between\r\nthem, and whenever their names came in, they cocked an ear apiece and\r\nlooked most thoroughly ashamed of themselves. A dog who has once gone\r\nmad and recovered, the Inuit say, is safe against all further attacks.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo the tornaq did not forget us,\xe2\x80\x9d said Kotuko. \xe2\x80\x9cThe storm blew, the ice\r\nbroke, and the seal swam in behind the fish that were frightened by the\r\nstorm. Now the new seal-holes are not two days distant. Let the good\r\nhunters go to-morrow and bring back the seal I have speared--twenty-five\r\nseal buried in the ice. When we have eaten those we will all follow the\r\nseal on the floe.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat do YOU do?\xe2\x80\x9d said the sorcerer in the same sort of voice as he used\r\nto Kadlu, richest of the Tununirmiut.\r\n\r\nKadlu looked at the girl from the North, and said quietly, \xe2\x80\x9cWE build a\r\nhouse.\xe2\x80\x9d He pointed to the north-west side of Kadlu\xe2\x80\x99s house, for that is\r\nthe side on which the married son or daughter always lives.\r\n\r\nThe girl turned her hands palm upward, with a little despairing shake\r\nof her head. She was a foreigner, picked up starving, and could bring\r\nnothing to the housekeeping.\r\n\r\nAmoraq jumped from the bench where she sat, and began to sweep things\r\ninto the girl\xe2\x80\x99s lap--stone lamps, iron skin-scrapers, tin kettles,\r\ndeer-skins embroidered with musk-ox teeth, and real canvas-needles such\r\nas sailors use--the finest dowry that has ever been given on the far\r\nedge of the Arctic Circle, and the girl from the North bowed her head\r\ndown to the very floor.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAlso these!\xe2\x80\x9d said Kotuko, laughing and signing to the dogs, who thrust\r\ntheir cold muzzles into the girl\xe2\x80\x99s face.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh,\xe2\x80\x9d said the angekok, with an important cough, as though he had been\r\nthinking it all over. \xe2\x80\x9cAs soon as Kotuko left the village I went to the\r\nSinging-House and sang magic. I sang all the long nights, and called\r\nupon the Spirit of the Reindeer. MY singing made the gale blow that\r\nbroke the ice and drew the two dogs toward Kotuko when the ice would\r\nhave crushed his bones. MY song drew the seal in behind the broken ice.\r\nMy body lay still in the quaggi, but my spirit ran about on the ice, and\r\nguided Kotuko and the dogs in all the things they did. I did it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nEverybody was full and sleepy, so no one contradicted; and the angekok,\r\nby virtue of his office, helped himself to yet another lump of boiled\r\nmeat, and lay down to sleep with the others in the warm, well-lighted,\r\noil-smelling home.\r\n\r\n*****\r\n\r\nNow Kotuko, who drew very well in the Inuit fashion, scratched pictures\r\nof all these adventures on a long, flat piece of ivory with a hole at\r\none end. When he and the girl went north to Ellesmere Land in the year\r\nof the Wonderful Open Winter, he left the picture-story with Kadlu, who\r\nlost it in the shingle when his dog-sleigh broke down one summer on the\r\nbeach of Lake Netilling at Nikosiring, and there a Lake Inuit found\r\nit next spring and sold it to a man at Imigen who was interpreter on a\r\nCumberland Sound whaler, and he sold it to Hans Olsen, who was afterward\r\na quartermaster on board a big steamer that took tourists to the North\r\nCape in Norway. When the tourist season was over, the steamer ran\r\nbetween London and Australia, stopping at Ceylon, and there Olsen sold\r\nthe ivory to a Cingalese jeweller for two imitation sapphires. I found\r\nit under some rubbish in a house at Colombo, and have translated it from\r\none end to the other.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98ANGUTIVAUN TAINA\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n[This is a very free translation of the Song of the Returning Hunter,\r\nas the men used to sing it after seal-spearing. The Inuit always repeat\r\nthings over and over again.]\r\n\r\n      Our gloves are stiff with the frozen blood,\r\n        Our furs with the drifted snow,\r\n      As we come in with the seal--the seal!\r\n        In from the edge of the floe.\r\n\r\n      Au jana! Aua! Oha! Haq!\r\n        And the yelping dog-teams go,\r\n      And the long whips crack, and the men come back,\r\n        Back from the edge of the floe!\r\n\r\n      We tracked our seal to his secret place,\r\n        We heard him scratch below,\r\n      We made our mark, and we watched beside,\r\n        Out on the edge of the floe.\r\n\r\n      We raised our lance when he rose to breathe,\r\n        We drove it downward--so!\r\n      And we played him thus, and we killed him thus,\r\n        Out on the edge of the floe.\r\n\r\n      Our gloves are glued with the frozen blood,\r\n        Our eyes with the drifting snow;\r\n      But we come back to our wives again,\r\n        Back from the edge of the floe!\r\n\r\n      Au jana! Aua! Oha! Haq!\r\n        And the loaded dog-teams go,\r\n      And the wives can hear their men come back.\r\n        Back from the edge of the floe!\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nRED DOG\r\n\r\n     For our white and our excellent nights---for the nights of\r\n           swift running.\r\n         Fair ranging, far seeing, good hunting, sure cunning!\r\n     For the smells of the dawning, untainted, ere dew has departed!\r\n     For the rush through the mist, and the quarry blind-started!\r\n     For the cry of our mates when the sambhur has wheeled and is\r\n           standing at bay,\r\n               For the risk and the riot of night!\r\n               For the sleep at the lair-mouth by day,\r\n               It is met, and we go to the fight.\r\n                                 Bay! O Bay!\r\n\r\nIt was after the letting in of the Jungle that the pleasantest part of\r\nMowgli\xe2\x80\x99s life began. He had the good conscience that comes from paying\r\ndebts; all the Jungle was his friend, and just a little afraid of him.\r\nThe things that he did and saw and heard when he was wandering from one\r\npeople to another, with or without his four companions, would make many\r\nmany stories, each as long as this one. So you will never be told how\r\nhe met the Mad Elephant of Mandla, who killed two-and-twenty bullocks\r\ndrawing eleven carts of coined silver to the Government Treasury,\r\nand scattered the shiny rupees in the dust; how he fought Jacala, the\r\nCrocodile, all one long night in the Marshes of the North, and broke his\r\nskinning-knife on the brute\xe2\x80\x99s back-plates; how he found a new and longer\r\nknife round the neck of a man who had been killed by a wild boar, and\r\nhow he tracked that boar and killed him as a fair price for the knife;\r\nhow he was caught up once in the Great Famine, by the moving of the\r\ndeer, and nearly crushed to death in the swaying hot herds; how he saved\r\nHathi the Silent from being once more trapped in a pit with a stake\r\nat the bottom, and how, next day, he himself fell into a very cunning\r\nleopard-trap, and how Hathi broke the thick wooden bars to pieces above\r\nhim; how he milked the wild buffaloes in the swamp, and how----\r\n\r\nBut we must tell one tale at a time. Father and Mother Wolf died, and\r\nMowgli rolled a big boulder against the mouth of their cave, and cried\r\nthe Death Song over them; Baloo grew very old and stiff, and even\r\nBagheera, whose nerves were steel and whose muscles were iron, was a\r\nshade slower on the kill than he had been. Akela turned from gray to\r\nmilky white with pure age; his ribs stuck out, and he walked as though\r\nhe had been made of wood, and Mowgli killed for him. But the young\r\nwolves, the children of the disbanded Seeonee Pack, throve and\r\nincreased, and when there were about forty of them, masterless,\r\nfull-voiced, clean-footed five-year-olds, Akela told them that they\r\nought to gather themselves together and follow the Law, and run under\r\none head, as befitted the Free People.\r\n\r\nThis was not a question in which Mowgli concerned himself, for, as he\r\nsaid, he had eaten sour fruit, and he knew the tree it hung from; but\r\nwhen Phao, son of Phaona (his father was the Gray Tracker in the days\r\nof Akela\xe2\x80\x99s headship), fought his way to the leadership of the Pack,\r\naccording to the Jungle Law, and the old calls and songs began to ring\r\nunder the stars once more, Mowgli came to the Council Rock for memory\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nsake. When he chose to speak the Pack waited till he had finished, and\r\nhe sat at Akela\xe2\x80\x99s side on the rock above Phao. Those were days of good\r\nhunting and good sleeping. No stranger cared to break into the jungles\r\nthat belonged to Mowgli\xe2\x80\x99s people, as they called the Pack, and the young\r\nwolves grew fat and strong, and there were many cubs to bring to the\r\nLooking-over. Mowgli always attended a Looking-over, remembering the\r\nnight when a black panther bought a naked brown baby into the pack,\r\nand the long call, \xe2\x80\x9cLook, look well, O Wolves,\xe2\x80\x9d made his heart flutter.\r\nOtherwise, he would be far away in the Jungle with his four brothers,\r\ntasting, touching, seeing, and feeling new things.\r\n\r\nOne twilight when he was trotting leisurely across the ranges to give\r\nAkela the half of a buck that he had killed, while the Four jogged\r\nbehind him, sparring a little, and tumbling one another over for joy of\r\nbeing alive, he heard a cry that had never been heard since the bad days\r\nof Shere Khan. It was what they call in the Jungle the pheeal, a hideous\r\nkind of shriek that the jackal gives when he is hunting behind a tiger,\r\nor when there is a big killing afoot. If you can imagine a mixture of\r\nhate, triumph, fear, and despair, with a kind of leer running through\r\nit, you will get some notion of the pheeal that rose and sank and\r\nwavered and quavered far away across the Waingunga. The Four stopped at\r\nonce, bristling and growling. Mowgli\xe2\x80\x99s hand went to his knife, and he\r\nchecked, the blood in his face, his eyebrows knotted.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere is no Striped One dare kill here,\xe2\x80\x9d he said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is not the cry of the Forerunner,\xe2\x80\x9d answered Gray Brother. \xe2\x80\x9cIt is\r\nsome great killing. Listen!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIt broke out again, half sobbing and half chuckling, just as though the\r\njackal had soft human lips. Then Mowgli drew deep breath, and ran to the\r\nCouncil Rock, overtaking on his way hurrying wolves of the Pack.\r\nPhao and Akela were on the Rock together, and below them, every nerve\r\nstrained, sat the others. The mothers and the cubs were cantering off to\r\ntheir lairs; for when the pheeal cries it is no time for weak things to\r\nbe abroad.\r\n\r\nThey could hear nothing except the Waingunga rushing and gurgling in\r\nthe dark, and the light evening winds among the tree-tops, till suddenly\r\nacross the river a wolf called. It was no wolf of the Pack, for they\r\nwere all at the Rock. The note changed to a long, despairing bay; and\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDhole!\xe2\x80\x9d it said, \xe2\x80\x9cDhole! dhole! dhole!\xe2\x80\x9d They heard tired feet on the\r\nrocks, and a gaunt wolf, streaked with red on his flanks, his right\r\nfore-paw useless, and his jaws white with foam, flung himself into the\r\ncircle and lay gasping at Mowgli\xe2\x80\x99s feet.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGood hunting! Under whose Headship?\xe2\x80\x9d said Phao gravely.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGood hunting! Won-tolla am I,\xe2\x80\x9d was the answer. He meant that he was\r\na solitary wolf, fending for himself, his mate, and his cubs in\r\nsome lonely lair, as do many wolves in the south. Won-tolla means an\r\nOutlier--one who lies out from any Pack. Then he panted, and they could\r\nsee his heart-beats shake him backward and forward.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat moves?\xe2\x80\x9d said Phao, for that is the question all the Jungle asks\r\nafter the pheeal cries.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe dhole, the dhole of the Dekkan--Red Dog, the Killer! They came\r\nnorth from the south saying the Dekkan was empty and killing out by the\r\nway. When this moon was new there were four to me--my mate and three\r\ncubs. She would teach them to kill on the grass plains, hiding to\r\ndrive the buck, as we do who are of the open. At midnight I heard them\r\ntogether, full tongue on the trail. At the dawn-wind I found them stiff\r\nin the grass--four, Free People, four when this moon was new. Then\r\nsought I my Blood-Right and found the dhole.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow many?\xe2\x80\x9d said Mowgli quickly; the Pack growled deep in their throats.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI do not know. Three of them will kill no more, but at the last they\r\ndrove me like the buck; on my three legs they drove me. Look, Free\r\nPeople!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe thrust out his mangled fore-foot, all dark with dried blood. There\r\nwere cruel bites low down on his side, and his throat was torn and\r\nworried.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cEat,\xe2\x80\x9d said Akela, rising up from the meat Mowgli had brought him, and\r\nthe Outlier flung himself on it.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThis shall be no loss,\xe2\x80\x9d he said humbly, when he had taken off the first\r\nedge of his hunger. \xe2\x80\x9cGive me a little strength, Free People, and I also\r\nwill kill. My lair is empty that was full when this moon was new, and\r\nthe Blood Debt is not all paid.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nPhao heard his teeth crack on a haunch-bone and grunted approvingly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe shall need those jaws,\xe2\x80\x9d said he. \xe2\x80\x9cWere there cubs with the dhole?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNay, nay. Red Hunters all: grown dogs of their Pack, heavy and strong\r\nfor all that they eat lizards in the Dekkan.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWhat Won-tolla had said meant that the dhole, the red hunting-dog of the\r\nDekkan, was moving to kill, and the Pack knew well that even the tiger\r\nwill surrender a new kill to the dhole. They drive straight through the\r\nJungle, and what they meet they pull down and tear to pieces. Though\r\nthey are not as big nor half as cunning as the wolf, they are very\r\nstrong and very numerous. The dhole, for instance, do not begin to call\r\nthemselves a pack till they are a hundred strong; whereas forty wolves\r\nmake a very fair pack indeed. Mowgli\xe2\x80\x99s wanderings had taken him to\r\nthe edge of the high grassy downs of the Dekkan, and he had seen the\r\nfearless dholes sleeping and playing and scratching themselves in the\r\nlittle hollows and tussocks that they use for lairs. He despised and\r\nhated them because they did not smell like the Free People, because they\r\ndid not live in caves, and, above all, because they had hair between\r\ntheir toes while he and his friends were clean-footed. But he knew, for\r\nHathi had told him, what a terrible thing a dhole hunting-pack was. Even\r\nHathi moves aside from their line, and until they are killed, or till\r\ngame is scarce, they will go forward.\r\n\r\nAkela knew something of the dholes, too, for he said to Mowgli quietly,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is better to die in a Full Pack than leaderless and alone. This is\r\ngood hunting, and--my last. But, as men live, thou hast very many more\r\nnights and days, Little Brother. Go north and lie down, and if any live\r\nafter the dhole has gone by he shall bring thee word of the fight.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mowgli, quite gravely, \xe2\x80\x9cmust I go to the marshes and catch\r\nlittle fish and sleep in a tree, or must I ask help of the Bandar-log\r\nand crack nuts, while the Pack fight below?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is to the death,\xe2\x80\x9d said Akela. \xe2\x80\x9cThou hast never met the dhole--the\r\nRed Killer. Even the Striped One----\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAowa! Aowa!\xe2\x80\x9d said Mowgli pettingly. \xe2\x80\x9cI have killed one striped ape, and\r\nsure am I in my stomach that Shere Khan would have left his own mate for\r\nmeat to the dhole if he had winded a pack across three ranges. Listen\r\nnow: There was a wolf, my father, and there was a wolf, my mother, and\r\nthere was an old gray wolf (not too wise: he is white now) was my father\r\nand my mother. Therefore I--\xe2\x80\x9d he raised his voice, \xe2\x80\x9cI say that when the\r\ndhole come, and if the dhole come, Mowgli and the Free People are of\r\none skin for that hunting; and I say, by the Bull that bought me--by the\r\nBull Bagheera paid for me in the old days which ye of the Pack do not\r\nremember--_I_ say, that the Trees and the River may hear and hold fast\r\nif I forget; _I_ say that this my knife shall be as a tooth to the\r\nPack--and I do not think it is so blunt. This is my Word which has gone\r\nfrom me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThou dost not know the dhole, man with a wolf\xe2\x80\x99s tongue,\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nWon-tolla. \xe2\x80\x9cI look only to clear the Blood Debt against them ere they\r\nhave me in many pieces. They move slowly, killing out as they go, but in\r\ntwo days a little strength will come back to me and I turn again for the\r\nBlood Debt. But for YE, Free People, my word is that ye go north and eat\r\nbut little for a while till the dhole are gone. There is no meat in this\r\nhunting.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHear the Outlier!\xe2\x80\x9d said Mowgli with a laugh. \xe2\x80\x9cFree People, we must go\r\nnorth and dig lizards and rats from the bank, lest by any chance we meet\r\nthe dhole. He must kill out our hunting-grounds, while we lie hid in the\r\nnorth till it please him to give us our own again. He is a dog--and the\r\npup of a dog--red, yellow-bellied, lairless, and haired between every\r\ntoe! He counts his cubs six and eight at the litter, as though he were\r\nChikai, the little leaping rat. Surely we must run away, Free People,\r\nand beg leave of the peoples of the north for the offal of dead cattle!\r\nYe know the saying: \xe2\x80\x98North are the vermin; south are the lice. WE are\r\nthe Jungle.\xe2\x80\x99 Choose ye, O choose. It is good hunting! For the Pack--for\r\nthe Full Pack--for the lair and the litter; for the in-kill and the\r\nout-kill; for the mate that drives the doe and the little, little cub\r\nwithin the cave; it is met!--it is met!--it is met!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe Pack answered with one deep, crashing bark that sounded in the night\r\nlike a big tree falling. \xe2\x80\x9cIt is met!\xe2\x80\x9d they cried. \xe2\x80\x9cStay with these,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n said Mowgli to the Four. \xe2\x80\x9cWe shall need every tooth. Phao and Akela must\r\nmake ready the battle. I go to count the dogs.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is death!\xe2\x80\x9d Won-tolla cried, half rising. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat can such a hairless\r\none do against the Red Dog? Even the Striped One, remember----\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThou art indeed an Outlier,\xe2\x80\x9d Mowgli called back; \xe2\x80\x9cbut we will speak\r\nwhen the dholes are dead. Good hunting all!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe hurried off into the darkness, wild with excitement, hardly looking\r\nwhere he set foot, and the natural consequence was that he tripped full\r\nlength over Kaa\xe2\x80\x99s great coils where the python lay watching a deer-path\r\nnear the river.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cKssha!\xe2\x80\x9d said Kaa angrily. \xe2\x80\x9cIs this jungle-work, to stamp and tramp and\r\nundo a night\xe2\x80\x99s hunting--when the game are moving so well, too?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe fault was mine,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mowgli, picking himself up. \xe2\x80\x9cIndeed I was\r\nseeking thee, Flathead, but each time we meet thou art longer and\r\nbroader by the length of my arm. There is none like thee in the Jungle,\r\nwise, old, strong, and most beautiful Kaa.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow whither does THIS trail lead?\xe2\x80\x9d Kaa\xe2\x80\x99s voice was gentler. \xe2\x80\x9cNot a\r\nmoon since there was a Manling with a knife threw stones at my head and\r\ncalled me bad little tree-cat names, because I lay asleep in the open.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAy, and turned every driven deer to all the winds, and Mowgli was\r\nhunting, and this same Flathead was too deaf to hear his whistle, and\r\nleave the deer-roads free,\xe2\x80\x9d Mowgli answered composedly, sitting down\r\namong the painted coils.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow this same Manling comes with soft, tickling words to this same\r\nFlathead, telling him that he is wise and strong and beautiful, and\r\nthis same old Flathead believes and makes a place, thus, for this same\r\nstone-throwing Manling, and--Art thou at ease now? Could Bagheera give\r\nthee so good a resting-place?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nKaa had, as usual, made a sort of soft half-hammock of himself under\r\nMowgli\xe2\x80\x99s weight. The boy reached out in the darkness, and gathered in\r\nthe supple cable-like neck till Kaa\xe2\x80\x99s head rested on his shoulder, and\r\nthen he told him all that had happened in the Jungle that night.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWise I may be,\xe2\x80\x9d said Kaa at the end; \xe2\x80\x9cbut deaf I surely am. Else I\r\nshould have heard the pheeal. Small wonder the Eaters of Grass are\r\nuneasy. How many be the dhole?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have not yet seen. I came hot-foot to thee. Thou art older than\r\nHathi. But oh, Kaa,\xe2\x80\x9d--here Mowgli wriggled with sheerjoy,--\xe2\x80\x9cit will be\r\ngood hunting. Few of us will see another moon.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDost THOU strike in this? Remember thou art a Man; and remember what\r\nPack cast thee out. Let the Wolf look to the Dog. THOU art a Man.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLast year\xe2\x80\x99s nuts are this year\xe2\x80\x99s black earth,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mowgli. \xe2\x80\x9cIt is true\r\nthat I am a Man, but it is in my stomach that this night I have said\r\nthat I am a Wolf. I called the River and the Trees to remember. I am of\r\nthe Free People, Kaa, till the dhole has gone by.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFree People,\xe2\x80\x9d Kaa grunted. \xe2\x80\x9cFree thieves! And thou hast tied thyself\r\ninto the death-knot for the sake of the memory of the dead wolves? This\r\nis no good hunting.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is my Word which I have spoken. The Trees know, the River knows.\r\nTill the dhole have gone by my Word comes not back to me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNgssh! This changes all trails. I had thought to take thee away with me\r\nto the northern marshes, but the Word--even the Word of a little, naked,\r\nhairless Manling--is the Word. Now I, Kaa, say----\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThink well, Flathead, lest thou tie thyself into the death-knot also. I\r\nneed no Word from thee, for well I know----\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBe it so, then,\xe2\x80\x9d said Kaa. \xe2\x80\x9cI will give no Word; but what is in thy\r\nstomach to do when the dhole come?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey must swim the Waingunga. I thought to meet them with my knife in\r\nthe shallows, the Pack behind me; and so stabbing and thrusting, we a\r\nlittle might turn them down-stream, or cool their throats.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe dhole do not turn and their throats are hot,\xe2\x80\x9d said Kaa. \xe2\x80\x9cThere will\r\nbe neither Manling nor Wolf-cub when that hunting is done, but only dry\r\nbones.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAlala! If we die, we die. It will be most good hunting. But my stomach\r\nis young, and I have not seen many Rains. I am not wise nor strong. Hast\r\nthou a better plan, Kaa?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have seen a hundred and a hundred Rains. Ere Hathi cast his\r\nmilk-tushes my trail was big in the dust. By the First Egg, I am older\r\nthan many trees, and I have seen all that the Jungle has done.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut THIS is new hunting,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mowgli. \xe2\x80\x9cNever before have the dhole\r\ncrossed our trail.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat is has been. What will be is no more than a forgotten year\r\nstriking backward. Be still while I count those my years.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nFor a long hour Mowgli lay back among the coils, while Kaa, his head\r\nmotionless on the ground, thought of all that he had seen and known\r\nsince the day he came from the egg. The light seemed to go out of his\r\neyes and leave them like stale opals, and now and again he made little\r\nstiff passes with his head, right and left, as though he were hunting in\r\nhis sleep. Mowgli dozed quietly, for he knew that there is nothing like\r\nsleep before hunting, and he was trained to take it at any hour of the\r\nday or night.\r\n\r\nThen he felt Kaa\xe2\x80\x99s back grow bigger and broader below him as the huge\r\npython puffed himself out, hissing with the noise of a sword drawn from\r\na steel scabbard.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have seen all the dead seasons,\xe2\x80\x9d Kaa said at last, \xe2\x80\x9cand the\r\ngreat trees and the old elephants, and the rocks that were bare and\r\nsharp-pointed ere the moss grew. Art THOU still alive, Manling?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is only a little after moonset,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mowgli. \xe2\x80\x9cI do not\r\nunderstand----\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHssh! I am again Kaa. I knew it was but a little time. Now we will\r\ngo to the river, and I will show thee what is to be done against the\r\ndhole.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe turned, straight as an arrow, for the main stream of the Waingunga,\r\nplunging in a little above the pool that hid the Peace Rock, Mowgli at\r\nhis side.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNay, do not swim. I go swiftly. My back, Little Brother.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMowgli tucked his left arm round Kaa\xe2\x80\x99s neck, dropped his right close to\r\nhis body, and straightened his feet. Then Kaa breasted the current as\r\nhe alone could, and the ripple of the checked water stood up in a frill\r\nround Mowgli\xe2\x80\x99s neck, and his feet were waved to and fro in the eddy\r\nunder the python\xe2\x80\x99s lashing sides. A mile or two above the Peace Rock\r\nthe Waingunga narrows between a gorge of marble rocks from eighty to\r\na hundred feet high, and the current runs like a mill-race between and\r\nover all manner of ugly stones. But Mowgli did not trouble his head\r\nabout the water; little water in the world could have given him a\r\nmoment\xe2\x80\x99s fear. He was looking at the gorge on either side and sniffing\r\nuneasily, for there was a sweetish-sourish smell in the air, very like\r\nthe smell of a big ant-hill on a hot day. Instinctively he lowered\r\nhimself in the water, only raising his head to breathe from time to\r\ntime, and Kaa came to anchor with a double twist of his tail round a\r\nsunken rock, holding Mowgli in the hollow of a coil, while the water\r\nraced on.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThis is the Place of Death,\xe2\x80\x9d said the boy. \xe2\x80\x9cWhy do we come here?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey sleep,\xe2\x80\x9d said Kaa. \xe2\x80\x9cHathi will not turn aside for the Striped One.\r\nYet Hathi and the Striped One together turn aside for the dhole, and the\r\ndhole they say turn aside for nothing. And yet for whom do the Little\r\nPeople of the Rocks turn aside? Tell me, Master of the Jungle, who is\r\nthe Master of the Jungle?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThese,\xe2\x80\x9d Mowgli whispered. \xe2\x80\x9cIt is the Place of Death. Let us go.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNay, look well, for they are asleep. It is as it was when I was not the\r\nlength of thy arm.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe split and weatherworn rocks of the gorge of the Waingunga had been\r\nused since the beginning of the Jungle by the Little People of the\r\nRocks--the busy, furious, black wild bees of India; and, as Mowgli knew\r\nwell, all trails turned off half a mile before they reached the gorge.\r\nFor centuries the Little People had hived and swarmed from cleft to\r\ncleft, and swarmed again, staining the white marble with stale honey,\r\nand made their combs tall and deep in the dark of the inner caves, where\r\nneither man nor beast nor fire nor water had ever touched them. The\r\nlength of the gorge on both siaes was hung as it were with black\r\nshimmery velvet curtains, and Mowgli sank as he looked, for those were\r\nthe clotted millions of the sleeping bees. There were other lumps and\r\nfestoons and things like decayed tree-trunks studded on the face of the\r\nrock, the old combs of past years, or new cities built in the shadow of\r\nthe windless gorge, and huge masses of spongy, rotten trash had rolled\r\ndown and stuck among the trees and creepers that clung to the rock-face.\r\nAs he listened he heard more than once the rustle and slide of a\r\nhoney-loaded comb turning over or failing away somewhere in the dark\r\ngalleries; then a booming of angry wings, and the sullen drip, drip,\r\ndrip, of the wasted honey, guttering along till it lipped over some\r\nledge in the open air and sluggishly trickled down on the twigs. There\r\nwas a tiny little beach, not five feet broad, on one side of the river,\r\nand that was piled high with the rubbish of uncounted years. There were\r\ndead bees, drones, sweepings, and stale combs, and wings of marauding\r\nmoths that had strayed in after honey, all tumbled in smooth piles of\r\nthe finest black dust. The mere sharp smell of it was enough to frighten\r\nanything that had no wings, and knew what the Little People were.\r\n\r\nKaa moved up-stream again till he came to a sandy bar at the head of the\r\ngorge.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHere is this season\xe2\x80\x99s kill,\xe2\x80\x9d said he. \xe2\x80\x9cLook!\xe2\x80\x9d On the bank lay the\r\nskeletons of a couple of young deer and a buffalo. Mowgli could see\r\nthat neither wolf nor jackal had touched the hones, which were laid out\r\nnaturally.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey came beyond the line; they did not know the Law,\xe2\x80\x9d murmured\r\nMowgli, \xe2\x80\x9cand the Little People killed them. Let us go ere they wake.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey do not wake till the dawn,\xe2\x80\x9d said Kaa. \xe2\x80\x9cNow I will tell thee. A\r\nhunted buck from the south, many, many Rains ago, came hither from the\r\nsouth, not knowing the Jungle, a Pack on his trail. Being made blind by\r\nfear, he leaped from above, the Pack running by sight, for they were\r\nhot and blind on the trail. The sun was high, and the Little People were\r\nmany and very angry. Many, too, were those of the Pack who leaped into\r\nthe Waingunga, but they were dead ere they took water. Those who did not\r\nleap died also in the rocks above. But the buck lived.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBecause he came first, running for his life, leaping ere the Little\r\nPeople were aware, and was in the river when they gathered to kill.\r\nThe Pack, following, was altogether lost under the weight of the Little\r\nPeople.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe buck lived?\xe2\x80\x9d Mowgli repeated slowly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAt least he did not die THEN, though none waited his coming down with\r\na strong body to hold him safe against the water, as a certain old fat,\r\ndeaf, yellow Flathead would wait for a Manling--yea, though there were\r\nall the dholes of the Dekkan on his trail. What is in thy stomach?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n Kaa\xe2\x80\x99s head was close to Mowgli\xe2\x80\x99s ear; and it was a little time before\r\nthe boy answered.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is to pull the very whiskers of Death, but--Kaa, thou art, indeed,\r\nthe wisest of all the Jungle.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo many have said. Look now, if the dhole follow thee----\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAs surely they will follow. Ho! ho! I have many little thorns under my\r\ntongue to prick into their hides.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf they follow thee hot and blind, looking only at thy shoulders, those\r\nwho do not die up above will take water either here or lower down, for\r\nthe Little People will rise up and cover them. Now the Waingunga is\r\nhungry water, and they will have no Kaa to hold them, but will go down,\r\nsuch as live, to the shallows by the Seeonee Lairs, and there thy Pack\r\nmay meet them by the throat.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAhai! Eowawa! Better could not be till the Rains fall in the dry\r\nseason. There is now only the little matter of the run and the leap.\r\nI will make me known to the dholes, so that they shall follow me very\r\nclosely.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHast thou seen the rocks above thee? From the landward side?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIndeed, no. That I had forgotten.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGo look. It is all rotten ground, cut and full of holes. One of thy\r\nclumsy feet set down without seeing would end the hunt. See, I leave\r\nthee here, and for thy sake only I will carry word to the Pack that they\r\nmay know where to look for the dhole. For myself, I am not of one skin\r\nwith ANY wolf.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWhen Kaa disliked an acquaintance he could be more unpleasant than any\r\nof the Jungle People, except perhaps Bagheera. He swam down-stream,\r\nand opposite the Rock he came on Phao and Akela listening to the night\r\nnoises.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHssh! Dogs,\xe2\x80\x9d he said cheerfully. \xe2\x80\x9cThe dholes will come down-stream. If\r\nye be not afraid ye can kill them in the shallows.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhen come they?\xe2\x80\x9d said Phao. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd where is my Man-cub?\xe2\x80\x9d said Akela.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey come when they come,\xe2\x80\x9d said Kaa. \xe2\x80\x9cWait and see. As for THY Man-cub,\r\nfrom whom thou hast taken a Word and so laid him open to Death, THY\r\nMan-cub is with ME, and if he be not already dead the fault is none\r\nof thine, bleached dog! Wait here for the dhole, and be glad that the\r\nMan-cub and I strike on thy side.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nKaa flashed up-stream again, and moored himself in the middle of\r\nthe gorge, looking upward at the line of the cliff. Presently he saw\r\nMowgli\xe2\x80\x99s head move against the stars, and then there was a whizz in\r\nthe air, the keen, clean schloop of a body falling feet first, and next\r\nminute the boy was at rest again in the loop of Kaa\xe2\x80\x99s body.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is no leap by night,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mowgli quietly. \xe2\x80\x9cI have jumped twice as\r\nfar for sport; but that is an evil place above--low bushes and gullies\r\nthat go down very deep, all full of the Little People. I have put big\r\nstones one above the other by the side of three gullies. These I shall\r\nthrow down with my feet in running, and the Little People will rise up\r\nbehind me, very angry.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is Man\xe2\x80\x99s talk and Man\xe2\x80\x99s cunning,\xe2\x80\x9d said Kaa. \xe2\x80\x9cThou art wise, but\r\nthe Little People are always angry.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNay, at twilight all wings near and far rest for a while. I will play\r\nwith the dhole at twilight, for the dhole hunts best by day.
