He follows\r\nnow Won-tolla\xe2\x80\x99s blood-trail.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cChil does not leave a dead ox, nor the dhole the blood-trail,\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nKaa.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen I will make him a new blood-trail, of his own blood, if I can, and\r\ngive him dirt to eat. Thou wilt stay here, Kaa, till I come again with\r\nmy dholes?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAy, but what if they kill thee in the Jungle, or the Little People kill\r\nthee before thou canst leap down to the river?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhen to-morrow comes we will kill for to-morrow,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mowgli, quoting\r\na Jungle saying; and again, \xe2\x80\x9cWhen I am dead it is time to sing the Death\r\nSong. Good hunting, Kaa!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe loosed his arm from the python\xe2\x80\x99s neck and went down the gorge like\r\na log in a freshet, paddling toward the far bank, where he found\r\nslack-water, and laughing aloud from sheer happiness. There was nothing\r\nMowgli liked better than, as he himself said, \xe2\x80\x9cto pull the whiskers\r\nof Death,\xe2\x80\x9d and make the Jungle know that he was their overlord. He had\r\noften, with Baloo\xe2\x80\x99s help, robbed bees\xe2\x80\x99 nests in single trees, and\r\nhe knew that the Little People hated the smell of wild garlic. So he\r\ngathered a small bundle of it, tied it up with a bark string, and then\r\nfollowed Won-tolla\xe2\x80\x99s blood-trail, as it ran southerly from the Lairs,\r\nfor some five miles, looking at the trees with his head on one side, and\r\nchuckling as he looked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMowgli the Frog have I been,\xe2\x80\x9d said he to himself; \xe2\x80\x9cMowgli the Wolf have\r\nI said that I am. Now Mowgli the Ape must I be before I am Mowgli the\r\nBuck. At the end I shall be Mowgli the Man. Ho!\xe2\x80\x9d and he slid his thumb\r\nalong the eighteen-inch blade of his knife.\r\n\r\nWon-tolla\xe2\x80\x99s trail, all rank with dark blood-spots, ran under a forest of\r\nthick trees that grew close together and stretched away north-eastward,\r\ngradually growing thinner and thinner to within two miles of the Bee\r\nRocks. From the last tree to the low scrub of the Bee Rocks was open\r\ncountry, where there was hardly cover enough to hide a wolf. Mowgli\r\ntrotted along under the trees, judging distances between branch and\r\nbranch, occasionally climbing up a trunk and taking a trial leap from\r\none tree to another till he came to the open ground, which he studied\r\nvery carefully for an hour. Then he turned, picked up Won-tolla\xe2\x80\x99s trail\r\nwhere he had left it, settled himself in a tree with an outrunning\r\nbranch some eight feet from the ground, and sat still, sharpening his\r\nknife on the sole of his foot and singing to himself.\r\n\r\nA little before mid-day, when the sun was very warm, he heard the patter\r\nof feet and smelt the abominable smell of the dhole-pack as they trotted\r\npitilessly along Won-tolla\xe2\x80\x99s trail. Seen from above, the red dhole does\r\nnot look half the size of a wolf, but Mowgli knew how strong his feet\r\nand jaws were. He watched the sharp bay head of the leader snuffing\r\nalong the trail, and gave him \xe2\x80\x9cGood hunting!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe brute looked up, and his companions halted behind him, scores and\r\nscores of red dogs with low-hung tails, heavy shoulders, weak quarters,\r\nand bloody mouths. The dholes are a very silent people as a rule, and\r\nthey have no manners even in their own Jungle. Fully two hundred must\r\nhave gathered below him, but he could see that the leaders sniffed\r\nhungrily on Won-tolla\xe2\x80\x99s trail, and tried to drag the Pack forward. That\r\nwould never do, or they would be at the Lairs in broad daylight, and\r\nMowgli meant to hold them under his tree till dusk.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBy whose leave do ye come here?\xe2\x80\x9d said Mowgli.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAll Jungles are our Jungle,\xe2\x80\x9d was the reply, and the dhole that gave\r\nit bared his white teeth. Mowgli looked down with a smile, and imitated\r\nperfectly the sharp chitter-chatter of Chikai, the leaping rat of the\r\nDekkan, meaning the dholes to understand that he considered them no\r\nbetter than Chikai. The Pack closed up round the tree-trunk and the\r\nleader bayed savagely, calling Mowgli a tree-ape. For an answer Mowgli\r\nstretched down one naked leg and wriggled his bare toes just above the\r\nleader\xe2\x80\x99s head. That was enough, and more than enough, to wake the Pack\r\nto stupid rage. Those who have hair between their toes do not care to be\r\nreminded of it. Mowgli caught his foot away as the leader leaped up, and\r\nsaid sweetly: \xe2\x80\x9cDog, red dog! Go back to the Dekkan and eat lizards. Go to\r\nChikai thy brother--dog, dog--red, red dog! There is hair between every\r\ntoe!\xe2\x80\x9d He twiddled his toes a second time.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCome down ere we starve thee out, hairless ape!\xe2\x80\x9d yelled the Pack, and\r\nthis was exactly what Mowgli wanted. He laid himself down along the\r\nbranch, his cheek to the bark, his right arm free, and there he told the\r\nPack what he thought and knew about them, their manners, their customs,\r\ntheir mates, and their puppies. There is no speech in the world so\r\nrancorous and so stinging as the language the Jungle People use to show\r\nscorn and contempt. When you come to think of it you will see how this\r\nmust be so. As Mowgli told Kaa, he had many little thorns under his\r\ntongue, and slowly and deliberately he drove the dholes from silence to\r\ngrowls, from growls to yells, and from yells to hoarse slavery ravings.\r\nThey tried to answer his taunts, but a cub might as well have tried to\r\nanswer Kaa in a rage; and all the while Mowgli\xe2\x80\x99s right hand lay crooked\r\nat his side, ready for action, his feet locked round the branch. The big\r\nbay leader had leaped many times in the air, but Mowgli dared not risk\r\na false blow. At last, made furious beyond his natural strength, he\r\nbounded up seven or eight feet clear of the ground. Then Mowgli\xe2\x80\x99s hand\r\nshot out like the head of a tree-snake, and gripped him by the scruff\r\nof his neck, and the branch shook with the jar as his weight fell back,\r\nalmost wrenching Mowgli to the ground. But he never loosed his grip, and\r\ninch by inch he hauled the beast, hanging like a drowned jackal, up on\r\nthe branch. With his left hand he reached for his knife and cut off the\r\nred, bushy tail, flinging the dhole back to earth again. That was all he\r\nneeded. The Pack would not go forward on Won-tolla\xe2\x80\x99s trail now till they\r\nhad killed Mowgli or Mowgli had killed them. He saw them settle down\r\nin circles with a quiver of the haunches that meant they were going\r\nto stay, and so he climbed to a higher crotch, settled his back\r\ncomfortably, and went to sleep.\r\n\r\nAfter three or four hours he waked and counted the Pack. They were all\r\nthere, silent, husky, and dry, with eyes of steel. The sun was beginning\r\nto sink. In half an hour the Little People of the Rocks would be ending\r\ntheir labours, and, as you know, the dhole does not fight best in the\r\ntwilight.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI did not need such faithful watchers,\xe2\x80\x9d he said politely, standing up\r\non a branch, \xe2\x80\x9cbut I will remember this. Ye be true dholes, but to my\r\nthinking over much of one kind. For that reason I do not give the big\r\nlizard-eater his tail again. Art thou not pleased, Red Dog?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI myself will tear out thy stomach!\xe2\x80\x9d yelled the leader, scratching at\r\nthe foot of the tree.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNay, but consider, wise rat of the Dekkan. There will now be many\r\nlitters of little tailless red dogs, yea, with raw red stumps that sting\r\nwhen the sand is hot. Go home, Red Dog, and cry that an ape has done\r\nthis. Ye will not go? Come, then, with me, and I will make you very\r\nwise!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe moved, Bandar-log fashion, into the next tree, and so on into the\r\nnext and the next, the Pack following with lifted hungry heads. Now and\r\nthen he would pretend to fall, and the Pack would tumble one over the\r\nother in their haste to be at the death. It was a curious sight--the boy\r\nwith the knife that shone in the low sunlight as it sifted through the\r\nupper branches, and the silent Pack with their red coats all aflame,\r\nhuddling and following below. When he came to the last tree he took the\r\ngarlic and rubbed himself all over carefully, and the dholes yelled with\r\nscorn. \xe2\x80\x9cApe with a wolf\xe2\x80\x99s tongue, dost thou think to cover thy scent?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n they said. \xe2\x80\x9cWe follow to the death.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTake thy tail,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mowgli, flinging it back along the course he had\r\ntaken. The Pack instinctively rushed after it. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd follow now--to the\r\ndeath.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe had slipped down the tree-trunk, and headed like the wind in bare\r\nfeet for the Bee Rocks, before the dholes saw what he would do.\r\n\r\nThey gave one deep howl, and settled down to the long, lobbing canter\r\nthat can at the last run down anything that runs. Mowgli knew their\r\npack-pace to be much slower than that of the wolves, or he would never\r\nhave risked a two-mile run in full sight. They were sure that the boy\r\nwas theirs at last, and he was sure that he held them to play with as he\r\npleased. All his trouble was to keep them sufficiently hot behind him\r\nto prevent their turning off too soon. He ran cleanly, evenly, and\r\nspringily; the tailless leader not five yards behind him; and the Pack\r\ntailing out over perhaps a quarter of a mile of ground, crazy and blind\r\nwith the rage of slaughter. So he kept his distance by ear, reserving\r\nhis last effort for the rush across the Bee Rocks.\r\n\r\nThe Little People had gone to sleep in the early twilight, for it\r\nwas not the season of late blossoming flowers; but as Mowgli\xe2\x80\x99s first\r\nfoot-falls rang hollow on the hollow ground he heard a sound as though\r\nall the earth were humming. Then he ran as he had never run in his life\r\nbefore, spurned aside one--two--three of the piles of stones into the\r\ndark, sweet-smelling gullies; heard a roar like the roar of the sea in a\r\ncave; saw with the tail of his eye the air grow dark behind him; saw the\r\ncurrent of the Waingunga far below, and a flat, diamond-shaped head\r\nin the water; leaped outward with all his strength, the tailless dhole\r\nsnapping at his shoulder in mid-air, and dropped feet first to the\r\nsafety of the river, breathless and triumphant. There was not a sting\r\nupon him, for the smell of the garlic had checked the Little People for\r\njust the few seconds that he was among them. When he rose Kaa\xe2\x80\x99s coils\r\nwere steadying him and things were bounding over the edge of the\r\ncliff--great lumps, it seemed, of clustered bees falling like plummets;\r\nbut before any lump touched water the bees flew upward and the body of a\r\ndhole whirled down-stream. Overhead they could hear furious short yells\r\nthat were drowned in a roar like breakers--the roar of the wings of the\r\nLittle People of the Rocks. Some of the dholes, too, had fallen into the\r\ngullies that communicated with the underground caves, and there choked\r\nand fought and snapped among the tumbled honeycombs, and at last, borne\r\nup, even when they were dead, on the heaving waves of bees beneath\r\nthem, shot out of some hole in the river-face, to roll over on the black\r\nrubbish-heaps. There were dholes who had leaped short into the trees\r\non the cliffs, and the bees blotted out their shapes; but the greater\r\nnumber of them, maddened by the stings, had flung themselves into the\r\nriver; and, as Kaa said, the Waingunga was hungry water.\r\n\r\nKaa held Mowgli fast till the boy had recovered his breath.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe may not stay here,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cThe Little People are roused indeed.\r\nCome!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSwimming low and diving as often as he could, Mowgli went down the\r\nriver, knife in hand.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSlowly, slowly,\xe2\x80\x9d said Kaa. \xe2\x80\x9cOne tooth does not kill a hundred unless\r\nit be a cobra\xe2\x80\x99s, and many of the dholes took water swiftly when they saw\r\nthe Little People rise.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe more work for my knife, then. Phai! How the Little People follow!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n Mowgli sank again. The face of the water was blanketed with wild bees,\r\nbuzzing sullenly and stinging all they found.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNothing was ever yet lost by silence,\xe2\x80\x9d said Kaa--no sting could\r\npenetrate his scales--\xe2\x80\x9cand thou hast all the long night for the hunting.\r\nHear them howl!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nNearly half the pack had seen the trap their fellows rushed into, and\r\nturning sharp aside had flung themselves into the water where the gorge\r\nbroke down in steep banks. Their cries of rage and their threats against\r\nthe \xe2\x80\x9ctree-ape\xe2\x80\x9d who had brought them to their shame mixed with the yells\r\nand growls of those who had been punished by the Little People. To\r\nremain ashore was death, and every dhole knew it. Their pack was swept\r\nalong the current, down to the deep eddies of the Peace Pool, but even\r\nthere the angry Little People followed and forced them to the water\r\nagain. Mowgli could hear the voice of the tailless leader bidding his\r\npeople hold on and kill out every wolf in Seeonee. But he did not waste\r\nhis time in listening.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOne kills in the dark behind us!\xe2\x80\x9d snapped a dhole. \xe2\x80\x9cHere is tainted\r\nwater!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMowgli had dived forward like an otter, twitched a struggling dhole\r\nunder water before he could open his mouth, and dark rings rose as the\r\nbody plopped up, turning on its side. The dholes tried to turn, but the\r\ncurrent prevented them, and the Little People darted at the heads and\r\nears, and they could hear the challenge of the Seeonee Pack growing\r\nlouder and deeper in the gathering darkness. Again Mowgli dived, and\r\nagain a dhole went under, and rose dead, and again the clamour broke\r\nout at the rear of the pack; some howling that it was best to go ashore,\r\nothers calling on their leader to lead them back to the Dekkan, and\r\nothers bidding Mowgli show himself and be killed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey come to the fight with two stomachs and several voices,\xe2\x80\x9d said Kaa.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe rest is with thy brethren below yonder, The Little People go back\r\nto sleep. They have chased us far. Now I, too, turn back, for I am not\r\nof one skin with any wolf. Good hunting, Little Brother, and remember\r\nthe dhole bites low.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nA wolf came running along the bank on three legs, leaping up and down,\r\nlaying his head sideways close to the ground, hunching his back, and\r\nbreaking high into the air, as though he were playing with his cubs. It\r\nwas Won-tolla, the Outlier, and he said never a word, but continued his\r\nhorrible sport beside the dholes. They had been long in the water now,\r\nand were swimming wearily, their coats drenched and heavy, their bushy\r\ntails dragging like sponges, so tired and shaken that they, too, were\r\nsilent, watching the pair of blazing eyes that moved abreast.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThis is no good hunting,\xe2\x80\x9d said one, panting.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGood hunting!\xe2\x80\x9d said Mowgli, as he rose boldly at the brute\xe2\x80\x99s side, and\r\nsent the long knife home behind the shoulder, pushing hard to avoid his\r\ndying snap.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cArt thou there, Man-cub?\xe2\x80\x9d said Won-tolla across the water.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAsk of the dead, Outlier,\xe2\x80\x9d Mowgli replied. \xe2\x80\x9cHave none come down-stream?\r\nI have filled these dogs\xe2\x80\x99 mouths with dirt; I have tricked them in the\r\nbroad daylight, and their leader lacks his tail, but here be some few\r\nfor thee still. Whither shall I drive them?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI will wait,\xe2\x80\x9d said Won-tolla. \xe2\x80\x9cThe night is before me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nNearer and nearer came the bay of the Seeonee wolves. \xe2\x80\x9cFor the Pack,\r\nfor the Full Pack it is met!\xe2\x80\x9d and a bend in the river drove the dholes\r\nforward among the sands and shoals opposite the Lairs.\r\n\r\nThen they saw their mistake. They should have landed half a mile higher\r\nup, and rushed the wolves on dry ground. Now it was too late. The bank\r\nwas lined with burning eyes, and except for the horrible pheeal that had\r\nnever stopped since sundown, there was no sound in the Jungle. It seemed\r\nas though Won-tolla were fawning on them to come ashore; and \xe2\x80\x9cTurn\r\nand take hold!\xe2\x80\x9d said the leader of the dholes. The entire Pack flung\r\nthemselves at the shore, threshing and squattering through the shoal\r\nwater, till the face of the Waingunga was all white and torn, and the\r\ngreat ripples went from side to side, like bow-waves from a boat. Mowgli\r\nfollowed the rush, stabbing and slicing as the dholes, huddled together,\r\nrushed up the river-beach in one wave.\r\n\r\nThen the long fight began, heaving and straining and splitting and\r\nscattering and narrowing and broadening along the red, wet sands, and\r\nover and between the tangled tree-roots, and through and among the\r\nbushes, and in and out of the grass clumps; for even now the dholes were\r\ntwo to one. But they met wolves fighting for all that made the Pack,\r\nand not only the short, high, deep-chested, white-tusked hunters of the\r\nPack, but the anxious-eyed lahinis--the she-wolves of the lair, as the\r\nsaying is--fighting for their litters, with here and there a yearling\r\nwolf, his first coat still half woolly, tugging and grappling by their\r\nsides. A wolf, you must know, flies at the throat or snaps at the flank,\r\nwhile a dhole, by preference, bites at the belly; so when the dholes\r\nwere struggling out of the water and had to raise their heads, the odds\r\nwere with the wolves. On dry land the wolves suffered; but in the water\r\nor ashore, Mowgli\xe2\x80\x99s knife came and went without ceasing. The Four had\r\nworried their way to his side. Gray Brother, crouched between the boy\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nknees, was protecting his stomach, while the others guarded his back\r\nand either side, or stood over him when the shock of a leaping, yelling\r\ndhole who had thrown himself full on the steady blade bore him down. For\r\nthe rest, it was one tangled confusion--a locked and swaying mob that\r\nmoved from right to left and from left to right along the bank; and also\r\nground round and round slowly on its own centre. Here would be a heaving\r\nmound, like a water-blister in a whirlpool, which would break like a\r\nwater-blister, and throw up four or five mangled dogs, each striving to\r\nget back to the centre; here would be a single wolf borne down by two or\r\nthree dholes, laboriously dragging them forward, and sinking the while;\r\nhere a yearling cub would be held up by the pressure round him, though\r\nhe had been killed early, while his mother, crazed with dumb rage,\r\nrolled over and over, snapping, and passing on; and in the middle of the\r\nthickest press, perhaps, one wolf and one dhole, forgetting everything\r\nelse, would be manoeuvring for first hold till they were whirled away by\r\na rush of furious fighters. Once Mowgli passed Akela, a dhole on either\r\nflank, and his all but toothless jaws closed over the loins of a third;\r\nand once he saw Phao, his teeth set in the throat of a dhole, tugging\r\nthe unwilling beast forward till the yearlings could finish him. But the\r\nbulk of the fight was blind flurry and smother in the dark; hit, trip,\r\nand tumble, yelp, groan, and worry-worry-worry, round him and behind him\r\nand above him. As the night wore on, the quick, giddy-go-round motion\r\nincreased. The dholes were cowed and afraid to attack the stronger\r\nwolves, but did not yet dare to run away. Mowgli felt that the end was\r\ncoming soon, and contented himself with striking merely to cripple. The\r\nyearlings were growing bolder; there was time now and again to breathe,\r\nand pass a word to a friend, and the mere flicker of the knife would\r\nsometimes turn a dog aside.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe meat is very near the bone,\xe2\x80\x9d Gray Brother yelled. He was bleeding\r\nfrom a score of flesh-wounds.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut the bone is yet to be cracked,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mowgli. \xe2\x80\x9cEowawa! THUS do we do\r\nin the Jungle!\xe2\x80\x9d The red blade ran like a flame along the side of a dhole\r\nwhose hind-quarters were hidden by the weight of a clinging wolf.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy kill!\xe2\x80\x9d snorted the wolf through his wrinkled nostrils. \xe2\x80\x9cLeave him to\r\nme.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs thy stomach still empty, Outlier?\xe2\x80\x9d said Mowgli. Won-tolla was\r\nfearfully punished, but his grip had paralysed the dhole, who could not\r\nturn round and reach him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBy the Bull that bought me,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mowgli, with a bitter laugh, \xe2\x80\x9cit is\r\nthe tailless one!\xe2\x80\x9d And indeed it was the big bay-coloured leader.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is not wise to kill cubs and lahinis,\xe2\x80\x9d Mowgli went on\r\nphilosophically, wiping the blood out of his eyes, \xe2\x80\x9cunless one has also\r\nkilled the Outlier; and it is in my stomach that this Won-tolla kills\r\nthee.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nA dhole leaped to his leader\xe2\x80\x99s aid; but before his teeth had found\r\nWon-tolla\xe2\x80\x99s flank, Mowgli\xe2\x80\x99s knife was in his throat, and Gray Brother\r\ntook what was left.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd thus do we do in the Jungle,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mowgli.\r\n\r\nWon-tolla said not a word, only his jaws were closing and closing on the\r\nbackbone as his life ebbed. The dhole shuddered, his head dropped, and\r\nhe lay still, and Won-tolla dropped above him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHuh! The Blood Debt is paid,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mowgli. \xe2\x80\x9cSing the song, Won-tolla.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe hunts no more,\xe2\x80\x9d said Gray Brother; \xe2\x80\x9cand Akela, too, is silent this\r\nlong time.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe bone is cracked!\xe2\x80\x9d thundered Phao, son of Phaona. \xe2\x80\x9cThey go! Kill,\r\nkill out, O hunters of the Free People!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nDhole after dhole was slinking away from those dark and bloody sands to\r\nthe river, to the thick Jungle, up-stream or down-stream as he saw the\r\nroad clear.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe debt! The debt!\xe2\x80\x9d shouted Mowgli. \xe2\x80\x9cPay the debt! They have slain the\r\nLone Wolf! Let not a dog go!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe was flying to the river, knife in hand, to check any dhole who dared\r\nto take water, when, from under a mound of nine dead, rose Akela\xe2\x80\x99s head\r\nand fore-quarters, and Mowgli dropped on his knees beside the Lone Wolf.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSaid I not it would be my last fight?\xe2\x80\x9d Akela gasped. \xe2\x80\x9cIt is good\r\nhunting. And thou, Little Brother?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI live, having killed many.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cEven so. I die, and I would--I would die by thee, Little Brother.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMowgli took the terrible scarred head on his knees, and put his arms\r\nround the torn neck.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is long since the old days of Shere Khan, and a Man-cub that rolled\r\nnaked in the dust.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNay, nay, I am a wolf. I am of one skin with the Free People,\xe2\x80\x9d Mowgli\r\ncried. \xe2\x80\x9cIt is no will of mine that I am a man.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThou art a man, Little Brother, wolfling of my watching. Thou art a\r\nman, or else the Pack had fled before the dhole. My life I owe to thee,\r\nand to-day thou hast saved the Pack even as once I saved thee. Hast thou\r\nforgotten? All debts are paid now. Go to thine own people. I tell thee\r\nagain, eye of my eye, this hunting is ended. Go to thine own people.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI will never go. I will hunt alone in the Jungle. I have said it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAfter the summer come the Rains, and after the Rains comes the spring.\r\nGo back before thou art driven.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWho will drive me?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMowgli will drive Mowgli. Go back to thy people. Go to Man.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhen Mowgli drives Mowgli I will go,\xe2\x80\x9d Mowgli answered.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere is no more to say,\xe2\x80\x9d said Akela. \xe2\x80\x9cLittle Brother, canst thou raise\r\nme to my feet? I also was a leader of the Free People.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nVery carefully and gently Mowgli lifted the bodies aside, and raised\r\nAkela to his feet, both arms round him, and the Lone Wolf drew a long\r\nbreath, and began the Death Song that a leader of the Pack should sing\r\nwhen he dies. It gathered strength as he went on, lifting and lifting,\r\nand ringing far across the river, till it came to the last \xe2\x80\x9cGood\r\nhunting!\xe2\x80\x9d and Akela shook himself clear of Mowgli for an instant, and,\r\nleaping into the air, fell backward dead upon his last and most terrible\r\nkill.\r\n\r\nMowgli sat with his head on his knees, careless of anything else, while\r\nthe remnant of the flying dholes were being overtaken and run down by\r\nthe merciless lahinis. Little by little the cries died away, and the\r\nwolves returned limping, as their wounds stiffened, to take stock of the\r\nlosses. Fifteen of the Pack, as well as half a dozen lahinis, lay dead\r\nby the river, and of the others not one was unmarked. And Mowgli sat\r\nthrough it all till the cold daybreak, when Phao\xe2\x80\x99s wet, red muzzle was\r\ndropped in his hand, and Mowgli drew back to show the gaunt body of\r\nAkela.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGood hunting!\xe2\x80\x9d said Phao, as though Akela were still alive, and then\r\nover his bitten shoulder to the others: \xe2\x80\x9cHowl, dogs! A Wolf has died\r\nto-night!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBut of all the Pack of two hundred fighting dholes, whose boast was\r\nthat all jungles were their Jungle, and that no living thing could stand\r\nbefore them, not one returned to the Dekkan to carry that word.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHIL\xe2\x80\x99S SONG\r\n\r\n[This is the song that Chil sang as the kites dropped down one after\r\nanother to the river-bed, when the great fight was finished. Chil is\r\ngood friends with everybody, but he is a cold-blooded kind of creature\r\nat heart, because he knows that almost everybody in the Jungle comes to\r\nhim in the long-run.]\r\n\r\n     These were my companions going forth by night--\r\n           (For Chil! Look you, for Chil!)\r\n     Now come I to whistle them the ending of the fight.\r\n           (Chil! Vanguards of Chil!)\r\n     Word they gave me overhead of quarry newly slain,\r\n     Word I gave them underfoot of buck upon the plain.\r\n     Here\xe2\x80\x99s an end of every trail--they shall not speak again!\r\n\r\n     They that called the hunting-cry--they that followed fast--\r\n           (For Chil! Look you, for Chil!)\r\n     They that bade the sambhur wheel, or pinned him as he passed--\r\n           (Chil! Vanguards of Chil!)\r\n     They that lagged behind the scent--they that ran before,\r\n     They that shunned the level horn--they that overbore.\r\n     Here\xe2\x80\x99s an end of every trail--they shall not follow more.\r\n     These were my companions. Pity \xe2\x80\x98twas they died!\r\n           (For Chil! Look you, for Chil!)\r\n     Now come I to comfort them that knew them in their pride.\r\n           (Chil! Vanguards of Chil!)\r\n     Tattered flank and sunken eye, open mouth and red,\r\n     Locked and lank and lone they lie, the dead upon their dead.\r\n     Here\xe2\x80\x99s an end of every trail--and here my hosts are fed.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nTHE SPRING RUNNING\r\n\r\n     Man goes to Man! Cry the challenge through the Jungle!\r\n       He that was our Brother goes away.\r\n     Hear, now, and judge, O ye People of the Jungle,--\r\n       Answer, who shall turn him--who shall stay?\r\n\r\n     Man goes to Man! He is weeping in the Jungle:\r\n       He that was our Brother sorrows sore!\r\n     Man goes to Man! (Oh, we loved him in the Jungle!)\r\n       To the Man-Trail where we may not follow more.\r\n\r\nThe second year after the great fight with Red Dog and the death of\r\nAkela, Mowgli must have been nearly seventeen years old. He looked\r\nolder, for hard exercise, the best of good eating, and baths whenever\r\nhe felt in the least hot or dusty, had given him strength and growth far\r\nbeyond his age. He could swing by one hand from a top branch for half\r\nan hour at a time, when he had occasion to look along the tree-roads.\r\nHe could stop a young buck in mid-gallop and throw him sideways by the\r\nhead. He could even jerk over the big, blue wild boars that lived in\r\nthe Marshes of the North. The Jungle People who used to fear him for his\r\nwits feared him now for his strength, and when he moved quietly on his\r\nown affairs the mere whisper of his coming cleared the wood-paths. And\r\nyet the look in his eyes was always gentle. Even when he fought, his\r\neyes never blazed as Bagheera\xe2\x80\x99s did. They only grew more and more\r\ninterested and excited; and that was one of the things that Bagheera\r\nhimself did not understand.\r\n\r\nHe asked Mowgli about it, and the boy laughed and said. \xe2\x80\x9cWhen I miss the\r\nkill I am angry. When I must go empty for two days I am very angry. Do\r\nnot my eyes talk then?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe mouth is hungry,\xe2\x80\x9d said Bagheera, \xe2\x80\x9cbut the eyes say nothing.\r\nHunting, eating, or swimming, it is all one--like a stone in wet or dry\r\nweather.\xe2\x80\x9d Mowgli looked at him lazily from under his long eyelashes,\r\nand, as usual, the panther\xe2\x80\x99s head dropped. Bagheera knew his master.\r\n\r\nThey were lying out far up the side of a hill overlooking the Waingunga,\r\nand the morning mists hung below them in bands of white and green. As\r\nthe sun rose it changed into bubbling seas of red gold, churned off,\r\nand let the low rays stripe the dried grass on which Mowgli and Bagheera\r\nwere resting. It was the end of the cold weather, the leaves and\r\nthe trees looked worn and faded, and there was a dry, ticking rustle\r\neverywhere when the wind blew. A little leaf tap-tap-tapped furiously\r\nagainst a twig, as a single leaf caught in a current will. It roused\r\nBagheera, for he snuffed the morning air with a deep, hollow cough,\r\nthrew himself on his back, and struck with his fore-paws at the nodding\r\nleaf above.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe year turns,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cThe Jungle goes forward. The Time of New\r\nTalk is near. That leaf knows. It is very good.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe grass is dry,\xe2\x80\x9d Mowgli answered, pulling up a tuft. \xe2\x80\x9cEven\r\nEye-of-the-Spring [that is a little trumpet-shaped, waxy red flower\r\nthat runs in and out among the grasses]--even Eye-of-the Spring is shut,\r\nand... Bagheera, IS it well for the Black Panther so to lie on his back\r\nand beat with his paws in the air, as though he were the tree-cat?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAowh?\xe2\x80\x9d said Bagheera. He seemed to be thinking of other things.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI say, IS it well for the Black Panther so to mouth and cough, and howl\r\nand roll? Remember, we be the Masters of the Jungle, thou and I.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIndeed, yes; I hear, Man-cub.\xe2\x80\x9d Bagheera rolled over hurriedly and sat\r\nup, the dust on his ragged black flanks. (He was just casting his winter\r\ncoat.) \xe2\x80\x9cWe be surely the Masters of the Jungle! Who is so strong as\r\nMowgli? Who so wise?\xe2\x80\x9d There was a curious drawl in the voice that made\r\nMowgli turn to see whether by any chance the Black Panther were making\r\nfun of him, for the Jungle is full of words that sound like one thing,\r\nbut mean another. \xe2\x80\x9cI said we be beyond question the Masters of the\r\nJungle,\xe2\x80\x9d Bagheera repeated. \xe2\x80\x9cHave I done wrong? I did not know that the\r\nMan-cub no longer lay upon the ground. Does he fly, then?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMowgli sat with his elbows on his knees, looking out across the valley\r\nat the daylight. Somewhere down in the woods below a bird was trying\r\nover in a husky, reedy voice the first few notes of his spring song.\r\nIt was no more than a shadow of the liquid, tumbling call he would be\r\npouring later, but Bagheera heard it.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI said the Time of New Talk is near,\xe2\x80\x9d growled the panther, switching\r\nhis tail.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI hear,\xe2\x80\x9d Mowgli answered. \xe2\x80\x9cBagheera, why dost thou shake all over? The\r\nsun is warm.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is Ferao, the scarlet woodpecker,\xe2\x80\x9d said Bagheera. \xe2\x80\x9cHE has not\r\nforgotten. Now I, too, must remember my song,\xe2\x80\x9d and he began purring and\r\ncrooning to himself, harking back dissatisfied again and again.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere is no game afoot,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mowgli.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLittle Brother, are BOTH thine ears stopped? That is no killing-word,\r\nbut my song that I make ready against the need.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI had forgotten. I shall know when the Time of New Talk is here,\r\nbecause then thou and the others all run away and leave me alone.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n Mowgli spoke rather savagely.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut, indeed, Little Brother,\xe2\x80\x9d Bagheera began, \xe2\x80\x9cwe do not always----\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI say ye do,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mowgli, shooting out his forefinger angrily. \xe2\x80\x9cYe DO\r\nrun away, and I, who am the Master of the Jungle, must needs walk alone.\r\nHow was it last season, when I would gather sugar-cane from the fields\r\nof a Man-Pack? I sent a runner--I sent thee!--to Hathi, bidding him to\r\ncome upon such a night and pluck the sweet grass for me with his trunk.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe came only two nights later,\xe2\x80\x9d said Bagheera, cowering a little; \xe2\x80\x9cand\r\nof that long, sweet grass that pleased thee so he gathered more than any\r\nMan-cub could eat in all the nights of the Rains. That was no fault of\r\nmine.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe did not come upon the night when I sent him the word. No, he was\r\ntrumpeting and running and roaring through the valleys in the moonlight.\r\nHis trail was like the trail of three elephants, for he would not hide\r\namong the trees. He danced in the moonlight before the houses of the\r\nMan-Pack. I saw him, and yet he would not come to me; and _I_ am the\r\nMaster of the Jungle!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt was the Time of New Talk,\xe2\x80\x9d said the panther, always very humble.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPerhaps, Little Brother, thou didst not that time call him by a\r\nMaster-word? Listen to Ferao, and be glad!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMowgli\xe2\x80\x99s bad temper seemed to have boiled itself away. He lay back with\r\nhis head on his arms, his eyes shut. \xe2\x80\x9cI do not know--nor do I care,\xe2\x80\x9d he\r\nsaid sleepily. \xe2\x80\x9cLet us sleep, Bagheera. My stomach is heavy in me. Make\r\nme a rest for my head.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe panther lay down again with a sigh, because he could hear Ferao\r\npractising and repractising his song against the Springtime of New Talk,\r\nas they say.\r\n\r\nIn an Indian Jungle the seasons slide one into the other almost without\r\ndivision. There seem to be only two--the wet and the dry; but if you\r\nlook closely below the torrents of rain and the clouds of char and dust\r\nyou will find all four going round in their regular ring. Spring is the\r\nmost wonderful, because she has not to cover a clean, bare field with\r\nnew leaves and flowers, but to drive before her and to put away the\r\nhanging-on, over-surviving raffle of half-green things which the gentle\r\nwinter has suffered to live, and to make the partly-dressed stale earth\r\nfeel new and young once more. And this she does so well that there is no\r\nspring in the world like the Jungle spring.\r\n\r\nThere is one day when all things are tired, and the very smells, as they\r\ndrift on the heavy air, are old and used. One cannot explain this, but\r\nit feels so. Then there is another day--to the eye nothing whatever has\r\nchanged--when all the smells are new and delightful, and the whiskers of\r\nthe Jungle People quiver to their roots, and the winter hair comes away\r\nfrom their sides in long, draggled locks. Then, perhaps, a little rain\r\nfalls, and all the trees and the bushes and the bamboos and the mosses\r\nand the juicy-leaved plants wake with a noise of growing that you can\r\nalmost hear, and under this noise runs, day and night, a deep hum. THAT\r\nis the noise of the spring--a vibrating boom which is neither bees, nor\r\nfalling water, nor the wind in tree-tops, but the purring of the warm,\r\nhappy world.\r\n\r\nUp to this year Mowgli had always delighted in the turn of the seasons.\r\nIt was he who generally saw the first Eye-of-the-Spring deep down among\r\nthe grasses, and the first bank of spring clouds, which are like nothing\r\nelse in the Jungle. His voice could be heard in all sorts of wet,\r\nstar-lighted, blossoming places, helping the big frogs through their\r\nchoruses, or mocking the little upside-down owls that hoot through the\r\nwhite nights. Like all his people, spring was the season he chose for\r\nhis flittings--moving, for the mere joy of rushing through the warm air,\r\nthirty, forty, or fifty miles between twilight and the morning star, and\r\ncoming back panting and laughing and wreathed with strange flowers. The\r\nFour did not follow him on these wild ringings of the Jungle, but went\r\noff to sing songs with other wolves. The Jungle People are very busy\r\nin the spring, and Mowgli could hear them grunting and screaming and\r\nwhistling according to their kind. Their voices then are different from\r\ntheir voices at other times of the year, and that is one of the reasons\r\nwhy spring in the Jungle is called the Time of New Talk.\r\n\r\nBut that spring, as he told Bagheera, his stomach was changed in him.\r\nEver since the bamboo shoots turned spotty-brown he had been looking\r\nforward to the morning when the smells should change. But when the\r\nmorning came, and Mor the Peacock, blazing in bronze and blue and gold,\r\ncried it aloud all along the misty woods, and Mowgli opened his mouth to\r\nsend on the cry, the words choked between his teeth, and a feeling came\r\nover him that began at his toes and ended in his hair--a feeling of pure\r\nunhappiness, so that he looked himself over to be sure that he had not\r\ntrod on a thorn. Mor cried the new smells, the other birds took it\r\nover, and from the rocks by the Waingunga he heard Bagheera\xe2\x80\x99s hoarse\r\nscream--something between the scream of an eagle and the neighing of\r\na horse. There was a yelling and scattering of Bandar-log in the\r\nnew-budding branches above, and there stood Mowgli, his chest, filled to\r\nanswer Mor, sinking in little gasps as the breath was driven out of it\r\nby this unhappiness.\r\n\r\nHe stared all round him, but he could see no more than the mocking\r\nBandar-log scudding through the trees, and Mor, his tail spread in full\r\nsplendour, dancing on the slopes below.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe smells have changed,\xe2\x80\x9d screamed Mor. \xe2\x80\x9cGood hunting, Little Brother!\r\nWhere is thy answer?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLittle Brother, good hunting!\xe2\x80\x9d whistled Chil the Kite and his mate,\r\nswooping down together. The two baffed under Mowgli\xe2\x80\x99s nose so close that\r\na pinch of downy white feathers brushed away.\r\n\r\nA light spring rain--elephant-rain they call it--drove across the Jungle\r\nin a belt half a mile wide, left the new leaves wet and nodding behind,\r\nand died out in a double rainbow and a light roll of thunder. The spring\r\nhum broke out for a minute, and was silent, but all the Jungle Folk\r\nseemed to be giving tongue at once. All except Mowgli.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have eaten good food,\xe2\x80\x9d he said to himself. \xe2\x80\x9cI have drunk good\r\nwater. Nor does my throat burn and grow small, as it did when I bit the\r\nblue-spotted root that Oo the Turtle said was clean food. But my stomach\r\nis heavy, and I have given very bad talk to Bagheera and others, people\r\nof the Jungle and my people. Now, too, I am hot and now I am cold, and\r\nnow I am neither hot nor cold, but angry with that which I cannot see.\r\nHuhu! It is time to make a running! To-night I will cross the ranges;\r\nyes, I will make a spring running to the Marshes of the North, and back\r\nagain. I have hunted too easily too long. The Four shall come with me,\r\nfor they grow as fat as white grubs.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe called, but never one of the Four answered. They were far beyond\r\nearshot, singing over the spring songs--the Moon and Sambhur Songs--with\r\nthe wolves of the pack; for in the spring-time the Jungle People make\r\nvery little difference between the day and the night. He gave the sharp,\r\nbarking note, but his only answer was the mocking maiou of the little\r\nspotted tree-cat winding in and out among the branches for early birds\xe2\x80\x99\r\nnests. At this he shook all over with rage, and half drew his knife.\r\nThen he became very haughty, though there was no one to see him, and\r\nstalked severely down the hillside, chin up and eyebrows down. But never\r\na single one of his people asked him a question, for they were all too\r\nbusy with their own affairs.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mowgli to himself, though in his heart he knew that he had\r\nno reason. \xe2\x80\x9cLet the Red Dhole come from the Dekkan, or the Red Flower\r\ndance among the bamboos, and all the Jungle runs whining to Mowgli,\r\ncalling him great elephant-names. But now, because Eye-of-the-Spring is\r\nred, and Mor, forsooth, must show his naked legs in some spring dance,\r\nthe Jungle goes mad as Tabaqui.... By the Bull that bought me! am I the\r\nMaster of the Jungle, or am I not? Be silent! What do ye here?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nA couple of young wolves of the Pack were cantering down a path, looking\r\nfor open ground in which to fight. (You will remember that the Law of\r\nthe Jungle forbids fighting where the Pack can see.) Their neck-bristles\r\nwere as stiff as wire, and they bayed furiously, crouching for the first\r\ngrapple. Mowgli leaped forward, caught one outstretched throat in either\r\nhand, expecting to fling the creatures backward as he had often done in\r\ngames or Pack hunts. But he had never before interfered with a spring\r\nfight. The two leaped forward and dashed him aside, and without word to\r\nwaste rolled over and over close locked.\r\n\r\nMowgli was on his feet almost before he fell, his knife and his white\r\nteeth were bared, and at that minute he would have killed both for no\r\nreason but that they were fighting when he wished them to be quiet,\r\nalthough every wolf has full right under the Law to fight. He danced\r\nround them with lowered shoulders and quivering hand, ready to send in\r\na double blow when the first flurry of the scuffle should be over;\r\nbut while he waited the strength seemed to ebb from his body, the\r\nknife-point lowered, and he sheathed the knife and watched.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have surely eaten poison,\xe2\x80\x9d he sighed at last. \xe2\x80\x9cSince I broke up the\r\nCouncil with the Red Flower--since I killed Shere Khan--none of the Pack\r\ncould fling me aside. And these be only tail-wolves in the Pack, little\r\nhunters! My strength is gone from me, and presently I shall die. Oh,\r\nMowgli, why dost thou not kill them both?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe fight went on till one wolf ran away, and Mowgli was left alone on\r\nthe torn and bloody ground, looking now at his knife, and now at his\r\nlegs and arms, while the feeling of unhappiness he had never known\r\nbefore covered him as water covers a log.\r\n\r\nHe killed early that evening and ate but little, so as to be in good\r\nfettle for his spring running, and he ate alone because all the Jungle\r\nPeople were away singing or fighting. It was a perfect white night,\r\nas they call it. All green things seemed to have made a month\xe2\x80\x99s growth\r\nsince the morning. The branch that was yellow-leaved the day before\r\ndripped sap when Mowgli broke it. The mosses curled deep and warm over\r\nhis feet, the young grass had no cutting edges, and all the voices of\r\nthe Jungle boomed like one deep harp-string touched by the moon--the\r\nMoon of New Talk, who splashed her light full on rock and pool, slipped\r\nit between trunk and creeper, and sifted it through a million leaves.\r\nForgetting his unhappiness, Mowgli sang aloud with pure delight as he\r\nsettled into his stride. It was more like flying than anything else, for\r\nhe had chosen the long downward slope that leads to the Northern Marshes\r\nthrough the heart of the main Jungle, where the springy ground deadened\r\nthe fall of his feet. A man-taught man would have picked his way with\r\nmany stumbles through the cheating moonlight, but Mowgli\xe2\x80\x99s muscles,\r\ntrained by years of experience, bore him up as though he were a feather.\r\nWhen a rotten log or a hidden stone turned under his foot he saved\r\nhimself, never checking his pace, without effort and without thought.\r\nWhen he tired of ground-going he threw up his hands monkey-fashion to\r\nthe nearest creeper, and seemed to float rather than to climb up into\r\nthe thin branches, whence he would follow a tree-road till his mood\r\nchanged, and he shot downward in a long, leafy curve to the levels\r\nagain. There were still, hot hollows surrounded by wet rocks where he\r\ncould hardly breathe for the heavy scents of the night flowers and the\r\nbloom along the creeper buds; dark avenues where the moonlight lay in\r\nbelts as regular as checkered marbles in a church aisle; thickets where\r\nthe wet young growth stood breast-high about him and threw its arms\r\nround his waist; and hilltops crowned with broken rock, where he leaped\r\nfrom stone to stone above the lairs of the frightened little foxes. He\r\nwould hear, very faint and far off, the chug-drug of a boar sharpening\r\nhis tusks on a bole; and would come across the great gray brute all\r\nalone, scribing and rending the bark of a tall tree, his mouth dripping\r\nwith foam, and his eyes blazing like fire. Or he would turn aside to the\r\nsound of clashing horns and hissing grunts, and dash past a couple of\r\nfurious sambhur, staggering to and fro with lowered heads, striped with\r\nblood that showed black in the moonlight. Or at some rushing ford he\r\nwould hear Jacala the Crocodile bellowing like a bull, or disturb a\r\ntwined knot of the Poison People, but before they could strike he would\r\nbe away and across the glistening shingle, and deep in the Jungle again.\r\n\r\nSo he ran, sometimes shouting, sometimes singing to himself, the\r\nhappiest thing in all the Jungle that night, till the smell of the\r\nflowers warned him that he was near the marshes, and those lay far\r\nbeyond his farthest hunting-grounds.\r\n\r\nHere, again, a man-trained man would have sunk overhead in three\r\nstrides, but Mowgli\xe2\x80\x99s feet had eyes in them, and they passed him from\r\ntussock to tussock and clump to quaking clump without asking help from\r\nthe eyes in his head. He ran out to the middle of the swamp, disturbing\r\nthe duck as he ran, and sat down on a moss-coated tree-trunk lapped in\r\nthe black water. The marsh was awake all round him, for in the spring\r\nthe Bird People sleep very lightly, and companies of them were coming\r\nor going the night through. But no one took any notice of Mowgli sitting\r\namong the tall reeds humming songs without words, and looking at the\r\nsoles of his hard brown feet in case of neglected thorns. All his\r\nunhappiness seemed to have been left behind in his own Jungle, and he\r\nwas just beginning a full-throat song when it came back again--ten times\r\nworse than before.\r\n\r\nThis time Mowgli was frightened. \xe2\x80\x9cIt is here also!\xe2\x80\x9d he said half aloud.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt has followed me,\xe2\x80\x9d and he looked over his shoulder to see whether\r\nthe It were not standing behind him. \xe2\x80\x9cThere is no one here.\xe2\x80\x9d The night\r\nnoises of the marsh went on, but never a bird or beast spoke to him, and\r\nthe new feeling of misery grew.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have surely eaten poison,\xe2\x80\x9d he said in an awe-stricken voice. \xe2\x80\x9cIt must\r\nbe that carelessly I have eaten poison, and my strength is going from\r\nme. I was afraid--and yet it was not _I_ that was afraid--Mowgli was\r\nafraid when the two wolves fought. Akela, or even Phao, would have\r\nsilenced them; yet Mowgli was afraid. That is true sign I have eaten\r\npoison.... But what do they care in the Jungle? They sing and howl and\r\nfight, and run in companies under the moon, and I--Hai-mai!--I am dying\r\nin the marshes, of that poison which I have eaten.\xe2\x80\x9d He was so sorry for\r\nhimself that he nearly wept. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd after,\xe2\x80\x9d he went on, \xe2\x80\x9cthey will find\r\nme lying in the black water. Nay, I will go back to my own Jungle, and I\r\nwill die upon the Council Rock, and Bagheera, whom I love, if he is not\r\nscreaming in the valley--Bagheera, perhaps, may watch by what is left\r\nfor a little, lest Chil use me as he used Akela.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nA large, warm tear splashed down on his knee, and, miserable as he was,\r\nMowgli felt happy that he was so miserable, if you can understand\r\nthat upside-down sort of happiness. \xe2\x80\x9cAs Chil the Kite used Akela,\xe2\x80\x9d he\r\nrepeated, \xe2\x80\x9con the night I saved the Pack from Red Dog.\xe2\x80\x9d He was quiet\r\nfor a little, thinking of the last words of the Lone Wolf, which you,\r\nof course, remember. \xe2\x80\x9cNow Akela said to me many foolish things before he\r\ndied, for when we die our stomachs change. He said... None the less, I\r\nAM of the Jungle!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIn his excitement, as he remembered the fight on Waingunga bank, he\r\nshouted the last words aloud, and a wild buffalo-cow among the reeds\r\nsprang to her knees, snorting, \xe2\x80\x9cMan!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cUhh!\xe2\x80\x9d said Mysa the Wild Buffalo (Mowgli could hear him turn in his\r\nwallow), \xe2\x80\x9cTHAT is no man. It is only the hairless wolf of the Seeonee\r\nPack. On such nights runs he to and fro.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cUhh!\xe2\x80\x9d said the cow, dropping her head again to graze, \xe2\x80\x9cI thought it was\r\nMan.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI say no. Oh, Mowgli, is it danger?\xe2\x80\x9d lowed Mysa.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, Mowgli, is it danger?\xe2\x80\x9d the boy called back mockingly. \xe2\x80\x9cThat is all\r\nMysa thinks for: Is it danger? But for Mowgli, who goes to and fro in\r\nthe Jungle by night, watching, what do ye care?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow loud he cries!\xe2\x80\x9d said the cow. \xe2\x80\x9cThus do they cry,\xe2\x80\x9d Mysa answered\r\ncontemptuously, \xe2\x80\x9cwho, having torn up the grass, know not how to eat it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFor less than this,\xe2\x80\x9d Mowgli groaned to himself, \xe2\x80\x9cfor less than this even\r\nlast Rains I had pricked Mysa out of his wallow, and ridden him through\r\nthe swamp on a rush halter.\xe2\x80\x9d He stretched a hand to break one of the\r\nfeathery reeds, but drew it back with a sigh. Mysa went on steadily\r\nchewing the cud, and the long grass ripped where the cow grazed. \xe2\x80\x9cI will\r\nnot die HERE,\xe2\x80\x9d he said angrily. \xe2\x80\x9cMysa, who is of one blood with Jacala\r\nand the pig, would see me. Let us go beyond the swamp and see what\r\ncomes. Never have I run such a spring running--hot and cold together.\r\nUp, Mowgli!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe could not resist the temptation of stealing across the reeds to Mysa\r\nand pricking him with the point of his knife. The great dripping bull\r\nbroke out of his wallow like a shell exploding, while Mowgli laughed\r\ntill he sat down.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSay now that the hairless wolf of the Seeonee Pack once herded thee,\r\nMysa,\xe2\x80\x9d he called.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWolf! THOU?\xe2\x80\x9d the bull snorted, stamping in the mud. \xe2\x80\x9cAll the jungle\r\nknows thou wast a herder of tame cattle--such a man\xe2\x80\x99s brat as shouts in\r\nthe dust by the crops yonder. THOU of the Jungle! What hunter would have\r\ncrawled like a snake among the leeches, and for a muddy jest--a jackal\xe2\x80\x99s\r\njest--have shamed me before my cow? Come to firm ground, and I will--I\r\nwill...\xe2\x80\x9d Mysa frothed at the mouth, for Mysa has nearly the worst temper\r\nof any one in the Jungle.\r\n\r\nMowgli watched him puff and blow with eyes that never changed. When\r\nhe could make himself heard through the pattering mud, he said: \xe2\x80\x9cWhat\r\nMan-Pack lair here by the marshes, Mysa? This is new Jungle to me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGo north, then,\xe2\x80\x9d roared the angry bull, for Mowgli had pricked him\r\nrather sharply. \xe2\x80\x9cIt was a naked cow-herd\xe2\x80\x99s jest. Go and tell them at the\r\nvillage at the foot of the marsh.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe Man-Pack do not love jungle-tales, nor do I think, Mysa, that a\r\nscratch more or less on thy hide is any matter for a council. But I will\r\ngo and look at this village. Yes, I will go. Softly now. It is not every\r\nnight that the Master of the Jungle comes to herd thee.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe stepped out to the shivering ground on the edge of the marsh, well\r\nknowing that Mysa would never charge over it and laughed, as he ran, to\r\nthink of the bull\xe2\x80\x99s anger.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy strength is not altogether gone,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cIt may be that the poison\r\nis not to the bone. There is a star sitting low yonder.\xe2\x80\x9d He looked at it\r\nbetween his half-shut hands. \xe2\x80\x9cBy the Bull that bought me, it is the Red\r\nFlower--the Red Flower that I lay beside before--before I came even\r\nto the first Seeonee Pack! Now that I have seen, I will finish the\r\nrunning.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe marsh ended in a broad plain where a light twinkled. It was a long\r\ntime since Mowgli had concerned himself with the doings of men, but this\r\nnight the glimmer of the Red Flower drew him forward.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI will look,\xe2\x80\x9d said he, \xe2\x80\x9cas I did in the old days, and I will see how\r\nfar the Man-Pack has changed.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nForgetting that he was no longer in his own Jungle, where he could do\r\nwhat he pleased, he trod carelessly through the dew-loaded grasses till\r\nhe came to the hut where the light stood. Three or four yelping dogs\r\ngave tongue, for he was on the outskirts of a village.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHo!\xe2\x80\x9d said Mowgli, sitting down noiselessly, after sending back a deep\r\nwolf-growl that silenced the curs. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat comes will come. Mowgli, what\r\nhast thou to do any more with the lairs of the Man-Pack?\xe2\x80\x9d He rubbed his\r\nmouth, remembering where a stone had struck it years ago when the other\r\nMan-Pack had cast him out.\r\n\r\nThe door of the hut opened, and a woman stood peering out into the\r\ndarkness. A child cried, and the woman said over her shoulder, \xe2\x80\x9cSleep.\r\nIt was but a jackal that waked the dogs. In a little time morning\r\ncomes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMowgli in the grass began to shake as though he had fever. He knew that\r\nvoice well, but to make sure he cried softly, surprised to find how\r\nman\xe2\x80\x99s talk came back, \xe2\x80\x9cMessua! O Messua!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWho calls?\xe2\x80\x9d said the woman, a quiver in her voice.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHast thou forgotten?\xe2\x80\x9d said Mowgli. His throat was dry as he spoke.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf it be THOU, what name did I give thee? Say!\xe2\x80\x9d She had half shut the\r\ndoor, and her hand was clutching at her breast.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNathoo! Ohe, Nathoo!\xe2\x80\x9d said Mowgli, for, as you remember, that was the\r\nname Messua gave him when he first came to the Man-Pack.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCome, my son,\xe2\x80\x9d she called, and Mowgli stepped into the light, and\r\nlooked full at Messua, the woman who had been good to him, and whose\r\nlife he had saved from the Man-Pack so long before. She was older,\r\nand her hair was gray, but her eyes and her voice had not changed.\r\nWoman-like, she expected to find Mowgli where she had left him, and her\r\neyes travelled upward in a puzzled way from his chest to his head, that\r\ntouched the top of the door.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy son,\xe2\x80\x9d she stammered; and then, sinking to his feet: \xe2\x80\x9cBut it is no\r\nlonger my son. It is a Godling of the Woods! Ahai!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAs he stood in the red light of the oil-lamp, strong, tall, and\r\nbeautiful, his long black hair sweeping over his shoulders, the knife\r\nswinging at his neck, and his head crowned with a wreath of white\r\njasmine, he might easily have been mistaken for some wild god of a\r\njungle legend. The child half asleep on a cot sprang up and shrieked\r\naloud with terror. Messua turned to soothe him, while Mowgli stood\r\nstill, looking in at the water-jars and the cooking-pots, the grain-bin,\r\nand all the other human belongings that he found himself remembering so\r\nwell.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat wilt thou eat or drink?\xe2\x80\x9d Messua murmured. \xe2\x80\x9cThis is all thine. We\r\nowe our lives to thee. But art thou him I called Nathoo, or a Godling,\r\nindeed?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am Nathoo,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mowgli, \xe2\x80\x9cI am very far from my own place. I saw this\r\nlight, and came hither. I did not know thou wast here.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAfter we came to Khanhiwara,\xe2\x80\x9d Messua said timidly, \xe2\x80\x9cthe English\r\nwould have helped us against those villagers that sought to burn us.\r\nRememberest thou?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIndeed, I have not forgotten.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut when the English Law was made ready, we went to the village of\r\nthose evil people, and it was no more to be found.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat also I remember,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mowgli, with a quiver of his nostril.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy man, therefore, took service in the fields, and at last--for,\r\nindeed, he was a strong man--we held a little land here. It is not so\r\nrich as the old village, but we do not need much--we two.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhere is he the man that dug in the dirt when he was afraid on that\r\nnight?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe is dead--a year.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd he?\xe2\x80\x9d Mowgli pointed to the child.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy son that was born two Rains ago. If thou art a Godling, give him the\r\nFavour of the Jungle, that he may be safe among thy--thy people, as we\r\nwere safe on that night.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe lifted up the child, who, forgetting his fright, reached out to play\r\nwith the knife that hung on Mowgli\xe2\x80\x99s chest, and Mowgli put the little\r\nfingers aside very carefully.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd if thou art Nathoo whom the tiger carried away,\xe2\x80\x9d Messua went on,\r\nchoking, \xe2\x80\x9che is then thy younger brother. Give him an elder brother\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nblessing.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHai-mai! What do I know of the thing called a blessing? I am neither\r\na Godling nor his brother, and--O mother, mother, my heart is heavy in\r\nme.\xe2\x80\x9d He shivered as he set down the child.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLike enough,\xe2\x80\x9d said Messua, bustling among the cooking-pots. \xe2\x80\x9cThis comes\r\nof running about the marshes by night. Beyond question, the fever\r\nhad soaked thee to the marrow.\xe2\x80\x9d Mowgli smiled a little at the idea of\r\nanything in the Jungle hurting him. \xe2\x80\x9cI will make a fire, and thou shalt\r\ndrink warm milk. Put away the jasmine wreath: the smell is heavy in so\r\nsmall a place.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMowgli sat down, muttering, with his face in his hands. All manner of\r\nstrange feelings that he had never felt before were running over him,\r\nexactly as though he had been poisoned, and he felt dizzy and a little\r\nsick. He drank the warm milk in long gulps, Messua patting him on the\r\nshoulder from time to time, not quite sure whether he were her son\r\nNathoo of the long ago days, or some wonderful Jungle being, but glad to\r\nfeel that he was at least flesh and blood.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSon,\xe2\x80\x9d she said at last,--her eyes were full of pride,--\xe2\x80\x9chave any told\r\nthee that thou art beautiful beyond all men?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHah?\xe2\x80\x9d said Mowgli, for naturally he had never heard anything of the\r\nkind. Messua laughed softly and happily. The look in his face was enough\r\nfor her.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am the first, then? It is right, though it comes seldom, that a\r\nmother should tell her son these good things. Thou art very beautiful.\r\nNever have I looked upon such a man.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMowgli twisted his head and tried to see over his own hard shoulder, and\r\nMessua laughed again so long that Mowgli, not knowing why, was forced to\r\nlaugh with her, and the child ran from one to the other, laughing too.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNay, thou must not mock thy brother,\xe2\x80\x9d said Messua, catching him to\r\nher breast. \xe2\x80\x9cWhen thou art one-half as fair we will marry thee to the\r\nyoungest daughter of a king, and thou shalt ride great elephants.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMowgli could not understand one word in three of the talk here; the warm\r\nmilk was taking effect on him after his long run, so he curled up and\r\nin a minute was deep asleep, and Messua put the hair back from his eyes,\r\nthrew a cloth over him, and was happy. Jungle-fashion, he slept out the\r\nrest of that night and all the next day; for his instincts, which never\r\nwholly slept, warned him there was nothing to fear. He waked at last\r\nwith a bound that shook the hut, for the cloth over his face made him\r\ndream of traps; and there he stood, his hand on his knife, the sleep all\r\nheavy in his rolling eyes, ready for any fight.\r\n\r\nMessua laughed, and set the evening meal before him. There were only\r\na few coarse cakes baked over the smoky fire, some rice, and a lump of\r\nsour preserved tamarinds--just enough to go on with till he could get\r\nto his evening kill. The smell of the dew in the marshes made him hungry\r\nand restless. He wanted to finish his spring running, but the child\r\ninsisted on sitting in his arms, and Messua would have it that his long,\r\nblue-black hair must be combed out. So she sang, as she combed, foolish\r\nlittle baby-songs, now calling Mowgli her son, and now begging him to\r\ngive some of his jungle power to the child. The hut door was closed, but\r\nMowgli heard a sound he knew well, and saw Messua\xe2\x80\x99s jaw drop with horror\r\nas a great gray paw came under the bottom of the door, and Gray Brother\r\noutside whined a muffled and penitent whine of anxiety and fear.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOut and wait! Ye would not come when I called,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mowgli in\r\nJungle-talk, without turning his head, and the great gray paw\r\ndisappeared.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo not--do not bring thy--thy servants with thee,\xe2\x80\x9d said Messua. \xe2\x80\x9cI--we\r\nhave always lived at peace with the Jungle.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is peace,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mowgli, rising. \xe2\x80\x9cThink of that night on the road to\r\nKhanhiwara. There were scores of such folk before thee and behind\r\nthee. But I see that even in springtime the Jungle People do not always\r\nforget. Mother, I go.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMessua drew aside humbly--he was indeed a wood-god, she thought; but as\r\nhis hand was on the door the mother in her made her throw her arms round\r\nMowgli\xe2\x80\x99s neck again and again.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCome back!\xe2\x80\x9d she whispered. \xe2\x80\x9cSon or no son, come back, for I love\r\nthee--Look, he too grieves.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe child was crying because the man with the shiny knife was going\r\naway.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCome back again,\xe2\x80\x9d Messua repeated. \xe2\x80\x9cBy night or by day this door is\r\nnever shut to thee.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMowgli\xe2\x80\x99s throat worked as though the cords in it were being pulled, and\r\nhis voice seemed to be dragged from it as he answered, \xe2\x80\x9cI will surely\r\ncome back.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd now,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, as he put by the head of the fawning wolf on the\r\nthreshold, \xe2\x80\x9cI have a little cry against thee, Gray Brother. Why came ye\r\nnot all four when I called so long ago?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo long ago? It was but last night. I--we--were singing in the Jungle\r\nthe new songs, for this is the Time of New Talk. Rememberest thou?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTruly, truly.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd as soon as the songs were sung,\xe2\x80\x9d Gray Brother went on earnestly,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI followed thy trail. I ran from all the others and followed hot-foot.\r\nBut, O Little Brother, what hast THOU done, eating and sleeping with the\r\nMan-Pack?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf ye had come when I called, this had never been,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mowgli,\r\nrunning much faster.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd now what is to be?\xe2\x80\x9d said Gray Brother. Mowgli was going to answer\r\nwhen a girl in a white cloth came down some path that led from the\r\noutskirts of the village. Gray Brother dropped out of sight at once, and\r\nMowgli backed noiselessly into a field of high-springing crops. He could\r\nalmost have touched her with his hand when the warm, green stalks closed\r\nbefore his face and he disappeared like a ghost. The girl screamed, for\r\nshe thought she had seen a spirit, and then she gave a deep sigh. Mowgli\r\nparted the stalks with his hands and watched her till she was out of\r\nsight.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd now I do not know,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, sighing in his turn. \xe2\x80\x9cWHY did ye not\r\ncome when I called?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe follow thee--we follow thee,\xe2\x80\x9d Gray Brother mumbled, licking at\r\nMowgli\xe2\x80\x99s heel. \xe2\x80\x9cWe follow thee always, except in the Time of the New\r\nTalk.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd would ye follow me to the Man-Pack?\xe2\x80\x9d Mowgli whispered.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDid I not follow thee on the night our old Pack cast thee out? Who\r\nwaked thee lying among the crops?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAy, but again?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHave I not followed thee to-night?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAy, but again and again, and it may be again, Gray Brother?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nGray Brother was silent. When he spoke he growled to himself, \xe2\x80\x9cThe Black\r\nOne spoke truth.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd he said?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMan goes to Man at the last. Raksha, our mother, said----\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo also said Akela on the night of Red Dog,\xe2\x80\x9d Mowgli muttered.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo also says Kaa, who is wiser than us all.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat dost thou say, Gray Brother?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey cast thee out once, with bad talk. They cut thy mouth with stones.\r\nThey sent Buldeo to slay thee. They would have thrown thee into the Red\r\nFlower. Thou, and not I, hast said that they are evil and senseless.\r\nThou, and not I--I follow my own people--didst let in the Jungle upon\r\nthem. Thou, and not I, didst make song against them more bitter even\r\nthan our song against Red Dog.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI ask thee what THOU sayest?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThey were talking as they ran. Gray Brother cantered on a while\r\nwithout replying, and then he said,--between bound and bound as it\r\nwere,--\xe2\x80\x9cMan-cub--Master of the Jungle--Son of Raksha, Lair-brother to\r\nme--though I forget for a little while in the spring, thy trail is my\r\ntrail, thy lair is my lair, thy kill is my kill, and thy death-fight\r\nis my death-fight. I speak for the Three. But what wilt thou say to the\r\nJungle?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is well thought. Between the sight and the kill it is not good to\r\nwait. Go before and cry them all to the Council Rock, and I will tell\r\nthem what is in my stomach. But they may not come--in the Time of New\r\nTalk they may forget me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHast thou, then, forgotten nothing?\xe2\x80\x9d snapped Gray Brother over his\r\nshoulder, as he laid himself down to gallop, and Mowgli followed,\r\nthinking.\r\n\r\nAt any other season the news would have called all the Jungle together\r\nwith bristling necks, but now they were busy hunting and fighting and\r\nkilling and singing. From one to another Gray Brother ran, crying, \xe2\x80\x9cThe\r\nMaster of the Jungle goes back to Man! Come to the Council Rock.\xe2\x80\x9d And\r\nthe happy, eager People only answered, \xe2\x80\x9cHe will return in the summer\r\nheats. The Rains will drive him to lair. Run and sing with us, Gray\r\nBrother.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut the Master of the Jungle goes back to Man,\xe2\x80\x9d Gray Brother would\r\nrepeat.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cEee--Yoawa? Is the Time of New Talk any less sweet for that?\xe2\x80\x9d they\r\nwould reply. So when Mowgli, heavy-hearted, came up through the\r\nwell-remembered rocks to the place where he had been brought into the\r\nCouncil, he found only the Four, Baloo, who was nearly blind with age,\r\nand the heavy, cold-blooded Kaa coiled around Akela\xe2\x80\x99s empty seat.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThy trail ends here, then, Manling?\xe2\x80\x9d said Kaa, as Mowgli threw himself\r\ndown, his face in his hands. \xe2\x80\x9cCry thy cry. We be of one blood, thou and\r\nI--man and snake together.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy did I not die under Red Dog?\xe2\x80\x9d the boy moaned. \xe2\x80\x9cMy strength is gone\r\nfrom me, and it is not any poison. By night and by day I hear a double\r\nstep upon my trail. When I turn my head it is as though one had hidden\r\nhimself from me that instant. I go to look behind the trees and he is\r\nnot there. I call and none cry again; but it is as though one listened\r\nand kept back the answer. I lie down, but I do not rest. I run the\r\nspring running, but I am not made still. I bathe, but I am not made\r\ncool. The kill sickens me, but I have no heart to fight except I kill.\r\nThe Red Flower is in my body, my bones are water--and--I know not what I\r\nknow.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat need of talk?\xe2\x80\x9d said Baloo slowly, turning his head to where Mowgli\r\nlay. \xe2\x80\x9cAkela by the river said it, that Mowgli should drive Mowgli\r\nback to the Man-Pack. I said it. But who listens now to Baloo?\r\nBagheera--where is Bagheera this night?--he knows also. It is the Law.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhen we met at Cold Lairs, Manling, I knew it,\xe2\x80\x9d said Kaa, turning a\r\nlittle in his mighty coils. \xe2\x80\x9cMan goes to Man at the last, though the\r\nJungle does not cast him out.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe Four looked at one another and at Mowgli, puzzled but obedient.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe Jungle does not cast me out, then?\xe2\x80\x9d Mowgli stammered.\r\n\r\nGray Brother and the Three growled furiously, beginning, \xe2\x80\x9cSo long as we\r\nlive none shall dare----\xe2\x80\x9d But Baloo checked them.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI taught thee the Law. It is for me to speak,\xe2\x80\x9d he said; \xe2\x80\x9cand, though I\r\ncannot now see the rocks before me, I see far. Little Frog, take thine\r\nown trail; make thy lair with thine own blood and pack and people; but\r\nwhen there is need of foot or tooth or eye, or a word carried swiftly by\r\nnight, remember, Master of the Jungle, the Jungle is thine at call.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe Middle Jungle is thine also,\xe2\x80\x9d said Kaa. \xe2\x80\x9cI speak for no small\r\npeople.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHai-mai, my brothers,\xe2\x80\x9d cried Mowgli, throwing up his arms with a sob.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI know not what I know! I would not go; but I am drawn by both feet.\r\nHow shall I leave these nights?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNay, look up, Little Brother,\xe2\x80\x9d Baloo repeated. \xe2\x80\x9cThere is no shame in\r\nthis hunting. When the honey is eaten we leave the empty hive.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHaving cast the skin,\xe2\x80\x9d said Kaa, \xe2\x80\x9cwe may not creep into it afresh. It\r\nis the Law.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cListen, dearest of all to me,\xe2\x80\x9d said Baloo. There is neither word nor\r\nwill here to hold thee back. Look up! Who may question the Master of the\r\nJungle? I saw thee playing among the white pebbles yonder when thou wast\r\na little frog; and Bagheera, that bought thee for the price of a young\r\nbull newly killed, saw thee also. Of that Looking Over we two only\r\nremain; for Raksha, thy lair-mother, is dead with thy lair-father; the\r\nold Wolf-Pack is long since dead; thou knowest whither Shere Khan went,\r\nand Akela died among the dholes, where, but for thy wisdom and strength,\r\nthe second Seeonee Pack would also have died. There remains nothing but\r\nold bones. It is no longer the Man-cub that asks leave of his Pack, but\r\nthe Master of the Jungle that changes his trail. Who shall question Man\r\nin his ways?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut Bagheera and the Bull that bought me,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mowgli. \xe2\x80\x9cI would\r\nnot----\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHis words were cut short by a roar and a crash in the thicket below, and\r\nBagheera, light, strong, and terrible as always, stood before him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTherefore,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, stretching out a dripping right paw, \xe2\x80\x9cI did not\r\ncome. It was a long hunt, but he lies dead in the bushes now--a bull in\r\nhis second year--the Bull that frees thee, Little Brother. All debts\r\nare paid now. For the rest, my word is Baloo\xe2\x80\x99s word.\xe2\x80\x9d He licked Mowgli\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nfoot. \xe2\x80\x9cRemember, Bagheera loved thee,\xe2\x80\x9d he cried, and bounded away. At\r\nthe foot of the hill he cried again long and loud, \xe2\x80\x9cGood hunting on a\r\nnew trail, Master of the Jungle! Remember, Bagheera loved thee.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThou hast heard,\xe2\x80\x9d said Baloo. \xe2\x80\x9cThere is no more. Go now; but first come\r\nto me. O wise Little Frog, come to me!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is hard to cast the skin,\xe2\x80\x9d said Kaa as Mowgli sobbed and sobbed,\r\nwith his head on the blind bear\xe2\x80\x99s side and his arms round his neck,\r\nwhile Baloo tried feebly to lick his feet.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe stars are thin,\xe2\x80\x9d said Gray Brother, snuffing at the dawn wind.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhere shall we lair to-day? for from now, we follow new trails.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n*****\r\n\r\nAnd this is the last of the Mowgli stories.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nTHE OUTSONG\r\n\r\n[This is the song that Mowgli heard behind him in the Jungle till he\r\ncame to Messua\xe2\x80\x99s door again.]\r\n\r\n     Baloo\r\n\r\n     For the sake of him who showed\r\n     One wise Frog the Jungle-Road,\r\n     Keep the Law the Man-Pack make--\r\n     For thy blind old Baloo\xe2\x80\x99s sake!\r\n     Clean or tainted, hot or stale,\r\n     Hold it as it were the Trail,\r\n     Through the day and through the night,\r\n     Questing neither left nor right.\r\n     For the sake of him who loves\r\n     Thee beyond all else that moves,\r\n     When thy Pack would make thee pain,\r\n     Say: \xe2\x80\x9cTabaqui sings again.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n      When thy Pack would work thee ill,\r\n     Say: \xe2\x80\x9cShere Khan is yet to kill.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n      When the knife is drawn to slay,\r\n     Keep the Law and go thy way.\r\n     (Root and honey, palm and spathe,\r\n     Guard a cub from harm and scathe!)\r\n     Wood and Water, Wind and Tree,\r\n     Jungle-Favour go with thee!\r\n\r\n\r\n     Kaa\r\n\r\n     Anger is the egg of Fear--\r\n     Only lidless eyes are clear.\r\n     Cobra-poison none may leech.\r\n     Even so with Cobra-speech.\r\n     Open talk shall call to thee\r\n     Strength, whose mate is Courtesy.\r\n     Send no lunge beyond thy length;\r\n     Lend no rotten bough thy strength.\r\n     Gauge thy gape with buck or goat,\r\n     Lest thine eye should choke thy throat,\r\n     After gorging, wouldst thou sleep?\r\n     Look thy den is hid and deep,\r\n     Lest a wrong, by thee forgot,\r\n     Draw thy killer to the spot.\r\n     East and West and North and South,\r\n     Wash thy hide and close thy mouth.\r\n     (Pit and rift and blue pool-brim,\r\n     Middle-Jungle follow him!)\r\n     Wood and Water, Wind and Tree,\r\n     Jungle-Favour go with thee!\r\n\r\n\r\n     Bagheera\r\n\r\n     In the cage my life began;\r\n     Well I know the worth of Man.\r\n     By the Broken Lock that freed--\r\n     Man-cub, \xe2\x80\x98ware the Man-cub\xe2\x80\x99s breed!\r\n     Scenting-dew or starlight pale,\r\n     Choose no tangled tree-cat trail.\r\n     Pack or council, hunt or den,\r\n     Cry no truce with Jackal-Men.\r\n     Feed them silence when they say:\r\n     \xe2\x80\x9cCome with us an easy way.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n      Feed them silence when they seek\r\n     Help of thine to hurt the weak.\r\n     Make no banaar\xe2\x80\x99s boast of skill;\r\n     Hold thy peace above the kill.\r\n     Let nor call nor song nor sign\r\n     Turn thee from thy hunting-line.\r\n     (Morning mist or twilight clear,\r\n     Serve him, Wardens of the Deer!)\r\n     Wood and Water, Wind and Tree,\r\n     Jungle-Favour go with thee!\r\n\r\n\r\n     The Three\r\n\r\n     On the trail that thou must tread\r\n     To the thresholds of our dread,\r\n     Where the Flower blossoms red;\r\n     Through the nights when thou shalt lie\r\n     Prisoned from our Mother-sky,\r\n     Hearing us, thy loves, go by;\r\n     In the dawns when thou shalt wake\r\n     To the toil thou canst not break,\r\n     Heartsick for the Jungle\xe2\x80\x99s sake:\r\n     Wood and Water, Wind and Tree,\r\n     Wisdom, Strength, and Courtesy,\r\n     Jungle-Favour go with thee!\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nEnd of Project Gutenberg\xe2\x80\x99s The Second Jungle Book, by Rudyard Kipling\r\n\r\n*** END OF THIS PROJECT GUTENBERG EBOOK THE SECOND JUNGLE BOOK ***\r\n\r\n*****
